The Red Page
Dress
The chief business, or job, of the female
of our species is to dress. Some of hey, it is
true, find time for a rather fascinating
hobby, that of bearing and nourishing
babies at infrequent intervals; but the
majority wisely recognise that when a hobby
conflicts with one’s business, the hobby must
be given up. Some philosophers hold, how-
ever, that this hobby really extends the
female’s trade, since she can dress the pro-
duct as well as dress herself, thus getting an
added pleasure out of her profession.
The reason why the female dresses is in
order to appear beautiful. It will thus be
apparent that the female does not recognise
herself as inherently beautifuli —else no
police regulations would stop her from
shopping in a fig-leaf. The difference
between the female and beauty is that beauty
needs no adornment. The female recognises
that she was not designed by Nature to be
the beautiful sex. In every other kingdom
of zoology it is the male whom Nature pro-
vides with adornment: the human female is
like every other female in the animal king-
dom in lacking aesthetic adornment. But
the female of our species was early pro-
vided with a brain, and by the use of it she
soon set about remedying her deficiencies.
Thus she got a long start on the domestic
hen. The fowl may have regretted that she
had no comb and no tail-feathers and no
crow; but She had not the sense to pin a
lump of red meat beneath her chin, add to
her skirt a train of discarded rooster-
feathers, and get her tonsils cut. But the
female human recognised that unless she
got a move on she had no chance in a beauty
competition with the human male.
Acknowledging that her figure, with its
excessive development of hip, its short legs,
its long back, and its diminutive size, han-
dicapped as she was by her enforced special-
isation on the birth-rate problem, had no
chance aesthetically with the lordly male, she
applied her brain to the task of covering up
and transforming her nakedness. Cunningly
she argued that if she succeeded in disguis-
ing herself, the male, in a few generations,
would see her not as she was, but the
gorgeous, if grotesque, being she had made
of herself. Her short legs she concealed
with a skirt; her height she added to by
high heels, arguing that though her walk,
as a result of this disturbance of the balance
of her figure, became a waddle, the male in
time would regard this waddle as her
natural gait, and even find a sexual beauty
in it. At the other end of her body she
built up her height by fluffing out her hair
—that vegetable form of life that linked
her with such lowly forms of life as the
seaweed and the foliage of trees. And on
top of her hair, or pad, she piled a hat, and
on the top of the hat she grew some more
vegetables. Thus in time the male accepted
her as of practically his own height, and
did not consider that by longitudinal
measurement every female was resolved
into this algebraical formula: 7 inches,
vegetables and wire -f- 2 inches pad -f- 1
inch hair -f- 4 foot, something, female -j-
-2 inches leather heel. She next turned her
attention from her longitude to her* latitude.
At first she decided to call attention to her
hips by bisecting her body in the manner
of the ant; but about the beginning of the
20th century she lost interest in hips, and
eagerly relinquished her waist and flattened
her hips in order to get the straight line of
beauty—the line so like that possessed natu-
rally by the male.
Having thus, so to speak, put in the
foundations, she proceeded to build on them.
Recognising that, as a sex, the female has
less variation from the normal than the
male, she aimed in her attire to make up
for her painful lack of variation. She set
to work to compress and bulge portions of
her fluid figure in order to differentiate her-
self as much as possible from the mass of
females. She added color and differences of
texture. These adventitious aids gave her
outward semblance the individuality of
which as a member of a normal sex she felt
conspicuously lacking. And here she is to-
day, sefrenely confident that she had built up
from her naked foundations a structure
unique and beautiful.
But though her whole attention has been
absorbed in this long task, though she has
taken dress as her special trade, or job, it is
here precisely that the female has failed.
She has been forced to recognise, for in-
stance, that all her care will not keep her
beautiful, say, up to 40. All her care will
not keep her lovely through 24 hours. By a
special effort, and by elaborate labor, she
can brilliantly “fake” an appearance of
beauty. When going to some function she
produces, by a laborious alchemy, an appear-
ance that is momentarily beautiful; but it
depends on so many adventitious aids that
the lack of one will ruin the whole elaborate
edifice. Dressing is to her a sacrificial rite:
her dressing-room is a torture chamber at
which a rashly prying Wowser would faint
with horror. Her dressing-table, when a
female is “dressed,” is a grim array of the
discarded instruments of the rack. And the
effect thus heroically achieved lasts only a
few hours. Before she starts on her return
home the beauty is wearing off. It is not
meant to last.
Her complexion, her chief care, is her
chief weakness. Take any healthy male and
compare his complexion with that of any
healthy female. The male has never given
his complexion a thought: the female gives
hers every second thought. Which, on the
average, is the more healthy, the more
beautiful—the complexion shielded from the
sun and the wind and the rain, or that ex-
posed to every buffet of the elements? The
male, with his larger wisdom, chooses for
his beauty doctor Nature herself. The
female, by taking too much thought,
secludes her complexion from the sunshine,
the air, the rain, for which Nature, with
her long experience, has expressly prepared
it. And, with all her care, the complexion
of the female is ruined long before old age.
Compare any healthy old female with any
healthy old male. Here again Nature shows
that she meant the male to be the beautiful
sex. For she covers the wrinkles in
the male face with a beard. Occa-
sionally Nature takes a belated pity
upon the old female, and starts in
to provide her, too, with moustache and
side-whiskers. But the effort is only half-
hearted : Nature soon loses interest in old
females.
Jt
But, though the female cannot disguise
her face, she has an unlimited choice of
disguise in her clothing. Yet even in this
woman’s department she makes the most
grievous mistakes. She leaves exposed the
ugliest part of her anatomy, her elbow. The
male covers it up. Many of the female’s
dresses are expressly designed to expose
that sharp and ungraceful bone. The
trouble is that the female is not vain enough.
With dress as her chief business, how is it
that every female is not beautifully, that is,
simply and artistically, dressed?
The male is wiser, more vain. He dares
not make experiments in hue and material.
He puts himself into a uniform. He makes
no sartorial mistakes, because he has no
chance to make mistakes. No man in even-
ing dress, however absurd that dress now
is, offends against the laws of color. His
black and white do not clash with his com-
plexion, nor even with the complexions of
the females adjacent. What the female has
not yet learnt is that her sense of color,
like thpt of most males, is as yet too rudi-
mentary for her to experiment in costume.
She must put herself into uniform. A lot
of her have already done so, with pleasing
results. The nurse, the waitress, the capped
maid, the black-garbed shop-girl, the girl in
the ballet, the office girl, with her invariable
blouse and skirt —each of these is an object
lesson in the artistic improvement of the
human female.
„ t
But if the female must experiment with
herself, here is a valuable little book that
will teach her the rudiments of her job of
being beautiful in attire. Color in Dress,
by G. It. Audsley, LL.D. (Low, Marsden),
is “a manual for ladies on all matters con-
nected with the proper selection and har-
monious combination of colors suitable for
the various complexions, based on the indis-
putable phenomena of color.” There are
two harmonies of color, harmony of con-
trast and harmony of analogy, either of
scale or of hue. Tables of each are given.
But most valuable is the author’s elucidation
of the phenomena attending the simultaneous
contrast of color, the law of which is: “In
the case where the eye sees at the same time
two contiguous colors,- they will appear as
dissimilar as possible, both in their optical
composition and in the height of their tone.”
“If a strip of primary red is placed in
juxtaposition to a corresponding strip of
the primary blue, the red will appear to
incline to the orange scale, assuming to the
eye a scarlet hue; this is caused by orange
—the complementary of blue —being cast
over, so to speak, the red color. The blue
will, in like manner, incline to the green
scale, by the complementary of red —green —-
being cast over it.”
One practical lesson to be got from this
law is, for example, that if a purchaser has
for a considerable time looked at yellow
fabrics, and she is then shown orange or
scarlet stuffs, she takes them to be amaranth-
red, or crimson, for there is a tendency in
the retina, excited by yellow, to acquire an
aptitude to see violet; accordingly all the
yellow of the scarlet or orange stuffs
apparently disappears, and the eye sees red,
or a red tinged with violet.
Also, if a customer is shown, say, 14
pieces of red stuff, she will consider (he last
six pieces less beautiful than those first seen,
for her eyes, having been tired by a succes-
sion of red, acquire a tendency to see the
complementary of red—green. This green-
ness enfeebles the brilliancy of the red. The
remedy is to refresh the eye by the sight of
a pi&£.e of green hue.
The colors a female should wear depend
upon the tints of her complexion. The keyv-
tint of her complexion must be considered in
reference to the colors placed in juxtaposi-
tion to it, with special care not to injure it
by the formation of discords, or the casting
upon it of objectionable complementary tints,
by simultaneous contrast or by positive re-
flection. The author provides a scale of
complexion tint, ranging from blonde to
brunette, thus: Fair blonde, ruddy blonde,
pale brunette, florid brunette. But there is
a fifth note in the scale, between blonde and
brunette. This is the muddy blonde, though
the author prefers to term it the semi-
brunette. The fair blonde type has as sub-
divisions the cool blonde and the golden
blonde. The ruddy blonde has as a minor
type the Titian blonde. So there you are,
all nicely classified. The author proceeds to
indicate, not by his personal preferences of
taste, but by the laws of color, what each
tinted female should wear. Thus the fair
blonde must wear a color that will warm her
skin, deepen her eyes and enrich her hair.
So for her green is the only wear, for green
will impart to these three its complementary
red. Light blue, imposing its complementary
orange, will enrich flaxen hair, and by posi-
tive reflection add richness to blue eyes.
But the fair blonde must never wear red,
orange, yellow, purple or brown. Full de-
tails are given of color harmonies that will
suit the fair blonde. So Audsley runs
through the gamut of female complexions,
striking the right color chord for each.
Purple, last season’s only fashionable
wear, is the least satisfactory color for
beautifying its wearer. Any blonde who
wears purple will look sickly, yellow and
sallow. “No scientific argument can be
advanced in its favor for ordinary daylight
costume; and, as its beauty and color value
are positively lost in gas-light, and are
greatly injured in all other systems of arti-
ficial lighting, it is evident that it is un-
desirable for evening dress.” For purple
cannot be effectively neutralised by a suffi-
cient amount of orange-yellow, its comple-
mentary color, without producing a too pro-
nounced effect in any light. Still, if fashion
tells the female to wear purple, what is the
poor thing to do? But if you must have a
hat of violet velvet, you must line it under-
neath, adjoining the face, with some dead
cream-white material.
These few examples are given out of a
host of dicta furnished by the kindly author,
without, of course, any hope on this critic’s
part that they will be acted upon.
The Real Shakespeare
The Elizabethan Stage Society is an
organisation that produces the Elizabethan
drama as nearly as possible under Eliza-
bethan conditions upon an Elizabethan
stage. This is no antiquarian fad, but an
artistic attempt to restore Shakespeare to
the stage from which he has been so long
banished by the scene-painter, the Shake-
spearian “producer” and the Shakespearian
“star.” The presentation of these Eliza-
bethan plays has been in the nature of a
revelation to all modern students of Wil-
liam ; and the society during the last 10
years has given a long programme of suc-
cessful Elizabethan plays.
The founder and director of this society
is William Poel; and his Shakespeare in the
Theatre (Sidgwick and Jackson, per Dy-
mock’s Library) is, as may be imagined, a
mine of information and of brilliant dra-
matic criticism. In “The Stage of
Shakespeare,” Poel iconoclastically points
out, that Shakespeare’s plays were “in most
cases compositions hastily written to fulfil
the requirements of the moment and adapted
to the wants of his theatre and the capa-
bilities of his actors.” Just as plays are
written to-day. “Their plays were shaped
to suit the theatre of the day and no other.”
Shakespeare did not find his theatre inade-
quate to his needs; and Poel tells us how
workmanlike London’s theatrical representa-
tions then were. There is no evidence that
in order to indicate a change of scenery in
any Shakespearian play a board was hung
up with the name chalked on it. Nor was
there any need for such a device, since
Shakespeare himself considered it to be the
business of the dramatist to describe the
scene, and to call the attention of the audi-
ence to each change in locality; and, more-
over, hq does this so skilfully as to make
his scenic descriptions apnear as part of the
natural dialogue of the play. “The naked
action was assisted by the poetry; and much
that now seems superfluous in the descrip-
tive passages was neteded to excite action.”
And the construction of the stage, narrow
and projecting into the yard, concentrated
upon the\actors, surrounded on all sides by
spectators, the attention of the audience,
and brought the action into prominent re-
lief, deprived of all perspective. What aid
would scenery give to such acting?
J*
In modern representations of Shakespeare
we get only two-thirds of anv play, yet the
whole of such a play as Hamlet was spoken
in btiatcspcjire s time 1 a space of two inrt
a a \ t ll(Jlirs - Wow was uiis possible? First
the Elizabethan actor spoke blank verse with
a remarkable rapidity, “trippingly on thd
tongue. -The modern actor mouths Shake
speare. Then, only a few of the plays were
divided into acts, and even in the case of a
five-act drama there were no intervals
Shakespeare's plays in the early quartos had
no divisions to suggest where pauses were
made, it any during the representation
while in the first folio the editors began to
make divisions, but got tired. Rowe in
1709. was the first to divide the plays ’into
scenes and acts. Yet all the plays, with the
exception of The Tempest, were so con-
structed that characters who leave the
stage at the end of an episode were never
the first to reappear, a reappearance which
would have involved a short pause and an
empty stage; nor, even, does a character
who ends one of the acts marked in the folio
ever begin the one that follows. Poel claims
that a slavish adherence by the actor to
these unfortunate divisions for over 200
years has caused the representation of "the
plays to be in most cases unintelligent, while
also having been the means of misleading
scholars as to Shakespeare’s dramatic con-
struction. And every modern edition of
Shakespeare presents the plays neatly
snipped up into scenes and ’ acts—the
arbitary divisions of post-Elizabethan
editors!
“The comedies were written to be acted
without break, and gain immensely when so
given It cannot be urged too often that
Shakespeare invented his dramatic con-
struction to suit his own particular stage...
An Elizabethan audience was absorbed by the
story of the play, and thought little about
lapse of time or change of place What-
ever information Shakespeare wished the
audience to have on these matters, he put
into the mouths of his characters.”
Poel states that during the last 10 years
of Shakespeare’s life in London the dozen
plays he wrote were far in advance of the
requirements of the day, “since few of
them were printed during the poet’s life-
time.” Some of them, he infers, had only
one performance. Even Shakespeare had
his failures. Pie had outgrown the theatrical
taste of the day, and now cared only for
plays that were “well digested in’ the
scenes,” meaning well constructed.
Jt
As to the plays themselves, Poel has some
acute criticism. Thus The Merchant of
Venice, now played as a tragedy, was “The
Comicall Ilistorie of the Merchant of Ven-
ice,” and in it the story of the three chests
was regarded as of equal interest to the
pound of flesh. Nowadays we get little
about the three chests. Henry V. was “os-
tensibly a ‘Jingo’ play, written to open the
Globe playhouse with a patriotic flourish of
trumpets.” In Hamlet the first quarto stage
direction brings Ophelia on with a lute, and
not vegetables, in her hands ; the Ghost in the
Queen’s chamber wore a dressing-gown, and
not armor ; and Ophelia’s body was followed
to the grave merely by villagers and a soli-
tary priest, instead of the gorgeous funeral
procession we are so familiar with. So in
the Balcony Scene in Romeo and Juliet, the
dialogue in that place of danger must be
“rushed”—whereas nowadays stage Juliets
go through their billing and cooing as de-
liberately as they do their toilets. So to-day
we do not get Shakespeare; we get the
actor-manager’s wrong idea of Shakespeare.
Shakespeare’s plays are mutilated because
they are acted as five-act dramas, because
they have interpolated pictorial effects, and
because actors do not speak the verse with
the wondrous rapidity of the Elizabethans.
All these mutilations are the result of the
invention of the act-drop, the necessity,
which Shakespeare never felt, for a “cur-
tain.”
“How many realise that the art of
Shakespeare’s dramatic construction differs
fundamentally from that of the modern
dramatist? In fact, Pinero would no more
know how to set about writing a play for
the Elizabethan stage, in which the char-
acters appear in the course of the story in
26 different localities during 26 years, than
Shakespeare would know how to make 26
persons live their lives through a whole
play in one room or on one day.”
♦ '
A book of delightful drawings by Pamela
Colman Smith arrives at the right time. It
deals with The Russian Ballet ( Sidgwick
and Jackson, 4s. 6d. out here). The letter-
press, by Ellen Terry, is of the nature of
mere uninformative rhapsody. In England,
she says, the Russian dancers have perhaps
been acclaimed with more whole-hearted
fervor than elsewhere, because before their
coming the land was barren. For its draw-
ings, and for its drawings alone, the book
is worth while.
Science Progress, for July (55.) has a
valuable and common-sense article for the
layman upon Scientific National Defence,
stressing the need for conscription in Eng-
land, and the same review finds room in its
test tubes for such curious things as vol-
canoes, enzymes, phosphorescence, woman,
and the soul.
Just too late for Sydney comes How to
Diagnose Small-pox, by W. Wanklyn, a
manual for general practitioners (Smith,
Elder, 3s. 6d.).

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Gladness of Spring and Some
Explanations.
There is a languid softness in the air;
All day a little wind among the leaves
lias brought me whispers of things strange
and fair
I iom that rich North where tropic beauiv
weaves
Her lairest cloak to hide the old, brown
world.
Now the red sunset droops, her banners
furled
I pon the staff of evening, and the bare
A ide reddened sky waits tremulous to wear
Aight s diadem upon her cloudy hair.
(Which means that after the cold days we
had,
There is a sort of feeling everywhere
lhat things in general are not too bad,
that, so far as I am now aware,
If nothing serious happens before night,
the stars will probably give out some light.)
tt is the happy springtime, rich and free.
All life is powdered with a dust of dreams,
And through that golden shower there wakes
in me
The hidden music of the thing that seems,
b aint magic breathes as with desire to cheat
ihe voice that ever sobs about my feet
Its echo of each day’s reality,
And the wide sweetness of air, sky and sea
Laugh to the lilt of eldest melody.
(Which means that, as the spring is now’ on
tap,
I feel the job at which I earn a crust
Is precious little use. Who cares a rap?
iVe only do our graft because we must.
v\ u ow we’d chuck it quickly if we could,
\\ hile the bright sun outside is shining good.)
Now memory wakens from a winter's sleep.
All hanpy with the tender light that grows
• )ver the distance when the new days keep
' r .vst with the perfume of some faded rose
lhat once delight had worn against her
heart.
All things remembered seem of jov a part,
And in old meadows dreaming hands can
reap
Rich sheaves all ripe for binding. Ghost
hopes sweep
Clear winged as dawn out of time’s purple
deep
(Which means that when you think of how
you snent
Some of your time a year or two ago,
You feel that even paying cent, per cent.
Upon the simple debt of life you owe
Is really not too much for what you got
When pleasure, youth and health were on the
spot.)
Young spring, with perfumed days and silver
nights.
Sweet with warm stars, or murmurous with
doves,
Grows like a vintage kept for new delights
Pressed from the rinened grapes of elder
loves.
The happy skies remember to forget,
And in forgetfulness remember yet
The wonder songs, the glories and the lights
Of that air-builded palace on the heights
Whose door of golden fancy still invites.
(Which means that this peculiar time of
spring
Arranges things to keep our spirits gay.
We pick and choose at wall, and anything
Not worth remembering we may chuck away :
But kissing Jane beneath her small, pii.k
nose
Brings back the taste of Madeline and Rose.)
N.s.w.
PAT O'MAOEL.
The Treason of Truth.
Now damn the thing he told me,
And damn the ears that heard!
May night and death enfold me
if I believe his word—
I low red and sweet the thing I sorely miss,
The hourly dear surrender of her Ins.- !
His story hangs together,
I piece the parts with care—
But, oh, in golden weather
I kissed her falling hair,
And underneath a silver moon we went
When youth and pleasure yielded cent, per
cent.
A thousand memories thronging
Clive force to all he says—
Oh. bitter-sweet the longing
For old joy-careless days !
I feel the wonder-darkness of her eyes
As when their softening made my hot soul
wise.
So simple in the telling,
So plain his story seems :
But false hope still is dwelling
Amid the old sweet dreams.
And shall I care if what he says be true,
Remembering all the glad young truth I
knew V
To all his clear words spoken
Shall I give thought or heed
Who hold the golden token
Of each remembered deed?
I let the traitor thing he spoke go by,
And give his truth, if it be truth, the lie.
For what are truth and lying
But tricks of time and chance?
And this were time defying—
The memory of a glance
So full of that dear thing that has no name,
Beyond the scope of chance or wrong or
blame.
And if the tale he told me
From her own lips I heard.
May night and death enfold me
If I should hold that word
Against the thing I should no longer miss,
The sweet surrender of her new love kiss!
N.S.W.
D.M.W.

COMMER MOTOR LORRIES
l
Again Victorious
At the 7th Annual Motor Parade of the Commercial Motor Users’ Association, in
London, on May 12, “Commer” Lorries won high distinction. The 10 principal
makers of petrol chassis were represented by 201 vehicles of from 30cwt. to 5 tons
capacity. Of these “Commers” totalled 41 cars —aft average of over 20 per cent.
The other nine makes ranged from 30 lorries down to 7. Fifteen “Commers” were
awarded prizes—a higher proportion of honors than were received by any other
make.
Commer Driver Wins Championship
The Championship Prize of £lO was won by the driver of a “Commer.” This car
has run over 7000 miles without mishap of any kind, and without a single replace-
ment. Petrol consumption averaged no less than 15£ miles per gallon. The gross
ton miles per gallon averaged the extremely high figure of 82.
“Commers” are specially adapted for Australian needs and conditions. Anyone
interested in road transport should make early investigation of these cars. The
point of greatest significance is that “Commers” have gained their awards in the
country where they have been longest used.
JUST LANDED—3Ocwt. AND 2-TON MODELS

By sending direct to The Bulletin Office Twenty-
Six Shillings Sterling you can have The Bulletin
posted regularly to you every week for a year.
Cheques. Drafts, etc., which should he crossed, and
all Business Communications to be addressed to The
.1/ anager.
How to Address Letters.
Correspondents to The Bulletin would oblige the
paper (and, to an extent, themselves) by taking some
care as to methods of address.
Business letters (with regard to subscriptions, ad-
vertisements, etc.) should in no case be addressed to
the Editor, but to the Manager. To send them to
the Editor is to invite inattention.
It is not a good thing to address editorial letters
to anyone by name. “The Editor, The Bulletin,” is
the proper superscription for “copy)’ Delay, and
even loss, is sometimes caused by addressing to per-
sons instead of officials. The person may not be
there; the official always is.
Special Notice.
Insufficiently stamped letters are liable to be re-
fused.
The contents of this paper are copyright by the
publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
part of the contents of this number is hereby ex-
pressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read, and, where prac-
ticable, acknowledge in the “Correspondence” col-
umn all contributions submitted, whether in the form
of Political, Social or other Articles, Verse, Short
Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Australian sub-
jects, and not exceeding two columns in length, or,
say.: tOOO words, are specially acceptable), Paragraphs
Letters or Newspaper Clippings. All communications
will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when
the addiess is specified and stamps are enclosed. But
no MS. will be returned, or preserved, unless these
conditions are observed. When MS. is forwarded
without stamps for return it will be understood that
the copy is to be destroyed if not acceptable. Per-
sonal applications for return of MS. cannot receive
attention. The Bulletin desires, further, those
sending MS. to distinctly understand that it is not
responsible for the preservation or transmission of

To Amateurs in Black and White.
Anyone supplying to The Bulletin original
humorous or other topical drawings in line or wash,
of sufficient merit and interest and adapted for
direct reproduction, or any interesting photograph
illustrative of a current event, will be paid for same
on acceptance. Any picture containing a good idea,
but unfitted for direct reproduction—i.e., any picture
which has to be re-drawn —will be paid for as a
suggestion only. The conditions under which such
artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS.

THURSDAY, SEPTEMBER 18, 1913.
DECENTRALISATION AND
SUNDRIES.
One of the few bright spots of N.S.W.’s
present Government (the spot hasn’t actu-
ally materialised yet. but there are hopes
that it will do so) is the proposal that
Victoria should push some of its sft. Sin.
railways a little way into the Riverina dis-
trict, and give that country access to Mel-
bourne. And but for the fact that Vic-
toria has got the congestion misery quite
as badly as the Ma State, and won’t give
reasonable means of transit to such places
as Portland and Western Port, which are
many miles and a thousand years ahead of
Melbourne as harbors, the new idea would
be even better than it is—but that isn’t the
fault of the X.S.W. Government. If Vic-
toria spreads certain of its lines into
N.S.W. it will be, so far as these extensions
are concerned, simply a form of Private
Enterprise—that is to sav. that certain
lines, owned by 1,300.000 odd of Victorian
shareholders, will butt into N.S.W. for the
ournose of earning profit. The Private
Enterprise aspect of the business is to be
regretted, but apparently it can’t be helped.
Part of the district which it is proposed to
attach to Melbourne might be more logically
connected with Adelaide, but something has
gone wrong with the logic. Part of it might
possibly be served by a connection with
Twofold Bay. in N.S.W., if Twofold Bay
w r as developed on the same generous lines
as Sydney; but Twofold Bay is anathema
in Sydnev. just as Portland is in Melbourne.
Yet whatever may be wrong with the details
of the scheme, it has one supreme merit. It
is a recognition of the fact that the dog-in-
the-manger policy doesn’t pay. The old idea
was that, if any part of a State was too far
from the capital to trade profitably through
that capital, it was better to make it an
unneopled desert and a rabbit haunt and a
waste of cactus than to let it trade any-
where else. And thereby the State lost
population, revenue, defenders, land values
and all manner of assets.
The unwise old Congestion party has
raised a shriek, as might have been expected,
the loudest element of the shriek up to date
being a green pamphlet by one C.
Churchill Tucker, of London, apparently
an exported Cornstalk. In his herb-colored
document he sums up the case for Conges-
tion and parochialism in this mad fashion: —
In conclusion, I would say that why we want our
southern country friends to deal with us instead
of Melbourne is because we want our railways to
carry their produce and their purchases.
YVe want our stock and station and commission
agents to have the disposal of their produce.
We want our carters and wharf-laborers to handle
the produce.
Our clerks to do the clerical work.
Our port to get the dues; also outward and
inward wharfage, etc.
Our shipping to have the freights.
Our banks to hold the proceeds of sales and tran-
sact other matters of finance.
We also want our country friend? to buy from us
direct or from storekeepers who cfeal with N. S.
Wales firms, because we 'want our manufacturers
and merchants to supply the goods required, such
as furniture, agricultural machinery, boots and
clothing, hats, blankets, tinned meat, jams, and, in
fact, all necessities that are not locally produced.
We want our factory and store hands employed
in the supply of these necessities.
YVe want the profits of trade kept in N. S. YVales.
YY r e want the money made in N. S. Wales spent
in the State that has to bear the cost of State gov-
ernment
YY’e want to draw our people closer together, for
there is nothing like trade influence to achieve this
end.
We want N. S. Wales to be not only one of the
largest, but to continue to be the most prosperous
State of the Commonwealth.
To do this our legislators must give their first
attention to collecting the trade to N. S. Wales
ports, not to divert it into the ports of other States.
* * * .
It is a fair statement of the Congestion
party’s case—that is to say, it is a very
foolish statement of a very foolish proposi-
tion. For “our banks” (ie., N.S.W. banks)
don’t necessarily get \the business, even
though all the trade is dragged to Sydney ;
plenty of Sydney traders keep their accounts
in Victorian or Queensland or English
banks, and plenty of Melbourne and Ade-
laide traders do their business with N.S.W.
banks. .And “our stock and station and
commission agents” don’t necessarily handle
the produce because the trade comes to
Sydney; a whole lot of it goes to outside
firms with branches in Sydney, and a whole
lot of Melbourne and Adelaide trade goes to
N.S.W. firms with branches outside. Then
N.S.W. has such a tremendous faith in the
importing disease that, even if all the for-
eign business of the State is dragged
to Sydney, the chief result will apparently
be to increase the influx of foreign goods, to
the greater profit of trading firms which, in
a very large number of'cases, have their
headquarters and divide their profits in
London, or some other remote spot. The
statement that we “want to draw our people
closer together” is midsummer madness in
the case of a State which has drawn its
people so closely together that its one big
citv is now getting more than half of the
new population that comes to the country,
either by birth or importation. The
“profits of the trade” are not “kept in
N.S.W.” under the present arrange-
ment ; in fact, the profits are exported on
such a huge scale to pay for foreign goods
that even the most tremendous foreign bor-
rowing on record can’t prevent a steady de-
cline in the local stock of cash. And the
nlea that all N.S.W. trade should come to
Sydney, or be killed dead if the distance to
Sydney is too great to be profitably nego-
tiated. so that “our shipping” may “have
the freights” is a strangely moonstruck in-
spiration. Exports for foreign lands are
carried away from Sydney by British.
French, German, Japanese. Maoriland, and
occasionally by Swedish. Austrian, United
States. Chilian and other vessels, and the
Burns-Philp firm, which is mostly a
Queensland concern, does a lot of foreign
business. But “our” ships, in the purely
N.S.W. sense, are very, very scarce. Th°v
don’t even have a monopoly of the N.S.W.
coastal trade, or anything dimly resembling
one. In fact, you can meet about as much
of “our shinning” in Melbourne harbor as
in Sydnev harbor, and other States’ ship-
ping is similarly mixed.
This pamphlet is quoted because it em-
bodies in about the smallest possible space
the whole gospel of Parochialism. And the
author, who wants to keep all the profits of
N.S.W. trade in Sydney, is spending his
own profits in England. He didn’t even
give a Sydney printer whatever profit was
to be made out of publishing his narrative
about Sydney. It bears the imprint of a
London firm.
The inference drawn is that, except in the
matter of crowding an undue share of the
population into one bloated city of many
slums and exaggerated rents, the Congestion
policy has been a failure. It hasn’t kept
the supply of goods in the hands of N.S.W.
manufacturers or even in the hands of
N.S.W. importing firms. It hasn’t kept the
shipping business in the hands of N.S.W.
owners; in fact, the Australasian shipping
firms which _haY 7 e struck out most notably
into the foreign trade hail from Queensland
and Maoriland —-States which have in a
great measure avoided the Congestion dis-
ease. Sydney is overrun with banks, life
insurance offices, fire insurance offices, pas-
companies and other enterprises which
distribute their profits outside the State. The
number of absentees and outsiders who own
propertY" in Sydney is very large. The num-
ber of foreign bondholders who collect their
interest from the Treasury at Sydney is
enormous. Plainly the policy of crowding
as much as possible of the State’s trade and
as much as possible of the State’s popula-
tion into one centre, all by reason of a de-
ranged variety of local patriotism* hasn’t
produced the benefits that were expected.
In two cases N.S.W. has departed
to some small extent, from the Con-
gestion policy. Despite the invention of
dmeientiai railway rates to drag the trade
to Sydney, and despite the fact that the
break of gauge at the border gives Sydney a
distinct advantage, the dwellers alongside
the Sydney-Melbourne line and the Sydney-
Brisbane line have some sort of a chance to
trade with the most convenient market.
There is no reason to suppose that either
Sydney or the rest of N.S.W. has
lost through this partial departure from the
Great Local Superstition. Those railways
pay, while a very large number of dead-
end lines which connect only with the local
metropolis and drag goods needless and un-
conscionable distances to get there, don’t.
During the financial year which ended on
June 30, 1912, the State had 33 bankrupt
railways (or sections), and the aggregate
loss on them was £357.000, a great part of
which burden Sydney had to carry. It is
practically certain that the list is
longer now and the loss greater. Whether the
scrap of commission (largely collected by
foreign firms) which Sydney got by taking
the few goods which these bankrupt lines
carried off' the trucks (really owned by the
foreign Cohen), and putting them on to
foreign-owned shins, was equal to Syd-
ney’s share of the loss, it is impossible to
say. Most likely it wasn’t.
As already mentioned, N.S.W. owns
two railways which represent some
slight departure from the Congestion dis-
ease, and they pay their way. Apart from
parasitic suburbs like Parramatta, health
resorts like Katoomba, and mining towns,
which are liable to spring up wherever min-
erals are found, the most prosperous country
centres of the State are situated beside these
lines. When a district is dependent on its
soil, and has no natural advantage over a
thousand other districts, it seems that the
best luck is with the place which has man-
aged to get furthest away from the Paro-
chial complaint. Albury and Wagga cer-
tainly do a lot of their trade with Mel-
bourne, and Armidale and Glen Innes have
an alternative outlet at Brisbane. Yet these
towns are more prosperous, and supply the
Sydney Treasury with more revenue, and
furnish the Sydney banks with more busi-
ness than all manner of dead-end places like
Day and Hell and Booligal, which are as
much as possible cut off from their nearest
exits in the supposed interests of the Ma
capital. The only counties dependent on
railway communication outside the metro-
politan area .and outside one or two mining
districts, which show any progress or any
population worth mentioning, or which con-
tribute enough to the Treasury at Sydney
to pay for their own axle grease and for th«
arrest of the local drunk, are those situated
on the routes which represent the two nota-
ble depai fures from the old narrow-minded
misery. They are very mild departures, yet
suggest that there are possibilities in
the business, and that a larger outlook mav
pay an even bigger dividend.
The proposal of the Holman-McGowen
Government to give certain parts of the
Riverina access to Melbourne is only a
small beginning in the matter of decentrali-
sation The great need is to develop everv
nrst-class deep-water port in Australia and
to connect with it the lands which natur-
a uu? on , g it: - Australia is, and will
probably be lor many years, an exporting
country in the matter of w T ool and when* -
and other produce, and it will be a great
gain to Australia if its wheat and wool and
other produce can take the shortest road (o
market. That historical benefactor who
made two blades of grass grow where
one grew before wasn’t in the same cate-
gory with the benefactor who made a bale
of hay—which is dried grass—travel one
mile where it travelled two miles before.
However small and preliminary it may be
the proposals- of the N.S.W. Ministry em-
body the recognition of a great principle,
ft is that a great State doesn’t live for the
sake ot one big citv. and that in a State of
144 counties the 143 which don’t happen to
contain the capital have still some claim
to consideration. There have been countries
in which the whole nation was supposed t<»
be of no account if. its interests conflicted
with those of the king (as in monarchical
h ranee i. or of the nobility (as in Poland),
or of the clergy (as in the old Papal terri-
tory ). The idea of a country in which the
interests of a nation are of no account as
compared with the porno and pride of a
single bulky centre is a similar develop-
ment. It. doesn’t seem much better than
some previous developments.
the resurrection,
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Liberals were hopeful of a joyous and general re-
surrection at the next political call.—Jot; Cook.
Now, Gabriel, whose name is Joe.
Blew loud his trumpet o’er the laud,
And they who long had slept below
Rose from their graves on ev’ry hanu.
And over-ran the earth in crowds,
With grave-clods clinging to their shrouds.
The ponderous old Tory gent,
Whose spaciou,s fat had long been dust,
Distributed where’er he went
Effluvia of mould and must,
And fainted at his second birth
To note such Change upon the earth,
A resurrected Socialist
Gazed sadly round and shook his head :
“The same abuses still exist.
Reform is very slow,” he said.
And close behind the Labor man
There stalked a Red Republican
Poor old Freetrade, whose grave-clothes
hung
In rags upon his wasted frame,
Wept loud to find himself among
Old friends who now denied his name,
And sought a champion fruitlessly
With Reid’s Dry Dog for company.
The dead Black Labor Advocate
Arose and promptly threw a fit
To find the land in such a state;
Its color pleased him not a bit.
And. with a heavy heart, forthwith
He sought to interview Bruce Smith.
An old Conservative whose law
Was, ‘‘Keep the worker in his placp ”
Grew purple at the things he saw
With horror on his ancient face'
He gave one snort of wild disdain
And clamored for his grave again.
A dead Protectionist arose,
And, as he scanned the imports list
He swore aloud and tore his clothes
And madly shook a bony fist.
“They haven’t got it yet!” he cried
‘■’Twas promised long before I died!’
A Hempire Booster just reborn
Waved high a flag with loud “Hip hips”
And noted with an eye of scorn
Australia’s new array of ships.
“Our battles will be fought,” said he
“And won upon the Northern Sea!”
And represented in that band
Was ev’ry Party ever seen :
Wreckers and saviors of the land,
And all the shapes and shades between •
Statesmen of ev’ry kind and hue.
Indeed, it was a motley crew.
And 10. amongst the grisly crowd.
Conservatives and Democrats,
Arose a conflict shrill and loud.
Pike to the noise of countless cats.
“The w\ rid would be at peace to-day.”
Each said, “if they had gone my way !”
And then, with measured tread and slow.
His famous trumpet in his hand.
Came Gabriel, whose name is Joe,
Unto the resurrected band.
They saw him ; and. with one accord.
“Brother !” the whole assembly roared.
“My friend !” exclaimed the Freetrade man.
“Mine!” yelled the staunch Protectionist!
“Mine !” cried the Red Republican.
“Nay, mine !” declared the Socialist.
And all along the motley line
The echo ran : “He’s mine ! He’s mine !”
And once again ’midst the reborn
Arose another wild affray.
Halos were twisted, shrouds were torn.
Forgotten oaths rained thick that day.
“Hold! Hold!” cried Joe. “Attend to me.
Let me disclose my policy !”
Then on a tombstone mounted Joe,
And there expounded his Great Plan.
But, as he spoke, ’midst those below
A small, disgusted whisper ran:
“Ah. what’s the use? It’s just the «ame!
Office !. .. . The old, old Party game !”
A Tory was the first to go.
“I’ve heard all this before.” said he.
“I’d honed for something fresh from Joe.
That’s always been our policy!”
One more disgusted snort be gave.
Then snuggled down into his grave.
And one by one they slunk away.
And sought their divers cemet’ries.
And one by one each turned to saw
“We hate such silly tricks as these.
Don’t blow that thing again, we pray.
Until you’ve something fresh to say.”
The evening lirht came dour and grey.
And 10, a sodden silence fell
Upon the resurrection day.
And not a soul was left to tell
rr hat sorry tale of graveyard woe.
But Gabriel, whose name is Joe.

PLAIN ENGLISH.
Hezekiah anti “The Bulletin.”
This paper hates to speak lightly of the
dead, even when the dead are as dead as
Hezekiah, one time king of Judah. Still
it_ feels bound, in self-defence, to take notice
of a sermon recently delivered at the Ly-
ceum Hall, Sydney, by a certain Rev. P. J.
Stephen. The following are some extracts
from the Sun’s report:—
THRIFTLESS YOUTHS.
ONE OF AUSTRALIA’S SINS.
THOUSANDS DODGING WORK.
CLERGYMAN’S FIERCE INDICTMENT.
The sermon preached at the Lyceum Hall on Sun-
day night by the Rev. P. J. Stephen would have
done a lot of young men good had they heard it.
It would have hit them right where they lived.
The story of how Hezekiah, King of Judah, faced
tremendous odds which beset him and eventually
won out was told. His father, Ahaz, was a vicious
and worthless man, and the task of the son to lift
Judah out of political and moral bondage was enor-
mous. The words of the preacher as applied to
Hezekiah were the basis of remarks to young men
who lived in Sydney. The handicap which Heze-
kiah had has proved the ruin of many a promising
life....
“A man of wide culture, but notorious for his fits
of passion, said, when someone rebuked him, ‘lt’s no
use talking, my father was just so, my boys are just
so. We cannot live at peace, and never could. We
are all possessed of the same devil.’. .. .
“Now listen ! If anyone says ‘That’s me,’ I want
him to remember Hezekiah. He won out in the end
despite his handicap. He mastered himself, and
self-mastery is essential to making life a success.
Another thing about Hezekiah was that he was not
afraid to work. The open road to success has
always been work. If young men think they are
going to win in the game of life without the sweat
of brain or body they are mistaken. Industrious
work is a prime necessity of life. ...
“The young fellow whose intellectual wardrobe
consists of The Bulletin and a few betting lists
will never make good.”
The Bulletin asks, with all seriousness,
what it has done that it should be classed
as lower than Hezekiah. What is wrong
with this parson, and what is the excite-
ment about, and why has Hezekiah been
dug up so suddenly? It isn’t on record
that that king did much for religion, sav*'
that he broke up the brass serpent of
Moses and called it “Nehushtan,” which
simply means “brazen,” and everybody
knew the old thing was brazen. It is said
that he made things unpleasant for the
Philistines, but they were a mere handful,
and it isn’t mentioned that Hl v ekiah ever
put on his armor and risked ius own pre-
cious life in the field, as did bad
Scriptural characters like Jehobam and
Ahab —picturesque old Aiiab of the
drought and the idols, who is said to have
knocked saucepans out of 33 kings who
came up against him. and made the great
Assyrian monarch see stars at the battle
of Karkar, and who is last heard of like a
strenuous warrior in his chariot, mortally
wounded but still refusing to lfe down.
When an enemy of size came up against
Hezekiah he stripped G n, '’s house of n s
riches to buy him off. When the enenu
came again Hezekiah the shmibl have been

and vvept and tore his clothes. He made a
great fuss when he had a boil. It is set
forth in Holy Writ that he “made a pool
and a conduit and brought water into the
city” (Jerusalem), which may be quite
accurate, for the pool is there yet. It is
very small and very dirty, and it smells
badly—this writer has smelled it. (The
water, by the way, must have been

was told that, by reason of his own

kingdom would be wrecked soon after
his death, and that his sons should be
“eunuchs in the palace of the King of Baby-
lon,” the selfish old Hebrew gentleman said :
“Is it not good if peace and truth be in my
days”—which was the same as Louis NV.’s
theory that “Things will last my time.”
Furthermore. Hezekiah seems to have
really been a kind of heathen, for the Law
of the Lord was re-discovered by accident
in the days of his grandson Josiaii ; and it
had been lost so long that the community
was utterly staggered to find out what the
Law of the Lord really was. Apparently
even the Oldest Jew Inhabitant hadn’t
heard of it. Taking one thing with another,
the much-advertised son of Ahaz and father
of Manasseh appears on the yellow old
leaf of history as a futile, selfish, but well-
meaning person—no soldier, not much of a
statesman, and very much afraid of death.
A narson ought to know Scripture history
better than to dig him un as a hero. Even
poor old Hoshea, of Israel, who held
Samaria during a three-years’ siege, when
his kingdom was at its last gasp, looks more
heroic. The next cleric who quotes Heze-
kiah to the detriment of this paper will bo
sued for libel to the uttermost twopence of
the statutes.
The Mystery Called Trade.
One Ernest Braby blossomed forth one
recent day in Sydnev Telegraph under the
heading: “Is England Decadent Under
Freetrade?” He said that it wasn’t, and
this paper doesn’t propose to contradict
him. because the matter isn’t really worth
while. But he quoted these (among other)
figures re “trade”—and “trade” is a poor
thing at the best —as evidence, and the evi-
dence is not quite convincing:—
Exports in 1910.—United Kingdom, £ 430,384.772 ;
Germany. £367,506,000; United States,
£356,267,000.
It seems to be largely a matter of how the
accounts are kept. There are four great
States in the world’s eye—the British Em-
pire, the Russian Empire, the United States
Republic and the German Empire. The last
is a small thing compared with the other
three, but it bulks large by sheer energy
and aggressiveness. Britain began with the
British Islands, and grew. Russia began
with the Grand Duchy of Moscow, and
grew. The United States began with a
small scrap of territory in the north-east
coast and an indefinite claim to some empty
regions beyond. But Russia doesn’t include
in its trade returns the dealings between
the old Grand Duchy and the later acquisi-
tions —Russian Poland. Novgorod, Lith-
uania. the Crimea, the Ukraine, Bessarabia.
Siberia, Russian Turkestan, etc. Finland
is the one scrap of Russ territory which is
regarded as foreign in this sense. Nor does
the United States include in its trade re-
turns the exchange of goods between the
original republic and its later Continental
additions —Florida. Louisiana, New Mexico,
Arizona, California and some sundries.
Prussia was the basis of the German Empire,
but when Prussia sells a pair of pants or a
bottle of gum to Bavaria or Hanover or
Wurtemburg, that isn’t included in the
commerce figures. These exchanges are re-
garded as a matter of local shopkeeping,
just like the trade which Poplar (London)
does with Wandsworth (London). But the
British trade which looms so large includes
even Bull’s buyings and sellings with the
Channel Islands, which are almost in sight
of his own coast and have been his property
for more than 800 years. And it includes
the vfflue of every scrap of commercial in-
tercourse with every other fragment of the
British Empire, including even (figures for
1912) the £5OOO worth of British products
sent to Ascension, the £lOOO worth sent to
Swaziland, and the £2OOO worth sent to Bri-
tish Somaliland, as well as the £85,000.000
worth sent to Australia. It is really a mat-
ter of classing as external trade what some
other countries define as internal trade. If
Britain kept its accounts as Russia does, in
the year 1912 fully £350.000.000 worth of
alleged commerce would have vanished out
of the category. This would have made Bri-
tain neither richer nor poorer, but it would
certainly have altered the appearance of
things.
The Futility of Treaties and Great
Powers, and Tripe of that Sort.
When some peaceful and non-resistant Aus-
tralians began to rage, a few years ago,
against local defence, a very common theory
of theirs was to the effect that this country
might in some way be declared neutral. The
Hague Peace Conference, which doesn’t
own a ship or a regiment or even a police-
man, was to be approached, and the Great
Powers were to be beguiled by some un-
known method into guaranteeing Australia,
at their own risk and cost, against attack
and against an inrush of colored immi-
grants. Recent events should convince
these harmless folk that the Great Powers
are a broken reed, and that the Lord only
helps him who helps himself, and even then
only provided he helps himself -harder than
the other fellow does. The integrity of Turkey
was guaranteed by several Great Powers,
and has been so ensured for a long time.
When the small Balkan States fell upon
Abdul Fez, these Powers didn’t move, but
they warned the small and angry States
that they wouldn’t be allowed to annex
any of Abdul , Fez’s territory. The plain-
tiffs annexed the defendant’s territory just
the same, and the Powers didn’t shift- in
fact, they presently lielned to fix up a treaty
by which almost all European Turkey (in-
cluding Adrianople, which went to Bul-
garia) was divided among the Confederates.
Then the Confederates started fighting
among themselves, and the Great I owers
warned them in a loud and terrible voice to
stop. They didn’t stop, and the Powers
only loafed. Turkey seized its opportunity,
and tore up the treaty of London, and re-
captured Adrianople. The Great Powers
gave it short notice to leave off, and it re-
plied to the effect that if the Powers didn t
like it they could lump it. The Powers
considered their rotten little antagonist
and regretfully lumped it. Bulgaria said it
would rely on the Powers to enforce the
-treaty of London and make Turkey hand
the lost city, but the six big nations
tell asleep. Nobody supposes that the Brit-
ish or I rench or Germans or Austrians or
Russians are a whit less brave or less fond
ot fighting than they were. But war has
become so vast and elaborate and so ruin-
ously . expensive that no nation goes cam-
paigning nowadays in any other nation’s
quarrel unless it can see a very big dividend
tor itself in the business, or unless its own
existence is seriously threatened by some
side issue of the disturbance and it has to
take up its gun in self-defence. It takes
000 years or so to pay the bill for a year’s
campaign; wherefore the Great Powers
look on while the most beautiful treaties
and guarantees—some of them even more
beautiful than the Anglo-Jap. arrangement
which is supposed to protect Australia —
are used as pipe-lights by tenth-rate
Governments that any really serviceable
nation could lick before breakfast, or at
least before lunch. The staggering appari-
tion of the bill has got the richest Govern-
ments down, and each one of them feels as
if it was a fallen horse with a pawnbroker
sitting on its head. International politics
have become, to all intents and purposes, a
game in which every State plays its own
hand. The casual old days of cheap war,
when even Norway didn’t mind sending a
few troops to help Greece, and Venice gave
Scotland a hand in a moment of stress,
are quite gone. Nations can no longer
afford to be affable and casual. This is
one reason why Australia should take its
defence system in earnest —which it can
hardly be said to have done up to date.

“Loyal” Ulster.
A great manv people have been moved, on
one or two occasions, to remark that the
Unionist or anti-Home-Ruler or “loyalist”
of Ulster (Ireland) is a fraud and a weari-
ness, a three-card trick, a small and adul-
terated beer, an undersized egg, and a
cheap cotton imitation of an umbrella that
doesn’t know when to come in out of the wet.
To-day that bumptious public swindler is
looming up so largely in the cablegrams
that they are incited to repeat the observa-
tion. Ireland is divided into four provinces,
and except for the fact that Dublin Uni-
versity is allowed to return two members,
and that, in accordance with old University
tradition, it elects a pair of Tory lawyers,
there is no anti-Home-Rule person returned
outside of Ulster. Leinster, Munster and
Connaught are solid for Irish Nationalism.
At last election 48 Home Rule members
were returned by these provinces unopposed.
In 17 cases a Home Ruler was returned
after a battle with another Home Ruler, but
the Unionists didn’t even put up the sorry
pretence of nominating a candidate. In
exactly three electorates outside Ulster
(apart, of course, from Dublin University)
did the Tory party think it worth while to
nominate a “loyalist” to carry its tattered
banner, and in each case the downfall was
memorable. In Ulster, however, there was
the usual battle. Ulster returns 33 mem-
bers, and 16 of those chosen at last general
election were in favor of Home Rule, while
17 were hostile. Eighteen out of the 33
were unopposed, and the 18 were equ-
ally divided; so there were actually
nine constituencies in allegedly Unionist
Ulster in which it wasn’t good enough for
Unionism to set up a competitor. In the
contested electorates —-15 in all —Unionism
scored 50,048 votes and Home Rule 42,925.
Even the city of Belfast returned one Home
Rule member out of four, and in London-
derry, where the great siege was, the Union-
ist only scraped in by 2415 votes against
the Home Rule man’s 2310. Ulster threat-
ens to rise and break things, and tear the
heavens asunder, and make a general smash
of facts and circumstances, if Ireland gets
the same measure of self-government that
is granted to the Isle of Man (about half-
way between England and Ireland), and to
the Channel Islands (off the south coast.
of England). Among these remarkable
Channel Islands <7O souare miles in all),
Jersey has a Parliament of its own, Alder-
ney has another, and Guernsey and Sark
have a Legislature between them, yet all this
Home Rule (for 70 square miles of country)
brings about no overwhelming disaster.
Ulster, as already mentioned, just contrives
at present to maintain a majority of one
against Home Rule (there was an occasion
when the majority was the other way
about), and in the other three-quarters of
the island no anti-llome Rule member can
get a footing, save the pair that are re-
turned by the 4997 hobbledehoys of the Uni-
versity. And the bare half of Ulster which
is “loyal,” according to its own definition
of “loyalty,” is the half which is occupied
bv foreigners, just as the very small scrap of
Italy which remained partially loyal to Aus-
tria in the Garibaldian days was the bit
that was occupied by Austrians, and the
fragment of Bulgaria which stood bv Turkey
in 1877 and ’7B was the fragment in which
the Turks predominated. Also, the “loyal”
speck of Ulster is the speck which objects
about equally to the prevailing religion of
Ireland and the established religion of
England. Many of its people view
George Y. as a disguised emissary of
Rome, Anglicanism and Romanism being so
much alike to the really solid Presbyterian
that it is hardly worth while to draw dis-
tinctions between them. One way and an-
other, the upheaval of “loyal” Ulster—the
place where originated the conspiracy to de-
throne and possibly murder young Queen
Victoria, and put that violent Orangeman,
the Duke of Cumberland, in her place—is
something of a jest.
A Disloyalist.
Norman Bayles, membah for Toorak,
where the best inotah people come from,
was responsible for a vain discussion in
Vic. Leg. Assembly re the utterances of
labor membah Webber on the subject of
King George and the British monarchy.
Webber has no boundless opinion of
kings and thrones, and in his other
capacity as mayor of Richmond (Melb.)
he dropped the toast of his Gracious Ma-
jesty’s health from his speech list.
Thus Bayles got an opportunity to drag
Webber’s “disloyalty” into Parliamentary
light, by moving that the House “emphati-
cally condemns” any conduct derogatory
to its royal figure-head. The remarks of
Bayles seemed more foolish than the
tilings young man Webber had said and
done. The “disloyalist” in question merely
reiterated the eternal truism that
monarchies are losing their hold on super-
stitious reverence. The fact that in Par-
liament he took an oath of allegiance to
the reigning Sovereign as nominal head of
the blessed Constitution should perhaps
have kept his mouth shut, but his opinion
that a Constitution could get along better
without a King was not an emphatically
condemnatory opinion anyhow. And
Webber hadn’t taken an oath to make the
King a subject for chronic drinking, or to
get on his ear under the table in an excess
of loyalty. Nor is it pretended that even
if Webber had beered and wined and
whiskied himself into an earlv grave or an
inebriate asylum over the King’s health the
King would have been one iota more
healthy. Norman Bayles’s speech in pro-
pounding the motion (which was bound to
be carried) voiced all the piffle that might
be expected of the chosen of Tooralt. He
said “the present King showed a kindly sen-
timent to the poor and was a cordial Eng-
lish gentleman.” Also Bayles made the
astounding statement that it is not right to
refer to any King as a possible “nincom-
poop.” Considering that Bayles’s great-
grandfather lived at the time of a George
111. who was often insane, whilst his eldest
son, the Regent, was a drunken black-
guard, who made sport of his royal and
“dotty” parent, the reference to nincom-
poopery was absurd. And as for George
the Fifth being an English gentleman with
kindly feelings for the poor, the chances
are that Victorian Labor member Webber
is more kindly disposed to the poor than
Norman Bayles. In any case, the fee'ings
of George. Webber or Bayles have
nothing to do with the monarchical prin-
ciple.
A Gerrymander.
The Redistribution of Seats Bill brought in
by the L j eake Government in S. Aus. is one
of the most brazen samples of gerrymander-
ing this generation has known. Whatever
the intention, the result of the redistribution
is to cut out all Labor strongholds and segre-
gate them from the other parts of existing
districts. Thus Port Pirie has been sliced
from the district of Stanley, which now re-
turns three Labor members, and is given
two members of its own, while the rest of
ihe constituency, being warranted to swal-
low Liberals, is also given two. There is a
clear gain of two to the Ministerial cause,
and a clear loss of one to the Laborites. The
same thing is done at Jack Verran’s cita-
del in Wallaroo, which with the mining
centres of Moonta and Kadina has been
chopped off from the surrounding district of
Yorke Peninsula: so two more good Liberals
are provided for, and one Labor man is
turned adrift. Then the metropolitan area,
which now sends to the Assembly 10 Labor
members and only two Liberals (these two
got in by a fluke at last election for Tor-
rens), is to be so dissected that there will be
no chance for tile Labor crowd to work its
wicked way in that wholesale manner. That,
at least, is what the Liberals hope, although
the experience of Westralia, where “sure”
Liberal seats were swept by Labor candi-
date* owing to popular indignation, may be
repeated in S.A. The ruthlessness of the
whole gerrymander can be judged from the
fact that the metropolitan quota for a mem-
ber is over 7000 voters, while the country
quota is as low as 2600, and the cunning
of the scheme is shown by the other fact
that in Barossa, where the country vote
swamps that of the town of Gawler, there
has been no attempt to cut the town away,
because that would have the effect of giving
the Labor party a seat which it cannot
capture at present. As the Government
has a bic majority in both the Council and
the Assembly, there is no chance to _ stop
the political swindle unless public opinion
can be aroused. “We are not willing to
submit the scheme to an independent Elec-
toral Commission : we intend to stick to our
Bill,” is the latest pronouncement.. Inci-
dentally the Bill increases the Council mem-
bers from 18 to 20 and the Assembly mem-
bers from 40 to 46. and all the new men
are confidently expected to be obsequious
Liberals.
Guff, Plain and Fancy.
“The Snake” writes a fragment: —
I sidled into the annual dinner of the New South
Wales Chamber of Manufactures one evening last
week at Aaron’s Exchange Hotel, Sydney, and
had a rare blow-out of plain and fancy guff. ihe
principal guests were representatives of the British
Parliamentary party, now being rushed through
a few acres of the continent, and the way they
scooped up the fried, boiled and scalloped gun
before passing the dish back to the Australians who
were waiting to wallow in it themselves was the big
thrill of the “dinnah,” as Lord Emmott (Under-
secretary of State for the Colonies) rightly chris-
tened or designated it. It was a dinnah; in tact,
you could, without being unduly charged with
perjury, say it was a Dinnah. But the low-minded
person that snarls at the Australian, \ ah, dis-
loyalist!” he would have been prosecuted, with the
utmost bulldog of the law, for bearing false wit-
ness. All the old stock gags that have made the
Hempire’s fame at the National Amphitheatre, the
Pavilion and other Saturday-night temples of the
nation were hallelujahed to breathlessness ; and bare
mention of the “dear ole mother-land” was the sign
for jackjohnsoning the table with forks, sauce-
bottles and assorted crockery. I came very near to
doing in a 71b. cruet myself, but that was when
president Pratten was tickling the welkin with
the lost chord of Australia. If I hadn t discov-
ered in time that I was making a Sheol of an uproar
just when the company had suddenly decided that
to whirl crockerv was vulgar, that cruet would have
come back to Aaron’s in splinters.
Pratten and Admiral King-Hall, when the cnance
came their wav, gave the Australian national note
the good, sturdy twang that should have dominated
the night’s chorus. “We are doing the best we can
to build up a young nation,” said Pratten man-
fully “strong to defend, and even to help if neces-
sary.’ We believe that the Imperial umbrella covers
the whole of John Bull’s numerous family, and we
are going to help Bull to hold the umbrella up.”
That went great with the assembly. Then he pic-
tured how it was to be done —partly with warships
built in AUSTRALIA, in AUSTRALIAN dockyards
capable of cradling Dreadnoughts, by AUSTRALIAN
workmen, from AUSTRALIAN steel, paid for with
AUSTRALIAN gold and manned and officered by
AUSTRALIAN citizens. Hereabouts his luck pet-
ered out. The crowd rose and howled that direful
drivel of our fathers, “Rule Britannia.”
It was then that Emmott arose and opined they
were at a dinnah —a very good dinnah—a dinnah
at which the food, drinks and smokes were, he
understood, Australian-made. Well, he didn’t think
he would be any the worse for that when he woke
in the morning) (Shrieks of laughter!) Here was
British wit right off the Imperial stove! None of
your d—d up-country touch there! For minutes he
stood, thin, straight* and cold of countenance, while
the tumult of annreciation raged round him. When
the last howl had been turned off, the noble guest
proceeded to produce proof of Iris official association
with Dowuing-street by referring to “this great
colony.” It he hadn’t looked like a British Minister
that would have made even a Melbourne detective
aware of his identity. Then, referring to the ques-
tion of Freetrade or Protection, he swatted the
admiring crowd in the fifth rib again by saying, quite
as if it were dead easy, he did not agree that the
manners and customs of Australia were the same as
those of England. He thought there was a little
difference in the customs. [Here the reader is
invited to cast his sparkling eye back to the gag
about the effects of an Australian-made dinnali for
what followed. The shrieks were deafening.] After
that, came Australia’s Minister for Customs, Groom,
who nailed himself to the British flag and .fluttered
in the applause. Ex-Premier Jambs McGowen
arose, wept on the shoulder of the dear, ole mo-tli-er
la-nd, and gave it as his imperishable opinion that
to the grand ole fa-th-er la-nd we owed everything
(not a dashed word about how much we owe Uncle).
Then one Greenwood, M.P., became vertical, and
made a popular hit (as the vaudeville advts. say)
by disagreeing with any suggestion that Australians
should think of Australia first. No! It should be
the Empire first and Australia second (great
applause, in which fully two waiters, in addition to
myself, did not join). We three had been so fed up
with fancy guff that we didn’t care a tinker’s palace
for anything but plain Australian any more that
night. So we unanimously went next door and
encompassed a plain Australian beer. It was the
best part of the Dinnah !
Concerning Wills and Bills.
From one of the crocodiles:—
The Vic. State Government is about to consider
a Bill introduced by Macke \, concerning the Con-
struction of Last Wills and Testaments. The pro-
posed law is based in common sense as opposed to
hidebound precedent, and deserves a smooth passage.
It will enable the Courts to give effect to a testa-
tor’s intention (if expressed clearly enough for
ordinary apprehension), instead of compelling them
to adhere to a rigid interpretation of teennical words
and phrases which have been used by the unwary
deceased (or his wretched solicitor) in unblissful
ignorance of their strict meaning in law. To exem-
plify : At present, if a person plainly expresses his
intention to devise property to (.say) Jones during
Jones’s life only, and then in a subsequent clause in
the will refers to such devise as being to Jones “and
his heirs,” the mere fact that he has used the
technical word “heirs” —although in obvious error as
to its legal significance—will result in the aston-
ished Jones obtaining an absolute estate “in fee
simple.” That is just one out of hundreds of instances
of the sort of farce Mackey is asking his co-legisla-
tors to put an end to. Practically the only people
who have known anything about such follies are the
horsehaired crocodiles, and hitherto they have not
thought it worth while to offer any very strenuous
objections, as their pockets have been filling through
the enormous amount of consequent litigation.
Wherefore, God speed the present tardy reform move-
ment.
And, while they are on the job, could not
Mackey and others strike out on another and no
less important tack? What is the objection (from
a national, as opposed to a horsetail standpoint) to
making it compulsory to file all wills before death?
Look at a few of the advantages:—
(1) About three-fourths of the thousand and one
species of fraud which are at present incidental to
the making and proving of wills would be done away
with. The instances of fraud (usually in pretty big-
estates) that have come before the Courts are appal-
ling, and the number of cases compromised or aban-
doned because the parties have not had the where-
withal to satisfy Costs, Esq., can best be left to the
imagination. Partly because dead men tell no tales,
tinkering with wills is one of the easiest crimes to
perpetrate and the most difficult to detect. It is
true that even now a person may file his will,
but very few people outside Gent. One are aware
of this fact, and Gent. One hardly ever sees tit to
exercise the power. Admittedly, the mere fact of
filing would have little direct effect on that branch
of fraud which is known in legal circles as Undue
Influence, but it would prevent disappointed benefici-
aries and others from indulging in interlineations,
obliterations, destructions and similar little amuse-
ments. That in itself is no small item.
(2) The testator’s intention is often utterly de-
feated in consequence of an unwitting non-compliance
with some technicality connected with (say) the
execution of attestation of his last farewell. Such
difficulties would be obviated by the Registrar seeing
that formalities were complied with. This would be
a boon to a man who had hired an erring lawyer, or
had chosen to dispense with professional services
altogether.
(3) If frauds and technical omissions were to the
above extent swept awav, there would be a vast
saving in the matter of Costs. It is indisputable
that wills have been bv far the most fertile source
of litigation, and it is equally indisputable that
solicitors do not as a rule spare expense where there
is money in an estate.
Naturally enough, people regard their final dis-
positions as more or less private, but they need have
no fear that the act of filing (or registering, if you
like the name better) would occasion any unwanted
publicity. Unlike (say) a registered mortgage,
which must be brought to light with every successive
transaction, the innards of a filed will * would not
be known until after the testator’s death, when the
usual search could be made on the usual conditions.
The only outsider who would be aware of the con-
tents would be a disinterested public official, and
appropriate penalties could be fixed for transgressions
on his part.
Probablv it will be urged against compulsory
filing that a dving man might have just sufficient
strength to sign the document, but not quite enough
to prolong h ; s last gasp till such time as the will
had been filed. It would be hard, it might be said,
if the objects of his bounty were told that the will
was a nullity simply on that account. But the
snb’tarv lesson would soon h" learnt that the best
policy was to get in early. Consider the analogv of
person who Raves it till too late to take out a
life insurance policy. Where’s the hardship?

and, one way and another, Holman’s Bill is suspected
of being more or less bait for the general elections.
Perhaps it is. That sort of thing has happened
once or twice before. Meanwhile, has anybody seen
a balance-sheet of Wade’s railway servants’ super-
annuation fund? It was a prize jest in the way of
reckless promise and impossible performance.
