Bert Bailey conducted a Band of Hope
meeting at the Palace (Sydney) on Satur-
day night, when a Great Moral Lesson, en-
titled “The Ninety and Nine,” was Sankey
and Moodied amidst the tearful approbation
of the brethren and sistren. As a Moral
Lesson the piece is as simple as it is Great.
Tom Silverton is a Fine, Manly Guy or
Bloke (it is an American story localised),
who falls in love with a rag and bone and
r bank of hair, and reckons that she is be-
yond compare, a Kiplingistic vampire fair,
who cleans him out and picks him bare,
and poisons . his heart with dank despair,
then gives him the frigid, glassy stare and
does a bunk with a millionaire, or some-
thing like that. But before she lets up on
Thomas she urges him to murder a parish-
ioner who has annoyed her. Thomas does
his best, and suffers all the remorse of the
"•uilty, although, as it turns out about 10.30
p.m., he hasn’t killed the parishioner at all.
Anyhow. Thomas gets up against Things in
General and fades to a secluded village
where he takes to the Cursed Der-rink. And
he makes no Error about it. The village peach
is so horrified that she decides to Save him.
She waits outside the pub for him, pleads
with him and croons Chapzander buck-up
hymns Where thev are calculated to do the
greatest quantity of good. No dividends.
She whispers a portion of the Lord’s Prayer
into his disreputable ear, and the Wretch
'•enlies with a whisky breath that can be
distinctly heard in the back stalls. She
ofifprs him Water to cool his internal fires
and he says it is the worst-looking drink
he has ever seen. Ned Duggan, who is
masquerading as her father, calls her Low,
and threatens to wallop her unless she gives
no, trying to save the ruin, but she says
she’ll bring the animal into the Stockyard
or • forfeit the gate-money. Finally, she
offers herself to the Outcast as a sacrifice
to , Spiritual Duty, and the staggered Out-
cast skies his thirst. He immediately cele-
brates the occasion by heroicallv driving a
railway-engine through a bushfire inferno
to save the cut-off people of the village that
formerly held their noses when he reeled
n ast. After that they know he has amt
Salvation, and they wrestle mightily with
the Lord for his desnaired-of life. When
bq corrms round, he finds himself safe within
the fold alongside the ninety and nine, and
there isn’t a jimjam on the horizon of the
future.. What trouble there is comes the
way of the Hypocritical Villain; which is
natural and nroper. Everybody is glad to
know that there isn’t enough love and
Christian L'ndness about to allow of any
being wasted on that base hound.
“The Ninety and Nine” provides Guy
Hastings with the first opnortunit.v since he
was imported by Bert Bailey and Co. to
show his true skill as an actor, and he
fastens to it as a good pro. starving for a
chance generally does. Few, if any. better por-
trayals of a man. drunk and down and out.
have been seen in Sydney. It is Hastings’
play entirely. The other main feature is
the dash of a railway-engine through a
seething bush fire, and as a stage sensation
it ought to attract every follower of the
drama.
John McCormack, good showman as well
as good artist, hit Sydney so hard with his
three punches—his Hibernian punch, his
popular punch and his operatic punch—that
he only dragged himself from the ring as
the train-guard’s whistle was sounding. The
earlier concerts were in the Town Hall ; an
extra extra was crowded in on Monday
afternoon at the Adelphi Theatre. And as
Sydney’s demand for John’s well-mixed
punch hadn’t even then been anything like
exhausted, the Taits, in a fine spirit of
resignation, said they supposed it must be
done, and booked their sweet acrobatic tenor
for four xxxtra Sydney concerts in October.
Polite vaudeville invaded the Little
Theatre (Sidney) last Saturday; and
tnough there were dull moiiit...s in the pro-
gramme presented by “The Butterflies,” the
entertainment was refreshingly novel and
of a high musical quality. Judging by the
hilarity of the audience and the surplusage
of encores, the new organisation is in for a
big success. Miss Cecilia Gold, a charming
comedienne, in her realistic “Cat Court-
ship,” with Mr. Waller, succeeds in miauw-
ing so musically that this critic is consider-
ing the question of dismissing the cat that
at present pervades his backyard and offer-
ing her the job. Also, she makes love so
seductively at certain occupants of the front
stalls that the manager might contemplate
the advisability of raising the price of these
seats. The usual balladists may be passed
over, without objurgations, however, since
such accidents happen even in the best-regu-
lated variety programmes. In Miss Marion
Armitage the company possess a comedienne
with a broad sense of humor and a voice of
12-inc-h calibre. Mr. Waller is a burlesque
actor and singer whose slight tendency to
clown does not disguise his real cleverness ;
and Mr. Watson shows himself as a com-
edian of slighter tonnage yet of consider-
able ability. But it is in the high musical
quality of the entertainment that “The
Butterflies” distinguish themselves from
similar organisations. In the pianist, Mr.
Dennett (who played Lizst’s “Rhapsodie
Honerroise” with brilliance and fervor), Mr.
Waller (violin) and Mr. Watson (’cello),
the company possesses a trio of musicians
worthy of any concert-platform. Their
burlesque of a Hungarian Orchestra was an
astonishingly ludicrous and yet artistic
musical turn. It could easily be the star
item on any variety programme. The bur-
lesque melodrama furnished sufficient excuse
for the existence of melodrama ; for if there
wasn’t any melodrama, such a burlesque
could not have happened. And that would
have been a distinct loss to the world, the
community, the population, or whatever you
may call it.
Arcady will be removed from Sydney
Criterion this week to the Royal, to allow
“Within the Law” to introduce itself at
the former establishment Saturday.
The new Sombra, Miss Hilda Vining, con-
tinues to gain friends for her quiet, artistic
performance, and her well-trained voice.
The Sydney Repertory Dramatic Society
will make its first appearance, on
October 2, at the Repertory Theatre,
Grosvenor-street, which has the dis-
tinction of being the handiest theatre
to The Bulletin oflice. Shaw’s “The
Devil’s Disciple” will be played for a season
of six days, by a cast predominantly pro-
fessional. This play Shaw terms a melo-
drama, and modestly mentions that it con-
tains the finest dramatic situation in modern
drama. Anyhow, it has got a gallows; but
Shaw does not hang a single dramatic critic
thereon. The play is a costume one, and the
costumes have been designed by Souter. The
scene is set in America at the time when
General Burgoyne aftd his British troops
were busily occupied in losing that portion
of British territory, amid the great smash
and surrender of Saratoga. The leading
part of Judith will be taken by Miss Aggie
Thorne (Mrs. Deery), whose reappearance
on the stage is a noteworthy event. Though
subscribers will have priority of booking, the
public will have the Opportunity to book all
other seats, on any night of the season.
The way of Bung gets no easier—in fact,
the ways of Sydney are bad for anybody’s
feet, but the feet of Fillemup are specially
tender. His latest cause for grief is the
doing away with the Tivoli interval. Still
our friend Blewrewin is the only one who
is hurt about it. And, in any case, a patron
who badly needs lubricating can miss one or
two turns —there are always one or two
worth missing. The latest item that is not
worth overlooking is that of the Eight
Saxones, purveyors of rag-time noises and
dances; and they also cut out all tedious
waits by doing their clobber-transformations
on the stage. Other turns that shouldn’t be
sacrificed to Beer are Tiebor’s Seals. Willette
Whitaker, Frank Parish and Williams and
Rankin.

“The Pit and the Pendulum,” a condensed
version of Edgar Allan Poe’s yarn of the
good old Inquisition days, is the latest in
Sydney picture thrills, but, beyond being
stocked above the average with horrors, it
is nothing out of the way. A brighter enter-
tainment is provided at the Crystal Palace
by Max Linder in “The Last Laugh,” a
lengthy but very cheerful comedy. A supple-
ment to the Topical Gazette. “The Aus-
tralian Navy at Portsmouth.” supplies a
good idea of what our own land is getting
for its Defence money.
By the time The Bulletin takes its
weekly walk abroad Ben Beno, the aerialist
at the National Amphitheatre, will have
concluded his season, and the “Chair of
Death” act will be no more. Also, there
will have departed from the Brennan-Fuller
co.’s Sydney house Marcell and Bell and
George Rowley; while the Dentines. Mac
and Maree, Karno and Francis, and Carl
Merlyn are under the identical orders that
the John Hop vave to Poor Joe. On the
word of the management the vacancies will
be filled with distinction, refinement, etc.
The etc. will include rag-time ; no etc. would
be complete without it.
West’s big summer season at the Sydney
Glaciarium will commence on Saturday
nieht. and the honorable occasion will be
marked by the production of “The Battle of
Waterloo,” one of the most ambitious at-
tempts to date in the art of cinematography.
Two thousand soldiers, 1000 cavalry arid
gun horses and 50 cannon were employed in
preparing the 116 scenes of Bill Adams’s
great victory.
“ THE ARCADIANS," AT THE SYDNEY
CRITERION.
Simplicitas (W. S. Percy) and Peter Doody
(Robert Whyle , jnr.), two simple Arcadians.
Three weeks ago there were six cases of
small-pox in Sydney, and the dailies howled
about the matter over columns of valuable
soace. As The Bulletin goes to press
this week 12 cases are reported, and the
fact is mentioned by the papers in the
merest whisper. Analogously, when Genee
first danced in Sydney these same sheets
went absolutely mad about her. Yet to
those who knew her she was a disappoint-
ment. She had strained one of her legs,
and was “going short,” as the horse-coper
hath it. At the time of writing she is at
her best. And in “La Camargo,” the fam-
ous ballet which was staged at Her Maj-
esty’s for the first time in these parts
on Saturday night, she gets a unique oppor-
tunity to show her extraordinary skill.
However, the shouting and the tumult are
stilled to all intents and purposes. The
press-agent, his eyes bright with mercenary
veneration, has apparently moved on to
another show. It is enough to make any
man with a fragment of sense of propor-
tion about his person turn misanthrope.
The unsatisfactory feature of the “Cam-
argo” ballet is the king. A more insen-
sately wooden admirer has seldom been
presented on any stage—and in the role of
lover Louis XV. was a notable enthusiast,
unless all the memoir-writers are shameless
liars. An interesting example of intelligent
anticipation, or something of the sort, is
provided by the mixing up of Louis Seize
furniture with the furniture of the period
in La Camargo’s boudoir. When the writer
discussed this play last week he hadn’t seen
the Hunting Ballet. All is as he suspected.
The Meet in an environment of mid-summer
roses; the misplaced spurs; the outrageous
drag-hounds; the bluff hunting men in red
flannel yachting caps—all the traditional im-
becilities are there. Having delivered these
few carps, the writer hastens to mention
that, in other respects, the show is beautiful
and interesting.

“Rutherford and Son,” the latest venture
of Adelaide Literary Theatre, is a first-plav
by Githa Soweiby, and introduces about
the most consistently unpleasant family vet
seen on any stage. Old Rutherford is “never
questioned, never answered back ; like Rod ”
The daughter and family drudge has at
i*U gone astray with Martin, an old and
trusted woi-Kinan, driven to “sin” by sheer
mouotony. Both of them, seemingly, are too
scared to suggest matrimony. The feeble
and querulous son has a wife, Mary, allowed
in the house solely because she has produced
a Rutherford heir, but herself treated as a
stranger. Now, this John, though a fool
has somehow stumbled on a discovery which
will put the declining glass-works, “Ruther-
ford’s,” the family fetich, on its legs again-
and, being pressed to explain it, he asks his
price. That should, presumably, be a part-
nership. Yet, though the old man has no
aim in life except to keep the business going
and hand it on to his son. he incomprehen-
sibly bullies Martin into divulging the trade
secret —and “sacks” him! John then bolts
with the cash-box, as a slight payment on
account; and the curtain falls on Mary
driving a bargain—a home for herself and
the infant, who shall in due course be sacri-
ficed to the Moloch of “Rutherford’s.” The
Literary players include three or four who
could probably get a professional engagement
at any moment. Wilfrid Neill was sound
and striking as the rugged Northcountry-
man, Douglas Walsh as the employee, and
Mrs. Geoffrey Burgoyne (a new and strong
recruit) as Mary, though hardly the “com-
mon little workgirl” that the author had in
view. Miss Robin was as artistic as ever
in the character of the lovestruck spinster,
and Mrs. Bastard brought down the house
in a too-brief appearance as a dram-drinking
gossip.

Adelaide is settling down to its new
arrangement of theatres. The planting of
the new and sumptuous Tivoli, just opposite
the Empire, gives the Central Market dis-
trict a fresh prominence. Now Mr. “Snowy”
Baker announces that he is going to rush
up a new Stadium right alongside, with
which to galvanise Adelaide’s spasmodic
boxing-interest into real life. The new
Wondergraph, opoosite the Royal, shows pic-
tures all day. So does a new Star enter-
prise, in the old Tivoli house; and so, of
course, does, the Rundle-street Pavilion,
which had till now a monopoly of that
branch. It is too early yet to say whether
all these filmers can find patronage. Agri-
cultural Show week has supplied a public
for all of them, and West’s and
Wondergraph find their most popular
pictures at night are the snaps showing
Dad and the whole Wayback family, as
seen on the unprecedentedly crowded
showground during the day. The Roval has
taken a jump from pantomime to melodrama,
and the long succession of Williamsonian at-
tractions stops for a bit: which means the
moving-on of manager Hazlitt. who seemed
to have become an Adelaide citizen. The
Marlow Co. —shortly to become a Willoughby
Co., it seems—starts with a “Beggar Girl’s
Wedding.” The King’s relies on TTuxham’s
tuneful Serenaders for its First Part, and
some new-come Japs, said to be specially
clever, are on the doorstep, so to speak.
At Melbourne Princess’s Allen Doone cast
oft the glad rags of romantic circumstance
and got himself up as an ancient light of
the R.C. Church, administering spiritual
comfort and Scripture quotations to the
scattered population of “a small town in
America.” In this unknown district the
shamrock gets equal cultivation with the
cabbage, and all the villagers are gently
brow 7 beaten by Father Doone into graciously
tolerating the shortcomings of their neigh-
bors. In such surroundings, where the
only excitements are the rent and the
weather, nothing ought to happen—and
nothing really does happen but the matri-
monial venture of Dr. Edward W T alsh. who,
mounting Vaulting Ambition for a win or a
place, conies a frightful cropper at the last
fence. Edward, it seems, wants to resist
the claims of the swmtest colleen in all the
wurruld and marry a lady embarrassed with
cash, but his Reverence neatly baulks the
project and persuades Ned that the colleen
is the prize for him. This is practically the
whole story of a play wherein Misther
Doone does nothing more than play the
rble of a peregrinating Father causing souls
to bloom like lilies undefiled, the while he
drops soothing homilies into every ear. His
vaunted “performance” is much in the
nature of an artless elocutionary recital
carried on in a minor key. Save Clive
Farnham, who is better than the bulk, all
the others are unimportant portrayers of
characters endowed with personalities of
milk and arrowroot, and apparently so polite
that thev couldn’t think of dving without
an apology. The scenery is only middling.

Margaret Dale Owen and a number of
other people, gave a riotous answer to the
inquiry, “Whose Baby Are You?” at Mel-

George Wirth just now is contemplating
the approach of Melbourne Show week with
the air of a man who would rather turn
noodle away than be prosecuted for over-
crowding. The Circus makes a special fea-
ture of the Dantes who Defy Death by biking
round a suspended saucer track, in the centre
of a large unstable cage, whilst two or three
anxious lions are barking below r . Which
seems a safe defiance to offer to animals that
evidently expect the saucer track to fall on
them, and are very annoyed wilh the tamer
who prevents their escape. Anyhow, the
writer suffers no terrors on the Dantes’ ac-
count. The only thing that thrills him at
the circus is the smile of the beauteous
Spanish girl, who stands on the swinging
slack wire with the easy assurance of an
angel seated on a cloud.

Of the seven Quinlan operas that kept the
money rolling into Her Majesty’s (Melb.)
last week, five were repeats, the “Faust”
revival being noteworthy for its change of
prima donna and tenor. The occasional
Felice Lyne appeared as a suitably young
and exceptionally petite Marguerite, whilst
Spencer Thomas was a pleasing representa-
tive of the duped old man who sold his soul
on her account, and got only a short term
of rejuvenation for his foolishness. Miss
Lyne sang always brightly, though some-
times rather nasally, in her Marguerite
effort. She looked an adorable little : "l.
but her gracefulness was somewhat wanting
in simplicity, and she showed less natural
feeling for her part than when she was
Gilda in “Rigoletto” a fortnight previously.
“Madame Butterfly,” on the Wednesday
night, stood the test of comparison with Mel-
bourne’s fond recollections of other produc-
tions of the Puccinian tale of love, even as
“Boh&me” had stood it —or even better. The
Quinlan production, many people will re-
member, leaves the Williamson setting of the
first act. to be desired. Butterfly’s entrance
with her wedding party, across a bridge in
the background, was more effective than the
Quinlan way of bringing her along. In all
other respects the revival is satisfying.
Jeanne Brola, the versatile soprano-of-all-
work, does well in the name part; Parker
makes an admirable American Consul: the
Suzuki of Mabel Dennis is distinctively
effective; Sydney Russell, Gladys Ancrum :
the men in minor parts make the most of
their opportunities ; and Maurice D’Oisly. as
Pinkerton, puts some emotional force into
his warble. Moreover, he almost succeeds
in being a debonair lieutenant in a white
linen suit.
It was an off-week for Perceval Allen, the
plump Wagnerian prima donna, and for
Francis Costa, the bnlkv tenor, will sings
English with difficulty. They are not wanted
in “The Rhinevold,” the first of the operas
in the revived “Ring” series, which came to
pass last Friday, before the least numerous
audience of the season, and thus falsified
the allegation that General Desire had been
nining for another “Rinv” cycle. Those
long-rested artists were hilled for two bouts
of strenuous exertion this week, however.
Again, on Monday, they will appear in “The
Twilivht of the Gods.” the drama that
records the decease of Wotan behind the
scenes, and shocks Siegfried’s widow by
reouiring her late husband’s corpse to raise
its rigid arm. Between the last two chap-
ters of the mystical Ringmarole a one and
?, D - performance of “Carmen” will happen
this Saturday (20th).
Quinlan attraction last Saturday was
■ Manon Lescaut,” for the first time in Eng-
vr iv° a st uge, and for the only time in
Melbourne this season. The opera was
produced as carefully as “Louise”
(which had two performances), though
the elaborate dressing of the chorus
in the second act was more gaudy
than neat. Some of the choristers,
and even Maurice D’Oisly, as the hero,
looked as though they had fallen into their
wigs and garments by accident rather than
design. “Manon Lescaut” adds no laurels
to Puccini’s wreath. The music, richly
melodious in the second act, seems mostly
out of touch with the story, and the story
is a Bland Holt drama perversion of WAbbt!
I’revost’s naughty but tedious old novel —
Manon as the artless, amorous maiden elop-
ing with her impecunious Chevalier; Manon
living luxuriously with an elderly gent., and
wanting to elope some more; Manon singing
like a bird in a convenient prison on the
ground floor, where her lover can kiss and
play handies with her between the bars of
the cage; and the escaped Manon wandering
with her lover on the theatrical rocks, and
dying dismally. The last act is short and
weak, the previous three acts short and
lively. Brola took the honors for her diver-
sified creation of the gentle minx; D’Oisly
labored, under a deplorable outfit; and the
emphatic W. J. Samuel, as Manon’s brother,
asserted himself undulv, as did the orchestra
when the individual voices were doing their
loudest.
Two more nights of “Arrah-Na-Pogue” will
bring Maggie Moore to a conclusion at Mel-
bourne King’s. The circumference of Maggie
in the disguise of Arrah is one of those
anomalies which Melbourne accepts for
friendship’s sake, and her modern rendering
of “The Wearin’ of the Green” is infused
with the sentiment of “Auld Lang Syne.”
Roberts, Max Clifton. Brown, Beckett, John
Forde and Miss Wilton are on the commit-
tee of this time-honored institution. Next
Saturday (20th) Euvenie Duggan and co.,
with Roberts included in the co., will appear
in “The Confession,” whereof the manage-
ment remarks that it is up to date. Also,
it has the blessing of the clergy, whose up-
to-dateness is usually more honored in the
speech than in the observance, as Hamlet
would suggest.

Hunter.” Meanwhile “Fanny’s First Play”

ture to public notice.

The Golden Troupe of Russian Dancers
are the latest arrivals at Melbourne Opera
House. Both sexes are about equally repre-
sented in this numerous party, and their glad
raiment makes a favorable impression when
they open the proceedings with a chant.
Subsequently their clothes are even gladder,
and their twanging of Russian equivalents
for the mandolin excites favorable comment.
After which they dance and spin in a
national manner —singly or in pairs, or all
together —until the house, in a manner of
speaking, is brought down about their ears.
Then they smile breathlessly and retire.
Twenty minutes of the Golden Troupe are a
great assistance to the bill. The other
principal ingredients are the Hoboes, and
Diamond and Beatrice, and the De Bruns,
the drawing-room dancers, having strength-
ened their attraction by waltzing up and
down a staircase, as though it were of no
consequence.
At Melbourne Athenaeum Hall next
Saturday (20th), Gregan McMahon starts
his tenth Repertory season with Galsworthy’s
“Strife.” This is the Capital v. Labor play
that worries the Fatman in his arrogance
and the worker in his wrath. Galsworthy
stands with one leg in the Trades Hall and
the other in the office of the Employers’
Lnion, snorting at stupidity on both sides.
Strife” is to be repeated on Monday. On
Wednesday, Thursday and Friday the ques-
tion of man’s salvation (which is a burning
question if you miss it) will be discussed in
Shaw’s “Major Barbara.” The scene of the
heated conversation is mostly a Salv’army
Shelter, where aching souls are saved by
cymbals, and conscience is awakened by the
earnest exhortations of the drum.

At Melbourne Olympia Edison’s Kineto-
phone is now giving speech to the speechless
picture-show. The new invention is a great
advance on the chronomegaphone with
which Spencer formerly surprised his pat-
rons. This is the ham of the per-
formance. The usual silent pictures which
precede and follow the Kineto. recitals are
the bread. “The White Slave,” the yarn
of an octoroon, was first favorite on Satur-
day, but “A Gentleman’s Gentleman” was
loudly supported by the sixpenny rush.
“Bloore” : What price the air of resigna-
tion about this communication regarding a
vacant postmistress-ship:—
Dear Sir, —Not hearing from you with regard to
the position of postmistress at Doodlepumpkin Creek,
I beg to inform you that I have got married.—Yours
obediently, Melinda Gargoyle.
“Chink” writes: —
The little one-horse township of Dunolly (Vic.)
has no remarkable claims to Fame—other than the
possession of the tinsmith’s shop in which Agent-
General Peter Mcßride was born and the little slab
hut in which Mrs. Peter—the lady who was pre-
sented at Court the other day—began her acquaint-
ance with this tiresome old globe, it (the township,
I mean) has not so man}' goats and geese as many
another Victorian village; neither has it as many
mullock-heaps and jam tins as various mining ham-
lets, but there is one point on which Dunolly can
show the way. This is in the supreme suitability
of most of its citizens for their respective billets.
There is a carpenter and builder whose name is
Wood ; the leading baker and cake-maker is one
Raison; the blacksmith is Strike; a near-by pub
bears the announcement “H. Berandie, licensee” ;
and to finish matters off nicely the coroner is Berri-
man. All we require now to make the circle com-
plete is a grave-digger named Shovel and an under-
taker named Christian Berrial.
“Inver G.”: “Jack" Meehan, Labor
M.L.A., N.S.W., is finishing in character-
istic style what is probably his last Par-
liament. During the censure debate the
other night he, for long, silently surveyed
and considered the Opposition. Having
made up his mind he delivered judgment
thus : “Well, you are a lot of dashed mon-
grels.” When he was pulled up for that he
said: “All right, Mr. Speaker, they’re not
what I said, but—they ought to be.” The
night after it was announced that Wood,
deputy-leader of the Wade party, had been
refused nomination by one of the suburban
Liberal leagues, the shearers’ representative
oftered his consolations across the floor:
“Never mind. Willie.” he said, “we’ll form
a lost dog party. There’ll be you, Willie,
and me, Willie, and —(uproar)-—and you
can be Premier. Willie.” In the sleepy
hours of one of the recent all-night sittings
the silver-headed Scobie was snoring with*
a pronounced’ Scotch accent in one of the
easy chairs at the back of the chamber.
John, himself newly waked, became annoyed,
and made his way to the sleeper and landed
him on the ear a flat-handed wallop that
resounded through the building. Scobie
leaped 10ft. When he descended he found
Meehan awaiting him with the brief expla-
nation : “You shouldn’t snore so hard !”
AT POVERTY POINT.
After all, “Jimmy” Nunn isn't going to quit the
stage. He has only made up his mind that it’s up
to some younger man to take on the arduous job of
stage-managing the Anderson productions.
“T. 0.1.”: Jonathan Dewhurst, an actor who
appeared in Australia in 1881, lately died, at the
age of 76 years, at Chorley, in Lancashire, where he
had been keeping an hotel. Very few playgoers will
now recall him. Brewer, in his Music and Drama,
says: “On August 22, 1881, an ‘eminent tragedian,’
Mr. J. Dewhurst, placed his Hamlet before a Sydney
audience. It was good, but without any originality.
A fine presence and a clear voice were of great use
to him.” In his younger days Dewhurst was fre-
quently associated with Henry Irving (1864), then
playing juvenile lead in the provinces, and he made
his first success, a little later, at Drury Lane play-
ing with Phelps in “Ivanhoe.” He retired from the
stage in 1886 —27 years ago. So he didn't die in
harness except in the sense that he died in beer
harness.
“Melb”: Nunn’s benefit was the worthy recogni-
tion of a worthy man. He is absolutely the most
modest actor 1 ever heard of. For 14 years he has
cast the parts for Bilanderson, and has always cast
himself beneath his ability, as evinced by his good,
intense rendering of Shylock at his benefit. If
happened to be a bad part in a drama at the
King’s, Nunn would clothe himself with it and turn
it into a good one. Indeed, when the good actor is
feeling good, he exults in a bad part. I remember
Cathcart. who had plaved the whole lead with C.
Kean, casting himself for the few lines of the Gaol
Governor in “Youth.” No play abounds more in
bad parts than “Measure for Measure” ; yet three
of them made the fortunes of three actors. John
Emery, father of Sam and grandad of Winifred, both
known in Australia, made the half-dozen lines of
Barnardine stand out more than anything else in
the piece; Hoskins, as Lucio, hit harder than any-
thing else in it at Sadlers Wells; and Pompey, the
clown, gave the eminent Webster his first start, after
he had been onlv a member of the orchestra.
“Ike Pagliacci”: Melb. Royal Agricultural Society
has refused space for side-shows, other than merry-
go-rounds and swinging boats, at its next annual
gathering, and the whiskered visitor from Dog Flat
will be robbed of much excitement. Incidentally,
the Society will miss considerable revenue in ground
rents. Presumably, the idea is to prevent the
swindling of Dad by shows such as even the immor-
tal Barnmn would have shied at, and it must be
confessed Australia has a vast selection of attrac-
tions warranted to swindle. Instance, “an oyster
that smokes.” observed at AVagga Show t’other day,
and the Wild Man eating red meat, prosectrted for
false pretences recently, but still continuing his
career of cannibalism. It is now a difficult matter
for respectable showmen (such do exist) to per-
suade a country crowd that they are not unmitigated
frauds, and it is up to them to form an association
that will do what the country agricultural societies
won’t do, viz., clear the gambler, the imnossible
hoop-la game people, and the exhibitors of indecent
and offensive shows off the face of the grounds.
German municipalities, which make money out of
annual fairs (purelv amusement concerns), control
their carnivals admirably. No “swank” advertising
is permitted. A giant of 7ft. must not ascend to
Bft. in the advts.. nor must a gentleman of 6cwt.
expand to 7ewt. Illusions must distinctly s f ate that
they are such, and there are. in most towns, strict
regulations regarding the exhibition of children.
Announced that Miss Fortescue, the beauty-actress
of a generation ago, is about to publish a book of
reminiscences. The £lO,OOO she got for “breach"
from Lord Garmoyle (profanely known as Lord
Gumboil) was a record until Daisv Markham got
£50.000 from the Marquis of Northampton the
other day.
(Continued on page 2ft.)
The alleged holding-up of the Broken Hill
express by armed and masked brigands, who
got away with Ll4O or so, wasn’t the sensa-
tional thing of the newspaper headings. As
with the “Central” fraud at Sydney, the
principal Broken Hill station is fixed on
the outskirts of the town, whence the express
crawls up through a narrow, tree-lined
reserve into the centre of the village. There
wasn’t any holding-up of the train. The
supposed brigands appear to have just
stenped on to the sluggish caterpillar, and
collared the day’s takings from the wooden
central booking-office, and stepped off again
before the caterpillar had reached the prin-
cipal station, where it begins to be a more
or less real express.
CURSING THROUGH BRASS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Four trombones in unison played the curse motif
with impressive effect. —Grand opera criticism.
The flute is a weakling ; it chirps and trills
And laughs like a stream in the ferny hills.
The brown violin is the slave of sin ;
It sobs and whispers all women to win ;
It shrieks, and hurries, and fidgets and quakes;
When anything's doing it giggles and shakes;
It quivers with passion and shivers with fear,
And plays up at once with the heart and the ear ;
While big brother ’cello and Pa Double bass
Are groaning and grunting with rather less grace.
The clash of the cymbals, barbaric and loud,
With savagery startles the chattering crowd.
The summer-day buzz of the dreamy bassoon
Flows quietly in like the light of the moon ;
But the cornet comes shouting ahead of the brass.
(His clear trumpet note makes him first in his
class.)
But when someone’s curse must be stressed in the
score,
Clear the musical deck and bring forward the Four !
bet them snort, blast and roar
Like wild elephants penned in a strange forest trap !
How wild is their force! E’en the drum’s thunder-
clap
Cannot deafen and daze like the blare of the “slide"
When it’s let loose to curse with three friends by its
side!
When enraged, don’t use words, or in court you’ll
atone;
Blow your sentiments splendidly through a trombone !
N.B.W.

By “Henry Horsecollar” :
It isn’t every day that, in the middle of a big
city, some £70,000 worth of premises and contents
double up and fall with a crash. So when Beard,
Watson’s great Sydney furniture warehouse suddenly,
in the dead of night, put both hands on its stomach,
and said it wasn’t feeling well, and then dropped
in, burying the magnificent carved oak sideboard,
and the vase, and the eight-day clock in the ruins,
there was some astonishment. Still there will pro-
bably be more astonishment later on. The place
that has gone down isn’t a patch on the places that
are going down. During the last few years of boom,
Sydney has been crazy in the matter of reconstruc-
tion. The architect came along, and looked at the
old building, and certified that the foundations
would stand the weight of another Storey, or two or
even three more storeys. Then, presumably, he got
the job. If he didn’t certify—well, probably some-
one else did, and someone else got the job. Build-
ings had the innards torn out of them and new
bowels put in till there was nothing left of the
original work save an outline and a smell of onions
from the caretaker’s premises in the basement. It
wasn’t so much a case of rebuilding as of throwing
a whole pyramid on top of the original hatbox.
There are two structures in my mind’s eye which 1
regard as among the next to go—two tall concerns
which some cheerful person piled on the top of an
aged next-to-nothing, and let in haste to the fleeting
financial agent, and persons of that sort. It is a
sign of the breaking of the boom when the boom
buildings begin to sag.

From the Oldest Inhabitant: —The Wool-
pack, the much-paragraphed old pub which
is now being demolished at Canterbury
(N.S.W.), is not 80 years old, as alleged;
70 is nearer the mark. Canterbury is one
of the oldest Sydney suburbs as Sydney
suburbs go. There was a settlement there-
abouts away back in the 1790’5. Robt.
Campbell, among others had a grant there.
In 1036 the place commenced to have a
commercial value. Messrs. Edward Knox,
Child and Kemble had come to Sydney for
the purpose of starting a sugar refinery.
They fixed on Campbell’s grant on Cook’s
River as a likely spat for operations.
Through Samuel Lyons they purchased 60
acres, and on this site in 1841 they com-
pleted their building. It is now used as a
bacon-factory. “A.S.C., 1841” is or .but
lately was to be seen cut into the stone
upon the front. A township soon grew up
around the sugar works. One Murphy
built the first pub ; it was in Unwin-street
—named after Solicitor Unwin, who in-
vested a fortune in sugar. The next was
the Hardener's Arms. Samuel Miller kept
this; he came from the Blue Bell, on the
corner of Erskine and Sussex streets, still
standing, and now the Sydney YYharf Lab-
orers’ i Union Institute. In 1847 Thomas
Kelpey kept the Gardeners’ Arms under
the name of “The Yorkshireman’s Coat of
Arms” ; but after George Hill and Frederick
Clissold had established woolwashing on
Cook's River, the name of the ancient pub
was again changed, and became the Wool-
pack.
A PANKHURST ITEM FROM
ENGLAND.
PRESSMAN (to Womans Rights agitator):
“ And do you honestly believe that a woman
should get a man's wages? ”
AGITATOR (grimly humorous): "It depends
upon whether she's married to him or not."
Asquith’s friend, Robert Bridges, the
new Poet Laureate, has at least mentioned
Australia. Here is the reference, in the
new man's typically rocky verse —if it is
verse
Came from the swarming counties accoutering,
And misty highlands of Caledonia,
With Cambria’s half-Celtic offspring,
And the ever-merry fighting Irish ;
Came too the new world’s hardy Canadians,
And from remote Australia, champions
Like huntsmen, and from those twin islands
Lying off antipodal beyond her.
It reads like the last possibility in the way
of wooden or leather struggle.

“Red Tape Junior”: Two members of
the N.S.W. Public Service recently retired,
after long service, on a pension, as per regu-
lations, and each was granted a gratuity of
£5OO by Parliament. One officer was once
Comptroller-General of Prisons; the other
was a messenger in the Legislative Assem-
bly. The Comptroller got his little bit be-
cause for a few years he sat on N.S.W.
Public Service Board as a temporary hand';
the old messenger got his because members
of Parliament, who knew him well, felt like
giving it to him. It has. transpired that the
well-known Public Service rule which pro-
hibits a public servant drawing a pension
and a gratuity at the same time has been
applied to the messenger, but a hole has
been found which allowed the ex-Comptrol-
ler-General of Prisons to pull off both
pension and £5OO. The messenger can only
get his £5OO when his pension lapses— i.e.,
when he is dead. The other can enjoy his
while he is alive. I read a moral in this:
It is better to be a Comptroller-General
than a messenger.

“Kangarookaburra” writes this :
The authorities of the Sailors’ Home, Melbourne,
are puzzled as to what to do with a veteran who,
having been an inmate for over two years, has .just
been discovered to be a woman.
Jerry Philpott, as she is called, states that she
changed her sex 50 years ago, though by what man-
ner of means she managed to accomplish it she can-
not be induced to explain.
“I have been a sailor, rouseabout, Church of Eng-
land curate and chauffeur,” she remarked ; “and
though efficient in everything I tackled, my pay
throughout has been wretchedly inadequate.”
Strange that the mistress of such a craft as the
ability to change one’s sex should never have put it
before the world as a money-making scheme. Think
of the vast possibilities! Take, for instance, the
case of a wealthy squatter who has an only daughter.
Isn’t it quite obvious that a man in his position
would be prepared to pay the very highest figure in
order to satisfy his craving for an heir?
Then what about the English suffragettes? Votes
would be immediately in easy reach of all of them,
and, I suppose, where such a large body is con-
cerned, the translation of Mary into John William
could he effected at wholesale rates, a much cheaper
proceeding than in cases where only individuals are
concerned.
Indeed, the opportunities seem to be endless, but
especially perhaps in regard to the bachelor to whom
a daily interchangeability of sex would be the great-
est blessing.
In the early hours of the morning he could, as a
female, both cook and lay the breakfast, interview
the butcher and cut out shaving altogether. Then
he could put back the sex-button to masculinity in
the hall prior to running to the station, and, if the
train should prove to be crowded all he’d have to do
would be to “female himself” again, and some bald-
headed old lunatic with a white beard would be sure
to get up and give him his seat. Again, he could,
if he wanted to, “re-male” on arriving at the citv,
only to get thoroughly unmanned at four o’clock so
that he needn’t pay for his afternoon tea.
And then the Spring Catalogues with their pretty
pink and white covers, their pale ribbons and curious
gold letters.
What a problem will now present itself. Sale-
time! Oh, Sale-time! “Which will I buy? Pyja-
mas or nighties?”
Conjure up the cry on board of the burning shin
when everybody is preparing to leave it. “Women
and children first!!” How many men would reach
for their sex-buttons?
Not 0ne....1 don’t think.
Yes, Jerry Philpott has a fortune in front of her.'
According to a cable. George Reid had a
narrow escape from death on leaving Lon-
don for Australia. While he was posing
for a photographer on the railway plat-
worm, his train moved out, and a scramble
ensued. Several heavyweights propelled the
heavier weight into the carriage ; but then
it was found that the children had been left
on the platform. After much trouble the
train was stopped and the family was re-
united. It seems to have been a badly-
managed exit altogether—quite as bad as
the sensational exits Barton used to make.
Barton, when he left for Brisbane or Mel-
bourne or Adelaide, was usually dragged by
the hair into the guard's van as the train
moved out, while his cab-horse denounced
him bitterly for infuriated driving, and
swore by its nose-bag never to have any
further truck with him.

“Mr. .Tames” published this advt. one
day last week : —-
WANTED—A Competent AUSTRALIAN LADY
TYPISTE, to travel Europe and the East. Must he
educated, healthy, of smart appearance, thoroughly
good tempered, and bright, cheerful disposition.
Applicants are requested to supply full particu-
lars of experience, age, height, personal appearance,
religion, family, references and a recent photo-
graph (returnable), when an interview can be
arranged in Sydney upon advertiser’s arrival.
A native servant, first-class travelling and hotel
expenses and a complete outfit provided.
Address in confidence to , , India.
If the S. M. Herald ever heard of the
Indian nigger potentate’s penchant for a
white miss it probably sniffed when it re-
ceived that advt. But the ten shillings
worth went in all the same. Now, in case
there should be any replies to the call from
India, it is up to somebody in a uniform to
inquire who the advertiser really is and
what lie- wants the healthy, good-tempered,
smart, brisk typist for, and why he is so
confoundedly particular about her age and
personal appearance.
THE POINT OF VIEW.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
St. Paul found occasion to refer to the matter.
It wou'd appear that it was not the clothes they
put o> so much as what they left off that the
apostle found fault with, and so it was in the pre-
sent day. The fashionable attire of some women
was almost outrageous. No matter who the man
was, there was nothing he admired so much as a
modest woman. —Dean McCullagli, at Bendigo.
Upon the whole I do not mind
These fashions of the freakish kind;
For all I care the modes may freak
Morn, noon and night and all the week;
And some I like to see.
A woman in an X-ray frock
My finer feelings doesn’t shock;
A maiden in a clinging skirt
My sense of fitness fails to hurt—
With thoughts of tragedy.
In fact, I often do the Block
With eye alert for some quaint frock.
To see some other fellow’s girl
Caught up in Fashion’s giddy whirl,
Leave off a thing or two
Does not appear to me unfit.
I truly do not mind a bit
When she lays bare her shapely arms
And otherwise displays her charms
For men like me to view.
Dear me! Where is the harm, I ask?
Why should we take a maid to task
Because she sails along the street
Appearing ravishinglv sweet
In sheath-skirt and a ring?
I vow she looks a perfect pearl—
Provided she is not my girl.
I’ll not have any girl of mine
Adopting fashions in that line!
That’s quite another thing.
There’s quite enough of this work done
By others; and my fancy one
Should surely dress, it seems to me,
W'ith some small show of modesty.
Nor argument nor pelf
Could e’er remove my stern ukase
That to no idler’s vulgar gaze
Shall she reveal an ankle trim.
These charms of hers are not for him ;
I want them all myself.
Men like a modest woman. Yes
Here is a truth, I must confess.
I trow, if she belong to me
I’d have her dote on modesty,
And be a very nun
To other men, but, all the same,
1 offer no excuses lame;
I own to flat hypocrisy ;
When others freak I cannot see
That any harm is done.
And when I toddle down the street
I love some shapely maid to meet
Arrayed in clothes that, on the whole,
Imperil one’s immortal soul.
As pious men have shown.
It’s very wicked, I suppose,
But when a girl leaves off some clothes
I have to own she beats the band ;
Provided, you will understand,
That she is not my own.
VICTORIA.

“Vic”: The feature of Australia which s 0
fn- has interested the British Parliamentary
visitors the most is the pathetic old
Speaker’s Chair, preserved at the National
Parliament House in Melbourne. It was
presented, with due ceremonial, bv Governor
Manners-Sutton in 1873. and there were
even tears shed on the occasion. His father.
Speaker Manners-Sutton, sat in this con-
traption in the first Reformed (British)
Parliament in 1833-4. Parliament House
was burnt, but people rushed in where
devils feared to tread even with their cast-
iron hoofs. and dragged the ancient bit of
furniture out of Gehenna’s bosom. It is
nov kept in the Melbourne Parliamentary
Library, being too big and heavy to be em-
bezzled and taken bv intoxicated members
to a House of Flossie, as allegedly happened
to the poor old Mace. Every visitor insists
on sitting in it, and consequently it has had
to be re-covered twice. Therefore it is not
exactly the same thing, but the old wood-
work gives the right kind of groan when a
20st. Toorakian dame plumps down. En-

is an Impostor, claiming to be the very
same chair, in a Freemasons’ Lodge, at
Sunderland : but ours is the real Maginnis,
or the Parliamentary Commission wouldn’t

* * * *
Nobody in the community gets less svm-
pathy when he is alive or is less inquired
about when he is dead than that soft-footed
jackal or financial agent of the parks who
is called a “tout.” Presumably he makes
at times something more substantial than
abuse and personal injury out of his “call-
ing. ’ else he would hardly follow it. yet it
is an intensely precarious job. When Bill
is discussing with Lizzie the prospects of
getting married and acquiring a cottage and
some time-pavment furniture, the reckless
apparition snuffles up out of the darkness
and demands the deposit that was being
saved up for the tables and chairs and lino-
leum as the price of that insanitary thing
he calls his “silence.” He has immense
quantities of silence about him. and if ; s
all for sale. Then if he has selected his Bill
wronglv he is either clubbed or shot. By

a, number of Bills go armed to the evening

from Sydney and the amount of lack of
interest taken in his case is the same as
usual.

the Government. Under

would not have been con-

time the Governor cried, “Oh, let the beggar
off.” And let off he was —a fair sample of
the caprice which formerly governed the

“Hamer” supplies a few statistics:—
There are 49 youths and 35 girls under 16 years
drawing- money under the Fisher’s Government’s
Invalid Pensions Act. Far the most interesting of
them (in fact, in his class he may be said to be
unique) is the permanent invalid wtat. 16, who is
also a “married man.” The most ancient of the
old age pensioners is 90. By the way, one Aus-
tralian mother who got off the maternity allowance
mark with extraordinary punctuality by weighing in
with an infant on October 10, 1912 (the day on
which Fisher’s Act came into force) earned another
fiver on August 24 last. Strenuous work, forsooth !

THE OTHER ONE: “Meat's the hurry ,

The sturdy backbone of this great country
is also about its sturdiest cadger. The
above somewhat trite reflection is prompted
chiefly by the recent case of Hans Irvine,
M.P., who was set upon by the Maryborough
(Vic.) P. and A. Society, and asked for a
donation. He demurred. Whereupon the
free and independent toilers reminded him
of the horses, vehicles and things they had
lent him in order to secure his election, and
reiterated their clamor for cash. Irvine re-
plied that he had imagined, in his ignorance,
that what they had done was in the cause,
not of boodle so much as of Liberalism.
Also, he pointed out that he could not legally
give anything during three months before or
after an election —a circumstance which was
liable to happen at any moment almost. At
the time of writing the self-respecting agri-
culturists had not thought of a new move in
the game of cash-extorting. But they will.
They have an absolute gift in such things.
The dairy inspector in the N.S.W. North
' Coast cowland gets poor encouragement in
his laudable task of converting Cocky to
habits of cleanliness. Owing to the iarge
area which he has to cover, he is able to
visit most localities but once a year. To
make matters worse, his coming is heralded
by a system of bush telegraphy, which isn’t
far behind wireless in effectiveness; and
when something goes wrong and an insani-
tary milk-squeezer is caught, this is the
brand of encouragement the inspector re-
ceives from the Bench :
At the police court, Casino, M. J. Seary was
charged under the Pure Food Act with having a
dirty dairy. The case for the prosecution was that
the bail floor v/as covered with manure, and fowls
roosted in the bails. There were heaps of manure in
the cowyard. The milkroom floor was dirty, and the
vat stand was covered with stale and mouldy curd.
The utensils were only partially cleaned. Close to
the milk room was a kerosene tin containing the
viscera of a calf, being boiled for pig feed. A cream
can contained a rope halter, and holes in two cream
cans were plugged with sour-smelling rag. The
accused pleaded guilty, and the Bench fined him £l.
Having been rescued from the manure of the
milk-room, and given a swill in the partly-
cleaned vessels, and permitted to stand
awhile alongside the bubbling, steaming
calves’ innards, the milk is separated and
dropped into cream-cans plugged with sour-
smelling rags. Then, being made into butter,
it is shipped to Europe, whereupon Europe
turns up its nose and buys margarine, made
of closely-inspected materials in closely-in-
spected factories. So the margarine trade
booms as few others have boomed in modern
times, and the butter trade is seriously
threatened, and the cow cocky yells to the
State to “do something.” And when the
State proceeds to do the very best thing it
can do, and hands the job over to some fool
of a magistrate, he finishes it in the absurd
manner above-mentioned.
A tale of Hob
Cunning:—
Those simple inno-
cents, the Liberal
leaguers, especially
the Women’s National
Leagues, might learn a
few points from the
Never-Toueh-Its. The
other day the Wear.
State Government took
an alleged local option
poll as to whether an
hotel license should be
granted to the bay-
side suburb of Chel-
sea. The law says
that the vote must be
taken over the whole
licensing district—
which in this case in-
cludes far-away places
like Dandenong, Chel-
tenham, etc. and
that at least, one-third
of the electors must
vote. The far-away
places naturally didn't
care a shandy whether
Chelsea got a pub or
not; if they had any
interest in the matter
they would rather
keep it publess, so it
was left to Chelsea,
Carrum and Aspendale
to roll up and decide
the issue. The Recha-
bites, having wrestled
valorouslv in prayer
for a space, first pla-
carded the district
with the device,
“Don’t Vote” ; then
they passed round
quiet hints that all
who did vote would
be assumed to be fav-
orable to a license
being granted, and
would be bovcotted
as far as possible bv
them ; and they wound
up by picketing the
oolling-booths, note-
books in hand, and
significantly record-
ing the names of all
those who voted.
Which explains why,
of 800 persons who
signed the petition asking for the.poll, less than 50
per cent, exercised the franchise. If these sainted
tricks are persisted in, the situation will have to
be met by an amendment of the Licensing Act, mak-
ing voting compulsory. It is not Local Option, but
No Ontion,. and No Option of that sort means No
License, which in a populous district merely means
Sly Grog.

“C.E.”: The substitution of oil for coal
in the British Navy is more important than
was that of steam for sail. While Britain’s
advantage through coal is palpable, oil places
it on only an equal footing with most rivals,
and inferior to some. Five of the greatest
battleships being built in England will use
oil only, and the new cruisers are to be
oil-ers. Five other battleships, forming
this year’s programme, are to burn coal,
with oil as auxiliary. The reason is that
oil is only required for ships of exceptional
speed and quality. The oil ships will thus
CONSOLATION.
TENANT: "The house is cornin' down."
LANDLORD: “I don't know so much about
that. But I'll tell you what IDO know.
The rent is going up!”
form a division by themselves. Yet the oil
ship will become universal, partly because
it will be speedier, partly because oil takes
only one-third the space of the coal that
is necessary to do the same work, partly
because the stokehold staff will be much
reduced, and partly because a supply of oil
can be got-in much more quickly than one
of the other brand of fuel. As for the re-
duction in crews, mostly in the stokehold,
the Navy estimate puts the saving at about
one-third —most important in these days
when there is such trouble to man the
monsters.

[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Unless respectable people love sin, how is it nnc
sible that there is such a large outnut nf r»rl' ? OS '

cellor of St. Paul's Cathedral. London ° h

Ballad of Tomlinson
tthy need the clergy plead about
Respectability?
The kind of sins we read about
(The scarlet ones decreed about)
Are not for you and me.
We walk the way of narrowness,
Hut still it keeps us gay
To read of how a Baroness
Went giddily astray.
It interests devoted wives
To speculate and guess
Just how they would conduct their lives
Were they the Baroness.
Vicariously we play the game,
And lapse by proxy thus;
While printed people bear the blame
Without involving us.
So why need clerics prate about
The kind of books we read ?
'Tis thus we juggle Fate about,
And safely grow elate about
The lives we'd love to lead!

AN EPILEPTIC FIT.
A famous London physician, who for vears
made a study of this disease, has, after a number of
experiments, found a prescription which affords re-
lief in almost a marvellous manner. This has been
put on the market by Mr. I. W. Nicholl, Pharmaceu-
tical Chemist, of Belfast, under the name of
“Ozerine,” and the number of unsolicited testimonials
received speak well for its efficiency as a remedy
against this appalling disease. Your chemist can
easily get it for you.*

A MATTER OF DRESS.
fuscon, Arizona, Monday Evening.—Accused of
wearing an X-ray dress, Miss Claire Simpson, when
arrested, demanded to be tried by a female jury.
As the Court was unable to secure sufficient jurors,
the Judge discharged the accused, holding himself
incompetent to decide whether tights worn under-
neath a dress were really immodest.
United, States cablegram.
“Now, Judge, tell me honestly, is there any-
thing wrong with that ? ”
“Waal, Miss, Ptn hardly competent to decide,
but I guess it looks ail right.''
Arthur Percy Douglas, who died in Eng-
land last week as the outcome of injuries
received in the Aisgill railway smash, was
Lnder-Secretary for Defence in Maoriland
from 1895 to 1901. He was born in Devon-
shire and educated for the navy, which he
entered in 1858. He was a lieutenant when
he flew. out. He then pushed the barrow
as an instructor to naval artillery volun-
teers at Liverpool, but skied the heartbreak-
ing job inside three years and departed for
Maoriland. In 1885 he came into pro-
minence as naval staff-officer (Providence
alone knows what a naval staff-officer had
to do then in M.L.) during a war scare,
and thence passed to the positions of sec-
retary to. the Council of Military Educa-
tion, major and staff-officer of artillery,
commander of the permanent force at
Lyttelton, and, finally, Under-Secretary.
After retirement in 1901 he went back to
Britain. He was 68 years old when a whole
express train fell upon him simultaneously;
also and incidentally, he was the fourth
baronet of his tribe. Being a mixture of
Percy and Douglas he kept himself in the
picture by meeting a violent death not far
from the snot where two verv remote in-
dividuals of that name bangrd each other,
with fatal results to one. at the battle of
Otterburn.
THE APOTHEOSIS OF POUTJCAL
AUDACITY.
John Sands is the oldest business name
left in Sydney. There are one or two older
houses, but they have changed their names.
John Sands opened his shop in 1837 —the
place still stands in George-street, opposite
Lassetter’s. In 1847 he removed to
premises near the General Post Office, and
part of the business is still carried on on
that site.
The Bulletin receives this letter from
M. E. Kozminsky, of the Austral Hat Mills,
Melbourne: —
With reference to H. Kozminski, Adelaide (in
“Answers to Correspondents” column, Bulletin
of the 28/8/T3), 1 would like to point out that
H.K. does not belong to our clan, nor did we know
that anyone else bearing such an illustrious name
was to be found in Australia outside of Victoria.
People are many, and names are few, and
even if you are called Gad Borndrunk you
may find yourself duplicated. When Walter
Besant invented Uncle Bunker he seemed to
have struck something fairly improbable. Yet
there is a small tribe of Bunkers in Mel-
bourne and another in Sydney, and doubt-
less some of these Bunkers are uncles. Pro-
bably if you threw 10 big rocks in the
Strand, London, at a busy hour, the ninth
one would hit a Bunker and the tenth would
brain a Kozminsky. This comes under the
heading of the cussedness of inanimate
objects.
Died recently at New Norfolk (Tas.),
Thomas Allen Shone, whose memory carried
him back over some rough bits of early
history. He was born 91 years ago on the
patch where he died, his father having been
one of the. first to hold land in the New
Norfolk district. Shone remembered his
father taking part in the famous “Black
Line” of ’3l, when a futile attempt was
made to round up all the Tasmanian
natives. The drive resulted in the capture
of a lame man and a boy ; all the rest got
into inaccessible country. The fastnesses
of Mount Dromedary, near New Norfolk,
became 10 years later the stronghold of
Cash’s bushranging gang (Cash, Kavanah
and Jones), who once stuck up the Shone
homestead. Cash, the Claude Duval of
Australian ’rangers, told the story in his
memoirs. . The . conclusion of his yarn, by
the way, is quite in the best style of the
"'fllDnt; —
I was obliged to superintend the business in con-
sequence of the great number of prisoners we had in
charge, as upon all occasions we were obliged to
look after Jones [Jones was a notoriously rough
hrutel. He borrowed a valuable gold watch from
Mr. McKay, a silver watch from Mr Shone, together
with rings, purses, etc. ; also, miniature nortrait. of
a young lady from Mr. Ferguson, which I told him
to return to that gentleman, as he apneared to set
some value upon it, remarking that, although I was
fond of looking at a pretty face, yet I did not wish
to indulge this weakness at the expense of another.
He expressed his regret at seeing such men so un-
fortunately situated, and observed that he wished
he had the power to take us out of the colony. The
young ladies as well as all others in the house,
seemed quite at their ease, well knowing they would
not be insulted.
Young Tom Shone turned up at the house
just when the “stripping” was in progress,
and had the honor of looking down Kav-
anah’s gun.
Colonel Stephen Mallarky, recently dead,
aged 65 years, was a Sydney native, son of
Stephen Mallarky, a cooper, and Mrs.
Mallarky, a milliner, both in business in
Pitt-street. The boy entered the Govern-
ment Printing Office in 1864, and while
there joined the volunteer forces, and toiled
up to be commandant of his regiment. Tom
Itichards, Government Printer of that day,
was an enthusiast in soldiering, and many
of the stall' served under him.
A letter from the Apple Island :
The retirement of John McCormick from the posi-
tion of Chief Engineer in the Lands and \\ orks
department of rasmania has removed a striking
personality from the public service of the Apple
speck. McCormick, who was Engineer for Existing
Lines at the time, succeeded the late Hudson as
manager of the State railways when Hudson went
from Tasmania to join Tait in Victoria. But
McCormick’s luck was rotten. A succession of
bad seasons and worse counsels, coupled with local
prejudices and political interference, topped with
an unfortunate run of ill-health, settled his railway
prospects and relegated him to the engineer’s branch
of the Works Department. Tnere his atrocious luck
stuck to him, till Parliament and public clamored
for an imported commissioner, and McCormick, a
big-hearted, stocky man, passed out from the service
for good.
Died in S. Aus.: (1) Charles Kimber. of
Clare, in his time mayor, miller, first
grower of currant grapes in that now vinous
district, M.L.A., and many other things.
At 88, with an incurable and agonising dis-
ease, the veteran refused to be tortured fur-
ther, and strangled himself with his pyjama-
strings. (2) T. W. Green, “chief clerk”
since 1890 in the Chief Secretary’s Depart-
ment, and trusted adviser of many politi-
cians holding that office. It was his life’s
work ; he joined at 16, and died at 57. Ilis
place is filled by Ilari'y Blinman, a member
of the Board of Cricket Control, and pos-
sibly the best batsman who never got into a
test-match.
Mrs. Jean Lees, Adam Lindsay Gordon’s
first love, died lately at Holly Bank, Wor-
cester (Eng.). There is a good deal about
her in a chanter of the collaborated book
by Douglas Sladen and Mrs. E. M. Hum-
phries—“Adam Lindsay Gordon and His
Friends in England and Australia.” Mrs.
Lees inspired several of Gordon’s poems,
but refused his offer of marriage. She may
have suspicioned that poet’s wife is not
a happy one, and that the stream of sonnets
to her charms might dry un after marriage,
and, be replaced by “Ballads on the Bonnet
Bill” or “Songs of Sadness on the Stringy
Steak.” She was a Miss Bridges, whose
father gentleman-farmed at Broughton
Hackett, near Worcester. A sister. Sally,
married one of Gordon’s pals, Charley
Walker.
The Blacks of the Western District
(N.S.W.) have just put up stained-glass
windows in a local church, in memory of four
departed members of the clan. They have
been a luckless family. The old man built a
braw new hoose at a cost of £16.000, moved
into it, and died three months later. Then
Neil, his son, ( built an £lB,OOO house, and
sailed to England in the Waratah. He never
lived in his new palace. About 18 months
ago, A.J., the eldest son. while motoring on
a fine road from Camperdown to Terang,
turned the machine over and passed out.
Only one son. Stewart, is left; and at time
of writing he isn’t feeling too well.
A MELTING MOMENT.
The Attorney-General is known all over Aus-
tralia as “Iceberg” Irvine. To-day he belied his
reputation, for he was as warm as anyone could wish
him to be. —S. M. Herald, on Marconi debate.
Thawing the iceberg.
■Dr. Watkin, Methodist minister of St.
Kilda (Vic.), is reputedly the oldest Aus-
tralian-born clergyman in active work. He
is 74. His father lived to be S 3 and his
mother 94. His eldest brother is 83. His
father was one of the earliest pilots in Wel-
lington (M.L.).
One of Maoriland’s most noted seamen
hauled up his earthly anchor last week at
Wellington after S 3
years on the tide of
Time. That was Cap-
tain Edward Wheeler.
He first went down to
the sea in 18-14 in the
ships of T. and W.
Smith, trading out of
London. lie served
hi s apprenticeship
with them, followed
the _ long routes to
India and China as
second and first offi-
cer, and was mate of
the old Lord Ashley,
in 1858, when that
steamer arrived in
M.L. and was subsi-
dised by the baby
Government of the
hour to run mails
along the coast.
Twelve months later
he w r as master of the
ship, which belonged
to Pearson and Cole-
man, of Hull, the firm
that afterwards
merged into the Inter-
colonial Royal Mail
Co. Wheeler com-
manded most of the
vessels in that line,
and when the co. sold
out he joined the
M.L. Steamship Co.
From that flag he
transferred to the
Union Steam Ship Co.,
and, as one of the “star”
skippers, remained
with it for about 20
years. The old man
told the writer once
that no accident had
ever happened to a
vessel under his com-
mand ; and there was
nothing handy in the shape of evidence to
confound him.
TREASURER
CANN (N.S.W.).
Died recently out West, among the
N.S.W. mulga, Mrs. Kelly, aged 73, widow
of the late “Stockwhip” Kelly, journalist
and Parliamentarian. She and her hus-
band were among the pioneers of Bourke.
the river town that you reach (if you want
to reach it. which you probably don’t) by a
railwa" that shoots dead-straight for a
longer distance than any other railway in
the world save a rival concern in Argentina,
and a Russian line which starts from St.
Petersburg and makes a beeline south-east
to a place that sounds like a sneeze and a
pair of hiccups.
James Newton, who once represented
Balranald in the N.S.W. State Parliament,
died at Hav on Sunday. lie was elected
as a Laborite in 3891, and sat through one
Parliament. At the next general election
he was beaten by James Ashton. He was a
saddler, and it was the Carriers’ Union that
pushed him into politics. It took a good
deal of pushing to get him in, but he got
out quite easily. Strenuous campaigning
wasn’t in his line.
Died at the comparatively early age of
48, H. C. Hinder, one of Sydney’s leading
operating surgeons. Twenty-two weeks ago
he pricked his finger while performing a
sentic operation, and blood-poisoning set in.
Then ensued a tremendous struggle witli
death, in which Sydney’s leading profes-
sional men joined. At an early stage am-
nutation might have averted fatal results,
but the eminent surgeon, who would never
hesitate to recommend an Imroic operation
on a patient, and perform it successfully,
stood out against losing a limb of his own.
The fight la«ted five months, with a verdict
for the Old Reliable on September 14.
“David Jones,” of Sydney chiffons, is
celebrating a jubilee and a half. The
. original David was a Welshman, who, land-
ing at Hobart Town with his family, opened
a drapery business there in 1834. The fol-
lowing year—in September—Appleton and
Jones came on to Sydney, and commenced
business in Pitt-street, where Farmer and
Co.’s original short stood. The partnership
was dissolved in 1838, and Jones re-
moved to premises on the corner of Pitt-
street and Barrack-lane (now Barrack-
street). In 1857 he retired from business:
but his successors, Thompson, Symonds and
Co. not being successful, he returned in
1800, and started to build up again the
business of the firm. He succeeded, and
after seven years’ hard work again retired,
lie died in 1873. aged 80. The third genera-
tion of Jones is now in management. The
second was killed in the big Redfern rail-
way accident some 20 years ago.
THE WOWSER'S TART.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
in view of the great moral and spiritual peril
attaching to dancing, theatre-going, and other amuse-
ments of a questionable character, the members and
adherents of the Church are strongly urged to ab-
stain entirely from such amusements, and not to
allow themselves or encourage others in any prac-
tices, however alluring or fashionable, which tend
to lower the tone of their Christian experience or
lessen their influence for good.— Resolution passed
at a recent meeting of the leaders of a Melbourne
Methodist Church.
James Meekling is a Methodist,
A Wowser cold and slow ;
He never gives his girl a twist,
Or takes her to a show.
For naught could be improperer
Than going to the opera—-
His pastor told him so.
He shrinks from questionable joys,
Avoids all social snares ;
To dissipate, like other boys,
James Meekling never cares.
They oft are jesting giddily
Or waltzing Merry-Widowly,
Whilst he is reading prayers.
But though he feeds upon the Word,
And humbly serves the Lord,
His fiancee is sometimes heard
Suggesting she is bored.
In tones a little querulous
She doubts if pleasures perilous
Should always be abhorred.
Last night I found a chance to speak
Of worldly things to May,
And when I pinched her blushing cheek
She did not say me nav.
Methought the maid seemed pleasable,
For when I termed her “squeezable”
She whispered “Go away.”
I’m sorry for the Wowser’s “tart”
Whose passions are repress’t;
The yearning to be gay and smart
Must wither in her breast.
But drab and dreary piety
Has spared her much anxiety
It may be for the best.
Kind Providence, I often think,
Ordains that godly wives
Shall nag the brutes who swear and drink
And lead them awful lives,
Whilst May, the would-be anarchist,
Gets married to a Methodist,
And wears the Wowser’s gyves.

In 1889, when the Vic. Life and General —which had
been mostly life and very little general—decided
that it was no use attempting- to get life business
any more, this eo. was formed to take over such
other business as there was. No new shareholders
were got in; the Life and General just formed a
second co., put the old shareholders down for as
many shares in the new as they had had in the old
co., and paid up their shares to 10s. apiece for them.
No new organisation was attempted. Already on
the same premises there was a fire and marine co.,
composed of very much the same people, and they
probably thought that if they pushed business it
would be only a case of robbing Paul to pay Barna-
bas. And in that spirit the co. has lived and moved
and had its being. There was plenty of room for
it. Its canvassers, had an active lot been put on,
would probably have got business for the other Vic-
toria instead of taking any from it, just as un-
questionably the activity of the Queensland Insur-
ance Co. has won business for other Australian
offices, or as the campaign of the Citizens’ (now
M.L.C.) made it easy for the A.M P. to quickly
build up an Industrial business. However, the Vic-
toria General apparently didn’t want to do busi-
ness. So it grabbed what profits there were, and
starved the reserves; and thus it frightened off
business that might have come to it without being
dragged. Hence, while other Australian offices have
doubled and trebled their premium income during
the last 15 years, this one actually has less now
than it had in 1900: —

The expense rate is low, but that is nothing to its
credit. Any office could achieve a low expense rate
if it didn’t hunt for new business. The paid capital
is £20,000; and this and such reserves as there are
find investment in real estate ( £21,273) and cash
and public securities. The real estate is in at
‘•book values,” which may mean anything. Nobody
seems to care much what it does mean. Probably
somebody would revalue it, only it would be a
bother. And the Victoria General and Guarantee
is obviously a co. that detests bother.

This ought to be the biggest fire office in Australia.
It started 35 years ago with a heap of money and
pursey influence behind it. Ever since then it has
depended on its money and its pursey influence —the
influence of the Millers and the Paynes and the
Knoxes and the McCrackens. It has been too re-
spectable to take off its coat and slog in; too re-
spectable also to yell at the top of its voice that it
was an Australian office and claimed the business of
Australia on that ground, backed up by its own vir-
tues. But if it hasn’t hustled for business it hasn’t
minded hustling for dividends. It even paid 36 p.c.
in a year when there wasn’t enough profit to pay 30
p.c. Now, the shareholders have their reward ; they
don’t get a penny more dividend to-dav than they
got 20 years ago. If instead of paying 36 p.c. divs.
20 years ago (they paid even more than that some-
times in the ’Bo’s and early ’9o’s) they had paid a
reasonable 10 or 15 p.c. and had put 10 p.c. by and
spent the other 11 p.c. in pushing for new business,
the premium income would now be perhaps five
times as big. and the profit £129,000 instead of
29,000. The South British was formed in the
'7o’s, like the Victoria, and didn’t have half the
latter’s pull. For many years its paid capital was
less than that of the Victoria. But it kept its divi-
dends down, and it showed some enterprise, and to-
day its premium income is more than three times
that of the Victoria, and even if it didn’t issue a
single poliev the interest earned by its accumulated
profits could pay a dividend twice as big as the Vic-
toria’s. Further, the shareholders have got in the
past three years, in dividends and bonuses, as much
as the Victoria’s have got in the twelve! That’s
what a little self-denis! celled with a lot of intelli-
gent hustle means in fire insurance.
Mere, in brief, i< the Victoria's underwriting his-
tory since 1895: —

The Bui,i.' t v entertains som° doubts about that
low expense rate. In the Published accounts pre-
miums are shown on one side: on the other “rein-
surances, discounts and commissions” are lumped,
of course the reinsurances and discounts must be
deducted from the premiums; but in doing that the
commissions are deducted also. This means that the
commissions, or some of them, are not included
among the expenses. Other offices show their pre-
mium income reinsurances and discounts onlv;
commissions are then reckoned as expenses. No fair
comnarison can therefore be made betw°en the ex-
pense rate of this and other offices. As for the
funds, they are, on the whole, very satisfactorily in-
vested. Approximately £170,000 is in deposits or
public securities. All round, from the policy-
holder’s point of view, better things couldn’t be de-
sired. The less the business is pushed—that is to
say, the fewer the risks taken —the greater, of
course, the policy-holder’s security; and the more
of the funds that are kept liquid, the surer he is of
getting his money. It’s all right for him ; it’s the
shareholders and not the policy-holders who suffer
from the Victoria’s large capacity for eating its
cake and then going soundly to sleep.

It’s beautiful while it lasts, but it can't last much
longer. Twenty-three years ago the co. gave up the
idea of chasing life or any other sort of policies.
The public had got too well used to another kind of
office —the one which offered policy-holders the pro-
fits by way of bonuses instead of collaring them for
the fattening of shareholders So this co., which
consists largely of the “money” Millers, sat down
comt'ortablv to wait for the death of the last policy-
holder, and ever since has been praying hard that
he will live a long while, because the longer he lives
and the more premiums he pays the better it is for
shareholders. Fortunately the co. didn’t have to
keep up an elaborate establishment of its own. There
are three “Victoria” offices, including one that
handles fire business in a pretty large way; and
since this Life remnant needs only the corner of the
manager’s eye, the expenses are small. The reserves
now consist of assurance fund, £97,701 ; contin-
gencv fund, £7314; unappropriated, £7640 ; less
£5OOO to be cut out of one or other (or the lot)
for the half-year's dividend. In theory the assur-
ance fund is the right amount accumulated against
policy liabilities ; but clearly it is regarded as more
than sufficient, because a part of it is regularly col-
lared for shareholders. How much more can be
divided in this way there is no telling. Besides the
reserves there is £40,000 of capital; and in pre-
paration for the last trump the total funds have
been gradually drawn out of fixed and put into
liquid investments. There is £14,439 in mortgages
and £6848 in house property, but practically every-
thing else is in cash or securities that can be
promptly converted into cash.

Thirty years old, and reserves of £30,258. A few
minutes ago this writer was growling at a Vic-
torian fire insurance co., of much the same age
as this, and with a good deal less capital, because
it hadn’t accumulated more than £147,000 of re-
serves. And to show that co. how ashamed it ought
to be of itself, he had quoted a Maori land insurance
concern which, though but a few years older, had
£603,000 of reserves. Further, while the insurance
concern has its big money mostly in cash or securi-
ties that can be converted quickly into cash, this
Wellington co. has most of its poor little lot in
machinery (which would be worth very little if the
business shut down) and in merchandise stock.
Meantime the insurance co. is paying a 36 p.c. div.
and the successful manufacturing co. is not too sure
of its 8 p.c. There is something radically wrong
with a system under which such anomalies exist.
A good many insurance offices die in childhood, and
that is alleged to be the justification for big profits
when one does pull through. But an even larger
proportion of manufacturing enterprises fail ; yet
when one lives on, there is a yell about robbery as
soon as it makes 10 p.c., and a shrill cry goes up
to Heaven to pull the tariff down so that the robber
may be torn to pieces by foreign comnetitors. To
earn its poor little £9228 a year, and lay bv its
poorer little £2822, it is safe to say that this co.
has to think harder, and sweat harder, and ri.-k
more than a financial concern making three times
the money. It is all wrong, and it will go on being
wrong until people don’t explode in horrified sur-
prise at a Protected manufacturer making even 15
p.c. in good times. And it’s not only the cus-
tomers who will have to leave off being explosives.
Workmen are the same. As soon as a concern shows
a decent profit, it’s cut, cut, cut for more monev.
Mostly they ought to get more money (and, inci-
dentally, do a great deal more work for it, as a
rule) ; but if they are going to fill their own
pockets and empty the boss’s—well, there’s going to
be even less inclination to put money into manufac-
tures than there is to-day. And Heaven knows
that’s little enough. Here is this co.—this success-
ful co.—with an overdraft and other liabilities
amounting to, £48,788. not to mention deposits
against contingent calls ( £6828). Not one balance-
sheet in the past 12 years has shown the co. to be
out of debt. With manufacture properly regarded,
there would be no need for this persistent plaster:
Capital would come in sufficient to enable the co.
to trade on its own money.

Until lately this co. didn’t issue printed statements
showing how it stood, and no two copies of the
audited accounts available were in the same form.
Also, the management had a good habit of paving
bonuses to the staff and a bad habit of mixing them
up with the dividend totals ; and as the capital was
constantly varying it wasn’t possible to separate
them by computing the exact amount of the divi-
dend. However, the foregoing is probably pretty
close to the facts of the case. The position at the
outset was unusual. It amounted to a struggle on
the part of the two very junior partners in the old
firm to carry on the business when senior-partner
r mnev died. They did it, these two verv junior
partners, Coakes and Ivilroe, helned by the Queens-
land Trustee Co., which left £50,000 of the Finney
money in the business on debentures. Chairman
Brentnall, of the Trustee Co., had a seat on the
Board to watch the interests of the estate; and
when, a couple of years ago, the last of the debentures
were paid off, they had got to like him well enough
to invite him to stay where he was, and he had got
to like them well enough to accept.
Of course, this wholesale removal of debenture
debt hasn't been accomplished wholly out of profits,
especially as the co. has been doing a lot of other
things besides. This comparison will show that
aspect better: —
1905. 1910. 1911. 1912. 1913.
Capital .. . .£29,462 £67,293 £85,193 £151,993 £153,193
l>ebt to Finney’s 42,704 27.45 b 24,407
Other liabilities 6",962 133,795 126,b98 128,798 120,900
