In eight years £123,731 of new capital has been
got in, and the co. now owes £23,234 more. This
big sum of money has been supplemented by over
45 62,000 of earnings in excess of dividends; and the
lot is represented by additions of approximately
£70,000 to the stock, £105.000 to the freeholds
and over £30,000 to the book debts. Calculated
on the capital, in the early days of the co. the pro-
fits were almost phenomenal; but the shareholders’
risks were also very large, partly because of the very
heavy liabilities, partly because nearly everything—-
capital and borrowed money alike —-was in perishable
stock and the like. To-day more than the entire
amount of the liabilities is represented by city free-
hold property, leaving the stock, book debts, etc.,
to represent the shareholders’ interest in the vigor-
ous concern. The goodwill and other hypothetical
assets, which originally stood at £17,566, have been
swept off the balance-sheet, and reserves of £44,602
(after paying the current dividend) have arrived.
Certainly whether there is 20s. in the £ of the
capital, reserves and liabilities depends on the valu-
ations ; hut everything that it seems at all reason-
able to charge to working expenses has been charged
—such as the transfer to new premises and in the
past year the re-wiring of the buildings for extra
electric light and power. Also, depreciation (amount
unspecified) is charged direct to profit and loss; and
stock is taken at landed cost or under. On the
other hand, there is very little lay-by now after
paying the 8 p.e. div. If there have not been very
considerable non-recurring expenses, the margin
would certainly be perilously small in view of the
fact that Queensland —and especially Brisbane—has
been enjoying a prosperity which to some extent is
fictitious, and which in any case cannot be ex-
pected to continue at quite the recent pitch.

As one-fifth of the capital was transferred from
reserves a year ago, the 74 p.e. div. is really 9 p.e.
on the capital apart from premiums. Premiums
have, however, amounted to almost as much as the
reserves that were capitalised. From the published
accounts it is imposible to tell much about the cur-
rent history of the co. Practically everything de-
pends on the amount written off profits to counter-
balance the depreciation of ships. If too much is
written off, the net profits are under-stated ; if too
little is written off, they are over-stated. All that
can be said is that “ship properties” stood at prac-
tically the same in December, 1912, as in June,
1910 (there was only about £5O difference),
although during that time the Lady Denman had been
added to the flotilla. From this it seems that in 24
years the co. made enough, in addition to the pro-
fits shown, to pay for the Lady Denman, less what-
ever it got for one ancient ark which dropped out
of the fleet. The boats certainly seem to be written
down pretty well: there are 16 of them, besides a
launch and a cargo Dreadnought, and the lot stand
in the hooks at £47,298, which no doubt includes
some payments on account of two new ferries. With-
out swearing to it, this paper is, therefore, of
opinion that the true profits, on the whole, have
been quite equal to the profits shown in the table.
In addition to its collection of floating odds and
arks, the co. has £33,735 in freeholds, wharves,
etc., and £3559 is owing as a secured debt—the
latter probably money coming due on land sold. The
bulk of the freehold consist's of Middle Harbor fore-
shores, which are being got rid of as opportunity
offers. There may be money in these properties
some day; meantime they produce taxes and in-
terest bills, along with a small revenue in the shape
of picnic traffic. Also, they come in handy for
raising the wind now and then. Three years ago
the co. owed £12.380 on mortgage. This was re-
duced as at December. 1912, to £BOOO. Now, pav-
ments having been called for on the new vessels, the
plaster is again £12.000; and there is an old dpht
of £3OOO or so to the bank. The co. is probablv
as good as it looks; which is to say after it has
pumped out its 74 p.e. its nose is very little fur-
ther above water than it was after the same opera-
tion six months previously.

The co. decided a year ago to take stock and pub-
lish accounts only once in the 12 months ; therefore
the foregoing table has been recast, and the figures
are given as though yearly accounts had been the rule
all along. Capital is now £125,000; but as
£103,625 of this is represented by shop premises,
the business has to be financed by loans, etc., to
the amount of £67,769. Of this, £45,000 has been
raised on mortgage. Seven years ago the liability
on this account was £67,500. The reduction has
not, however, been brought about by applying pro-
fits that way, but by raising another £25,000 of
capital. This is poor business; it simply means
that, instead of paying (say) 4 pc. for the money,
the co. is paying 10 p.e. As the security
for the principal is good, 8 p.e. would
be quite a reasonable dividend. If that
instead of 10 n.c. were paid, the di-ectors could
apply the £2500 thus saved to the reduction
of the mortgage. As it is, the dividend has been
jumped up from under 8 p.e. to 10 n.c. in two
years without reducing the mortgage hv one farth-
ing. Shareholders are really missing a great oppor-
tunity. If the property were unencumbered, the
return would be magnificent; very few cos., with
four-fifths of their capital in solid city fr'ehold, can
show from 12 to 15 p.e. The cheapest way to get
it unencumbered is to forego some of the present
dividend; the dearest way is to raise capital to
wipe it off and then pay 10 p.c. on that canffal t,
the security was good enough for others to lend
it at 4 p.c., it is simply gluttonous on the nart P
the shareholders (who ought to be the people 1
interested in protecting the co. against gluttonvlT
require 10 p.c. on money put up for the same nur
pose.

The Union, which grew out of a Starr-Bowkott
Society eight years ago, has apparently been doin<*
very well. But it is a bit early to say how or what
it has really been doing. When you lend a man a
fiver for 10s. a month interest, it may seem <viod
business until you try to collect the interest. When
he tells you that he has just bought a dog for £]o
and you’ll oblige by adding the 10s. to the loan’
and if he doesn’t pay lithe £5 10s. and interest you
can have the dog, it may still seem good business
until you learn that the dog is dead and the man
is broke. “Ours are all live loans,” this society
says, “and we have no foreclosed securities, nor
any properties in possession.” Of course not.’ The
dog isn’t dead yet. But he isn’t nearly as healthy-
looking as he was a while ago; and, anyway, the
market value of dogs is slumping, and so the s’ecur
ity isn’t what it was. Quitting the kennels, the
general Australian position is that property values
have been sharing in a boom that has been due to
a largely-artificial prosperity. We have been boom-
ing because we have been borrowing hard and spend-
ing recklessly ; and when the borrowing ends and the
repaying begins, values are liable to go as much
below normal as lately they have been above it.
And the co. that is likely to be hit hardest is the
one tbat has lent the most while the boom was on ;
and this society has done the greater part of its
lending in that period. Here are some figures:—
Liabilities Assets.

Certainly the figures have moved the right way dur-
ing the past year. Apparently the new advances
have been small, and the old advances have been
reduced by instalments, so that the total outstand-
ing is less than it was in 1912. If values hold up
for a year or two, so that the old advances may be
still further reduced, and if wide margins are de-
manded between present values and new advances,
things may come out all right. But the society has
been taking risks. Not the least of them is the
maintenance of a practically empty till. No doubt
it has an understanding with its banker and can
overdraw. But suppose anything Jvappens to its
banker and a few depositors drop in for their
money, what then ?

The new Gas Act didn’t hit the country cos. as hard
as it did the two of Sydney and the one of New-
castle. So far, only those provisions relating to
pressure and purity apply to the country cousins.
But if they overstep the mark the Government is
at liberty to add their names to Schedule 1, and
that means a standard price and a maximum divi-
dend. Till then, however, their most likely trouble
will come from the municipalities. Already several
towns are supplied with Socialistic electric light
and power, so tbat cos. which are still the only
pebble on the local beach are moving cautiously, which
will most likely mean a tendency to think twice
before going beyond 10 p.c. or indulging in bonus-
share distributions. This co. is sticking to the 10
p.c. rate, and meantime is strengthening its posi-
tion very considerably:—

During those 10 years the co. has, moreover, been
making pretty numerous extensions, as witness the
growth of the sale of gas and by-products from
£1566 as at June, 1903, to £2165 for the past six
months. Also, it has apparently not neglected re-
pairs and renewals. On a capital of £7500 it is a
highly satisfactory performance—from the share-
holders’ point of view, anyway.

Co. got into difficulties, it made a bargain by which
it transferred its Hobart reticulation scheme to this
Gas Co. It was a good stroke for the Gas Co.
Though a heap of trouble has been met by the
Power Co., so much money has been spent, and the
scheme on which it has been spent is so excellent,
that sooner or later the work must be completed.
and when it is completed gas for light and po' ve j’
would have just about as hard a struggle in Hobart
as it has in Launceston, where the co. pays only 4
p.c. and begins to find difficulty in rising even to
that height. The Gas Co. has been for years supply-
ing current in a small way. Npw,, however, the pity
council has turned gas down and takes electricity,
and the co. has had to do some hustling, and rusn
in more plant. When the Hydro. Electric scheme
is completed this co.’s business will probably take
on quite a different character ; and past figures are
probably little good to judge the future by. Oniv
this can be said: the co. is very firmly established,
and its contract gives it a still bigger and surer
pull for the future. Hitherto it has financed its

arranging old ones, is often made in constructing the
building and installing machinery ere looking round
for means of transport of the raw material, half-
manufactured and finished goods. Messrs. Adolf
Bleichert and Co., of London, have issued various
catalogues and leaflets, illustrating and describing a
few of the many transport systems and plants which
they have supplied, and which show the solution of

ploying such appliances. W. and ,J. Lempriere,

terested. A specially trained engineer is retained to
consult and advise.*

From June to September the Bacchus Marsh Shire
Council (Vic.) crushed and spread 3000 cub. yds. of
road metal, at an average cost of 2s. 6d. per cub.
vd., and the cost of the spalls per cub. yd. averaged
2s. 4d., making a total cost of 4s. lOd. per cub. yd.,
broken and spread. Their last contract under the
hand-breaking system amounted to 6s. 3d. per cub.
yd., stacked on the roadside, and spreading Is. ;
total 7s. 3d., as against 4s. lOd. — a sarinrj of 2s. sd.
per cub. yd. This result was obtained by using a
Bigelow 16in. x 9in. Patent Stoneerusher, and the
Austral Otis Eng. Co., Ltd., of Melbourne, are the
manufacturers of these breakers.* [3

Cos. like this, that depend almost whollv on the
wool clip, have their off-seasons, much the same as
nubs, housebreakers and cold feet. The Jan -June
half-year is this co.'s dull time, except when an extra-
dry spell happens along, and then the whole 12
months is a dull time. But to be ruinous a drought
lias to be nowadays a more serious thing than it was
when the country was under-improved and over-
stocked, and the possibilities of “dry” farming hadn't
been developed. Besides, the co. has got itself into
such a solid position that it can pull through a
moderate drought with no great inconvenience- and
unless things change very much in the Deniliquin
district, it looks as though it may have to do some
of the pulling-through this year. The financial posi-
tion has been wonderfully strengthened of late:
. , _ t 1903 1913.
Liquid assets .. .. £9,793 £23 64“>
Liabilities . . . . 30,693 20,976
Excess liabilities . . £20,900
Excess assets . . . . £2 666
Of that £20,976 of liabilities, £20,000 is repre-
sented by 4 pc. debentures; ten years ago there
were £30,000 worth. During that' period the eo
has no.t only paid off £lO,OOO of debentures, but
has added £ 13.000-odd to its surplus of liquid
assets, and has meantime spent an average of about
£2OOO a half-year in renewals and maintenance.
But the co. has a dry spot to deal with, and one
that doesn’t attract the closer-settler to any extent;
so it has poor hopes of anything brilliant in the way
of dividends. Deniliquin's wheat crop last year ran
to only 4546 acres and Moama’s to 9929 acres ; and,
in spite of all the temptations offered by good wheat
seasons, there isn’t much in between except sheep
and big landlords.
The Mirror Ltd.—There is plenty of
room lor a second afternoon paper in Melbourne, and
the promoters of the Mirr r propose to occupy
that room. Their prospectus asks for appii-
cations for 100,000 shares, 8 p.c. prefer-
ence ; another 50,000 will be offered as soon
as the paper comes out. As their bite the promoters
take 20,000 shares, which will be entitled to two-
thirds of all the divided profits in excess of the 8
p.c. above mentioned. The Melbourne Herald
showed a profit in the last 12 months of £42,839.
Assume that the Mirror presently makes a divisible
£30,000. Then the prefs., who put up all the money
and take all the risk, would get £lB,OOO, or 12 p.c.,
and the ordinaries, whose holders put up nothing
and take no risk, would get £12,000, or 60 p.c. on
the shares for which they have paid nothing. Fur-
ther, though the prefs. put up all the money, and
though there are 150,000 of them, they are to col-
lectively have only the same say in the co. as the
holders of the 20,000 other shares who have put up
no money ; with this proviso—that if at the end of
three years the prefs. are not getting their 8 p.c.,
then each of the whole 170,000 shares will have the
same voting power. In addition to getting 20,000
fully-paid shares carrying these extraordinary privi-
leges, the promoters are to receive in cash' £3500
to pay them for the work done up to and including
the registration of the co. Moreover, the managing
director, who is one of the four provisional direc-
tors, is to receive £5OO a year “and a commission
of 15 p.c. on the total advertisement revenue.” To
be any sort of a paper at all, the Mirror should
have an advertisement revenue of at least £25,000
a year. This will mean £3750 a year commission
for the managing director, plus, of course, his
£5OO. On an advertisement revenue of £25.000
a year it is not at all certain that the co. would pay
any dividend at all ; but the managing director
would still be entitled to his commission. With an
advt. revenue of £15.000. or less, the paper would
be a rank failure. Nevertheless for being managing
director of the rank failure the fortunate man,
unknown to The Bulletin, who is appointed in
advance to the job, would still be entitled to £2750
a year, taking the revenue at £15,000. As before
remarked, there is room for a second afternoon paper
in Melbourne, and there should be good money in
it. The Mirror may be the paper that is wanted ;
but the terms and conditions upon which the public
is invited to subscribe the money to establish it are
so bad that the present prospectus should be immedi-
ately withdrawn and another substituted.
Talking of metal-market prospects at the
B. H. Prop, meeting, Chairman Darling said that
the unexpected return to what he considered a high
nriee for lead was due to (11 low stocks, and (2)
“the unsettled conditions in Mexico, which are esti-
mated to result in a reduced output from that
country at the rate of about 50,000 tons per year.”
_ITe couldn’t sav how long this reduction would last.
The chances, as thev aopear to The Bulletin, are
that mining in Mexico has received a knock from which
it will take some years to recover. After putting up
with a state of anarchy for many weary months, a
number of European and Australian engineers are
giving it best A prohabl.v-typical case is that of V.
Bennett, a Broken TTill-trained man, who, after six
or seven years in Mexico, has at last got tired of
running a fort as well as a mine, and is on the way
home. Still, the Broken Hill people don’t expect the
high prices to last, and, says Darling, “ we must do
our utmost to keep our output at its highest, so that
when the decline takes place we shall at least have
realised as much as possible at the full rates.” As
for 7,l'ne, a decline (from £2l 2s. 6d.) seemed to
some experts more probable than any improvement;
while opinions expressed as to silver favor no
material alteration.

In 1- months the Gibraltar. Adelong
(N.S.W.), has obtained 67850 z. of gold from 6040
tons stone crushed and 5540 tons battery products
evanided. The profit w T as only £6035, which spells
high costs. One reason for that is that the reef, though
wide in places, is cut up bv mullock bars. At the
9-00 ft. level a crosscut picked up the reef 50ft. north
of the main winze, and proved’ a make 30in. thick,
97ft. in length, and worth 36dwt. The ore from
this level has to be hauled hv winch to the 700 ft.
level, and then trucked to the ‘haft. A new
shaft is badly required to open up the levels below
900 ft. at a reasonable cost.
estralia’s gold output for the eight
months ended August was 15,2050 z. more than for
the same period in 1912. Returns from Bullfinch,
tictorious, Corinthian North. Queen of the Hills,
Edna May and one or two other plants brought into
operation during the expired portion of the year were
responsible. Several of the old-established mines
are also looking a bit better/ but unless other new
sources of production materialise very soon, another
backsliding is inevitable.
Myrtle Central, at Weston’s (W.A.). re-
cently turned down by Edna May, is being nibbled
at by a Melbourne syndicate headed by stockbroker
Mere and others. It is proposed to give the owners
cash and shares in exchange for the show.
Lupton’s (W.A.) shutters are up and
tenders are being called for the purchase of its
belongings.
* * * *
Yuanmi (W.A.) is putting 10.000 tons a
month through its newlv-installed sulphide plant,
and things are officially stated to be looking up.
Advances made in gas-producer practice now permit
of the plant being fed with 6ft. lengths of wood, and
this saves a few hundreds a year in costs.
Oroya Links (W.A.) has started to
deepen the Croesus Prop, shaft, in order to get at
the good values met with in winzing. Much open-
cutting has lately been done in this part of the co.’s
holding, and an immense amount of ore, mostly low
grade, has been exposed. Shares are low, and, on
the whole, the outlook is fair.
*** ' *
Like the man behind the wheelbarrow.
Chaffers (W.A.) reckons that it has everything ahead
of it. Ore worth £6 7s. 6d. per ton is alleged to
have been driven on for a length of 85ft. at No. 16
level, and a big tonnage of highly payable stuff is
declared to be on view in other parts. The show is
being billed as one of rich deep level possibilities, and
already some of its admirers are talking of dividends.
Time enough, however, to enthuse over the prospects
when the proposed new plant is running and assays
are confirmed by mill returns. Even then, if every-
thing turns out all right, a lot of money will be
needed for deep development, and shareholders will
most likely have to wait a long time for their cut.
Marvel Loch (W.A.) has taken a further
4260 z. out of the Comet lease, and now has nearly
50 men employed on the show. If the co. wants to
acquire the property, £l5OO will have to be paid
on account before October 1, and a similar sum six
months later. Meantime, strenuous efforts are being
made to tear out as much gold as possible.
Geologist Malcolm Maclaren thus sum-
marises the position at the Ivanhoe (W.A.): —East
lode enters porphyry dyke at 1970 ft. in section 1 on
the north boundary, and at increasing depths down
to 3630 ft. in section 15 on the south boundary. The
porphyry zone is not necessarily barren or poor, but
the ore body will be erratic. He anticipates that the
lode below the porphyry bar will be the same as
above, and recommends a shaft being sunk continu-
ously to 3470 ft. level. In other words, this very
reliable expert believes that the poor zone now being
tackled in the present bottom workings of the mine
will pass away, and that good continuous values will
be again encountered at depth. Maclaren is now in
the east, but returns west in about a month’s time
to look at the Associated on behalf of the London
Board. * * *
Mararoa’s new leases are expected to
infuse new life into Norseman. They once formed
portion of a big area worked by the defunct Norse-
man Gold Mines, Ltd., which started with a capital
of £60,000, extracted £200,000, and died without
leaving shareholders anything. Portion of the pro-
perty was then let on tribute, but most of it has
never been touched. Attention was recentlv directed
to it bv the discovery of rich ore at 600 ft. in the
adjacent Viking lease, and the exhibition of rich
specimens did the rest.
Golden Butterfly (W.A.) is still paying
out. At the outset it was inferred by press reports
that values would average from 40s. to 50s. per ton,
that profits would approximate £l5OO a month, and
that generally the outlook was golden. Then came
a time when the co. found it had made a few mis-
calculations, and was obliged to stir up shareholders
loi more cash. Latter!,v it has, by the aid of calls
and revenue won from battery plates, just about man-
aged to make ends meet. Now it confesses that “in-
trusions are affecting values,’’ and that “it is a diffi-
cult matter to come to any conclusion respecting ore
reserves. Which apparently is a roundabout way
ot admitting that the mine is not nearly as good as
had been represented.
* * * *
Tindal’s (W.A.) has decided to have
another go. 1 resh capital has been obtained, and
work is about to be resumed on lines recommended
by 1 aul, well-known in connection with the flotation
processes. Past obstacles in the way of Tindal’s
success have been its short and rapidly hipping shoots
and its hard ground. Costs swallowed up more than
the receipts, and the small amount expended in
development landed the co. further in debt. Share-
holders used to be told that profits were assured, but
when the annual day of reckoning came the accounts
told a different tale. The Bulletin never had much
hope that it would pay, and has no more now.
Bullfinch Prop, will be in a position to
pay a shilling by the end of next month, but will
probably wait until the end of the year and then dis-
tribute 2s. Developments continue satisfactory in
the northern series, and the mine is still two years
or more ahead of the mill.
Sons of Gwalia (W.AA is substituting
gas for steam. Two big charcoal gas-generators are
being fixed in position, and are expected to bite
£250 off the monthly fuel account. A 660 h.p. gas
engine is on order, and should be in operation early
next year. This engine will do the work of the
present steam mill-engine, and will also supplv
power for sliming the mill product. Generally the
mine is looking well at depth, but its value is shrink-
ing visibly.
Black Range West (W.A.), which has
been sky-rocketting on specimen stone, has now left
off talking champagne and got down to its beer,
samples taken for 28ft. along the reef, which varies
J" width from 3ft. 6in. to Bft., have averaged 38s. 3d.
This is exclusive of the specimen stone. Values are
extremely erratic, but they haven’t got below 21s.
anywhere. On position, the B R. West always did
look good ; and the shareholders, whose patience has
been much tried, may yet get their reward..
Wallaroo and Moonta sounds a rather
more cheerful note in the interim report prepared
tor next week’s meeting in Adelaide. In the Wallaroo
the 2310 ft. and 2430 ft. drives from Taylor’s shaft
have opened up good blocks of ore (expected, of
course) ; and drilling from Young’s, though it hasn’t
gi\en anything striking, has proved ore which may
come in all right for the flotation process. This
process is the really big thing that has happened to
the co. of late years. The Bulletin in past half-
years poked mild borak at the ponderous old parties
who didn’t visibly stir a muscle as they reported
that the process was “being tried.” It knew that they
knew (and said so in April, 1912) that the experi-
mental flotation unit, when compared with mill con-
centration, was making a product three times as rich
in copper and leaving less than one-third as much
the tailings. Now they go as far as to admit
' , the great success which has accompanied the
methods of ore-dressing through flotation at Wallaroo
has led to the adoption of the process at Moonta ”
What flotation means is that, not only will more
metal be got out, but the concentration costs are
low, and. the concentrates being so much richer,
the smelting costs will be reduced, it being obviouslv
much less expensive to smelt one ton of 22 p.c. than
three tons of p.c. copper. There have been some
ore developments also at the Moonta mine, hut it is
noor stuff. Still, there’s the flotation process waitin'*
for that too.
Wallaroo and Moonta doesn’t publish accounts at
the half-vear. hut it furnishes a statement of liquid .
and semi-liquid assets and liabilities. The surplus on
June 30 was £149.102. On Dec. 31, 1912. it was
£165.888. Meantime a couple of 2s. dividends have
been paid. In view of the outlay on the flotation
plants and the pretty strenuous prospecting, it is
satisfactory that the liquid assets were drawn upon
onlv to the extent of £16,786 to pav these divi-
dends. Still, copper having had a temporary slump
since the half-vear ended, for the current quarter the
directors pay only Is. There are 160,000 shares.
A careful examination of radium from the
Radium Hill Co.’s works has been made in Pro-
fessor Rutherford’s laboratory. The tests prove it
to be free from the cheaper radio-active substances
which, according to the expert, can easilv be mis-
taken for radium. The next thing shareholders would
like to hear of is the sale of a good-sized parcel at a
remunerative price. A large increase of capital is
asked for, so the directors presumably consider thev
are through with experiments, and can produce
radium by the gram. A gram mav he worth £15.000
when bought in very small quantities for experi-
mental purposes: hut if dividends are to he forth-
coming on a capital of £BO,OOO. a sizeable lump of
the stuff will have to be not onlv produced hut
marketed. An enthusiastic estimate is 12.000 milli-
grams per annum, or 1000 per month; but such com-
mercial results are still miles away.
Vulcan Tin fN.Q.), which got back to
the div. list the other day, made only £562 net
during the half-vear to August 19 ; but the mine is
shaping for much better things than that. As already
noted, the co.’s development work gave verv satis-
factory results from 900 ft. downwards, a new shoot
worth from 2 to 4 p.c. having been proved there;
and an aerial ropewav is now being put in between
mine and mill to reduce costs. At the end of the
half-vear the co. had £5493 in cash, beside stores,
and owed less than £5. People who expect big
things out of 4 p.c. ore at Ardlethan mav he in-
terested to learn that the Vulcan recovery last half-
year was 3.6 p.c. black tin, and the net profit was
an almost exact 3s. per ton.
Woodlark Island, the interesting dab of
dirt outside Samarai (Papua), is feeling very good.
The King, one of the small claims on the local reef-
ing field, lately got down to the No. 4 level, and in
three weeks knocked 120 tons out of a good 4ft.
reef. This parcel gave 14920 z., worth £5147. From
the upper levels the yields have been: No. 1 level
and stopes (30ft. of ground), 300 tons for 810 oz. ;
No. 2 (12ft. of ground), 240 tons for 848 oz. ; No. 3
25ft. of ground), 630 tons for 42500 z. At No. 4
”the reef is still good underfoot.
Apropos the dispute at Mount Morgan
over the virtues of brigalow as a mine timber, the
local warden, who has been holding the official in-
quiry, has decided not to agree with either party.
He adjourns the case till March, 1914, and meantime
a test is to be made in a stope chosen for the
purpose.
Oxide Creeks have had a flutter in Sydney.
Contributing shares (2s. paid) have reached about
13s. ; fully-paid shares were at the same time quoted
at about 3s. This was good proof that the market
value of the shares had no connection with the value
of the mine. It seems that a few contributing shares
were sold by foolish people who didn’t have them;
and when they were called on to deliver the scrip,
the scrip wasn’t available. Presumably it had either
never been issued or Was locked up, and the people
who held the paper were able to demand their own
price. The Bulletin has no sympathy to waste on
the people who sold what they didn’t have; but that
does not end the matter. What ought to be probed
is whether the co. was ever really floated. If the
thing isn’t a mere peg on which to hang a gamble,
it ought to be easv to show that the money for the
shares was actually paid to the co., and that the
co. has it. The mine may be as good a
one as reports allege that it is; but there are
other things of first-rate importance. For instance,
(1) Does ti,e co. own the mine? (2) Has it paid
for it? (3) If it does own the mine, and has paid
for it, how many shares were applied and paid for,
and how much money is there to the credit of the
co. to open up the property ? Investors should get
a satisfactory answer to these questions before they
touch the shares.
Although very little is being said about
it, good progress is being made at Cock's Pioneer,
Eldorado (Vic.). The cutting of a deviation of the
creek to protect the paddock is well under way, and
a barge site has been holed out. The old machinery
has been dismantled and a new site for the outfit
has been pegged at the bottom end. of the leases, ft
is understood that electricity will continue to be used
to work the new plant. .Jack Dane, who actually
pulled off the deal in London, will deserve more than
a vote of thanks from the old shareholders when the
wheels commence to move and grind out something
better than exes.
The Shell Transport and Trading Co.,
the big tnostly-English concern which has got a
grip on the “Eastern" oil trade, has given Maori-
land best. It has been spending a good deal of
money to get a Maoriland supply on a commercial
scale, but with such poor results that it “does not
propose to do any more in that direction.” Last
year, by the way, this co.’s profits ran to nearly
1+ millions, and a div. of 30 p.c. was paid on the
capital of L 3,000,000 The co. is now probably
about the biggest individual employer of Javanese
and other colored labor in the world.

ERRYBMP.
BOUGHT A BOTTLE.
“I had pains in my legs, which the doctor said
was rheumatism,” writes Mr. Robert Cochrane,
builder, Waimiha (N.Z.). “His lotion did me no
good, and I suffered misery. I saw a friend using
some liniment, which he told me was Chamberlain’s
Pain Balm, and offered me his bottle to try it. It
did me so much good that I bought a bottle myself
and was soon free of all pains.”*

A WOMAN'S
LETTER
Sydney, September 15, 1913.
My Dear Arini, —
The British politicians and their female
relatives have finally fled south on their
way to Britain. A few of their motor and
other excursions were apparently arranged
on the assumption that every day contained
about 36 hours. Meals were served with
the profuse hospitality that feeds its vic-
tims on 17 courses or so of solid food every
three hours, and keeps them going with
light refreshments every half-hour in be-
tween. The flight to Yanco and Canberra,
with a wild gallop round the rim of Burrin-
juck was part of the 36-hours-in-every-day
itinerary. One of J. Bull’s innumerable
breakfasts on one consecutive morning was
to have occurred in the McCaughey’s shear-
ing-shed at Yanco. Luckily for the travel-
lers, the shearers happened to be finishing a
clip. The Emmott lady collected nothing
but a frilled-lizard in her up-country trek.
Surgeon Critchley Hinder, of this city,
who died of blood-noisoning after a long
fight with the Shadow, was known and es-
teemed as one of the most unselfish men in
the profession. He loved his work much
better than his fees —a curiously uncom-
mercial frame of mind which was often im-
posed on. Mrs. Hinder, who used to be
Miss Enid Pockley, has been for the last
seven or eight weeks her late husband’s de-
voted day nurse. A trained Nightingale
took the night watches. The medical pro-
fession here fought for Surgeon Hinder’s life
with everv remedy known to modern science
-—but the Light went out at last.
Dorothy King, the youngest grand-
daughter of the late Mrs. Thorne, of this
village, is. or ought to be, here as I gum up
this missive. She hurried home from the
U.S. to be present at brother Roy’s wed-
ding in November. The bride will be Mabel
Preston.
Mrs. Marcus Clarke has just passed
through Sydney on her way home to Oak-
leigh (Vic.). She is widow of the man
who wrote “For the Term of his Natural
Life.” A trip to see a married daughter,
Mrs. Ro«e Bradley, who lives at Lithgow,
brought Mrs. Marcus to this State.
Brewer E. Resell sends a postcard to a
friend in this town, which bears the dates
1863-1913. Lie says. “I am at the present
time here in Wusseralfingm. Wurtemberg.
It is this day, August 5. 50 years ago.
Uat I left bpre for our beautiful, sunny
Australia.” So the brewer has done a con-
siderable absence.
Journalist J. D. Fitzgerald drops in.
“Have been leading a strenuous life in
Europe and in U.S. A. Join family in Paris
on August 9, then Switzerland for a rest.
Daughter has made wonderful strides with
Burghi. of Milan. She makes her debut
next March as Gilda in ‘Rigoletto.’ ”
The horticultural Miss Brace strode ashore
on the Maheno gangway the other evening,
after doing 18 months’ hard labor in M.L., on
Hawke’s Bay soil.
T. E. Sedgwick, a wandering Londoner,
left his foggy port for Melbourne in the
Port Lincoln. He is in charge of 200 boys,
who are to be planted on the soil by the
Victorian Government. Sedgwick is an
industrious globe-trotter. He has scam-
pered throuah Maoriland twice, and once
across the Canadian frosts. This trip he
is "oing to stuff himself with facts con-
cerning the attractions of Australia for
British immigrants.
Arthur Rees, formerly of Melbourne Age,
has had his recent M.L. novel. “The Merry
published in London and the
U.S. Gordon and Gotch have arranged for
the Australian issue, which strikes this con-
tinent immediately—or a week later.
The last English mail brought a few
informative remarks from Grace Watson.
She says in extra-large type : “If you want
domestics, fares must be advanced and re-
paid out of wages.... Sydney has a most
undeserved reputation here (London) as a
place for immigrant women to stay away
from. This is largely due to the inadequate
provision for them on their arrival in your
port.” Grace adds in parenthesis: “If the
women electors of Australia could form
any idea of the deplorable conditions for
women workers in Britain, they would
never rest till thev had done their level best
to help thousands of them to emigrate.”
Widows and waitresses. I understand, are
being carefully collected by the Common-
wealth. Steady instalments of husbands
and mothers-in-law may follow.
C. C. Reade, an ex-Maoriland journalist
now in the Fog. is booked for a lecture
tour in our island. “Garden CitPs”
will be the floral trimming of his dis-
course. It is to be delivered here, as per
invoice, in 1914.
Skipper Harrassowitz. who used to steer
one of the German liners alongside Aus-
tralian wharves, is now at the wheel of the
vast Atlantic craft, Berlin. This steamer,
which would make the Ark look like a bath
tub. cuts through the green seas between
Noo Yark and Bremen. Miss Harrassowitz,
a niece of the above mariner, went off to
the Fatherland in the Zieten.
A well-known M.L. stethoscope, Dr.
Hogg, passed this way the other day on the
long, wet road to Europe.
Commonwealth banker Dennison Miller
has torn off the necessary cheque to buy
Cliffbrook. This overlooks the tumbling
surf and the mixed bather at Coogee. An
acreage large enough to support several
cows and a herd of goats goes with the
house.
Mrs. Eisner, a heavily-gilded N’Yoi'ker
(do you notice how German the American
married lady is?) recently swept out of
these metropolitan premises with a few
mountainous Saratogas. She’ll unpack
some of her baggage in Colombo. A month
in Ceylon’s dusky isle is included in this
traveller’s gallop round the globe.
John Spencer Brunton, who recently
bought Glen Yarrah (Double Bay) won’t
enter into possession till March. Pending
the arrival of the new owner, a dining-room
and other alterations will be added to the
harbor views and picturesque gables of the
leafy homestead. The present name is to
be wiped out with a fresh coat of paint.
The old house will answer (if you call loud
enough in 1914) to Gladswood. It is to be
called after an ancient property in Scot-
land, where the Bruntons once lairded it
gaily ove’’ local rustics amid the screech of
bagpipes. Perhaps it’s Oledswood, which
has a more of a plaid accent than Glad-etc.
After two months’ ski-exercise on Kosci-
usko’s snowy pate, Norwegian Consul Fay
has torn himself away and returned to his
Melbourne office.
The King’s School’s Headmaster, Stacy
Waddy, who is away on leave of absence, is
going to cut his trip to Europe short. This
will give him a month or so in Ceylon, while
brother E. F. Waddy and the N.S.W. team
are cricketing in that island. These two
parsons, cricketers and pedagogues follow a
strenuous triangle of occupations. They’re
both as keen at bat and ball as the average
girl is about a new hat.
Some Sydney folks will recall the little
Count de Marzac, who was in these parts
awhile ago. His countship is of the genuine
vintage, and grows on the banks of the Dor-
dogne. In a French chateau, covered with
green whiskers and earwigs, the owner has
stuffed hospitality down the throats of any
wandering Australians he met on this side.
The last batch to be whirled off to the de
Marzac country lair were the R. H. Todds
and Violet Ross-Johnson, of this town. They
put it on record that French roads are too
polite to bump you twice in the same place.
Miss Weigall, a daughter of the late head-
master of Sydney Grammar School, is culti-
vating poultry and the home-grown egg out
Hurstville way. The place is roomy enough
to hold some of the Horticultural dames who
are scratching round for a temporary plot
of earth. If these ladies can all agree to-
gether for five minutes, they may make up
their minds to do a little spade work in the
Hurstville acreage.
The Fred Walkers, of Chatswood, are
coming back in the Otranto. He is some-
thing moderately tremendous in the frozen
meat trade.
The most edifying entertainment next week
will be the conversazione in the Sydney
’Varsity Medical School. This sociai con-
flagration will blaze with information, which
is to be turned on to the parents, sisters and
best girls of grads and undergrads, free
of charge.. Every department in the learned
pile will bristle with facts, specimens (in and
out of glass cases), and microscopic details.
Spectacled guides will gently lead the visi-
tors up to bottled science (beautifully pre-
served in spirits), and fill them with as much
knowledge as they can hold at one sitting.
Dipsomaniacs who attend in the hope of
getting a chance to drink the spirits and then
pawn the preserved lizard, and further dissi-
pate on the proceeds, are solemnly warned off.
Governor Strickland has notified his inten-
tion of being present. Either the hon. gradu-
ate sec., Dr. Schlink, or the hon undergrad,
sec., B. C. Pockley, will answer any inquiries
from ’Varsity boys re this reunion of the
Bolus Brotherhood.
Though the wattle, or most of it, sold here
on September 1 wasn’t worth buying, being
in the sere and yellow fluff, it was quite
worth selling. The Free Kindergarten
people got about £9B Is. llfd. for the lot
they coaxed a soft-hearted public to take
from them. Mrs. Devereux, the playwright’s
wife, was a most persuasive assistant in the
cause. This will bolster up the £4OO deposit
on the purchase of a Kindergarten Training
College. Hilton, which the painter and the
paperhanger are going to smarten up for this
purpose, used to be the home of the late
John Rae, a one-time Under-Sec. for Lands
in N.S.W. He went to live in the now con-
gested suburb of Darlinghurst at a date when
it was regarded as rather reckless to dwell
so far from the city, and he lived to see blue
metal take the place of mud and bushrangers,
and the kangaroo fleeing in horror before the
tramcar. Some of his sketches are useful
contributions to the records of Sydney vil-
lage in its querulous infancy.
Supposing the antemic city clerkess should
carefully inspect the basement of the Town
Hall this week, she will be stunned at the
enterprise of her own sex. Women who
are making cash in out-door pursuits are
going to demonstrate the right way to bully
fowls into laying eggs at 2s. a dozen. The
What-to-do-with-our-Girls’ Exhibition, which
opens with a terrific flow of oratory before
this meets your eye, is also responsible for
110 different schemes for keeping us out
of mischief. The Farran Sisters, of Moss
Vale district, have reached the scientific
phase in their attack on the fruits of the
earth. They will evaporate an apple while
you wait, and lay out a baby orchard as
an evening pastime.
Bessie Knight Brown, of Mount Irvine,
is in charge of the dairy section of the
What-to-do Exhibition. Bessie knows the
artful cow in all her moods and muddy
tracks, and, when Strawberry tries to hit
her with the clod of soil at the end of her
tail, the said Bessie can dodge with the best.
She is going to demonstrate everything that
can happen to milk, except the rain that
suddenly falls into it when it bangs against
the suburban back fence. Assisting dairy-
maids, gowned in lavender muslin and white
aprons, will be as unlike the real thing as
possible. Blucher boots and cornsack cos-
tumes are reserved for Mrs. Mooly’s private
At Homes.
Mrs. Beryl Ireland’s miniatures and
water-color sketches are on view in Anthony
Horderns’ Fine Art Gallery. A few oil
paintings in small allotments are included
in this moderately-good show. The minia-
tures are the best examples of the artist’s
work. Mrs. Ireland knows how to dress
her subjects. A Marguerite type of girl
with fair plaits and a brown dress is an
effective pictorial portrait. An attache
militaire with a crescendo moustache has a
becoming dash of red over his left ribs. The
Duke of Wellington looks as mild as a
drawing-room swain in a miniature in-
dexed “copy on paner.” But the work in
this, as in another copy, “The Last Phase,”
is good. The Portrait of Gen6e bears a
general family likeness to that muscular
sylph. A few charming portrait studies on
ivory—notably a dark-haired damsel with a
scarlet blossom in her hair, and a small
Danish baby named Olaf —and a generous
helDing of mediocre work of no startling
interest, make up the exhibition. The land-
scapes are too ladylike. Westminster
Bridge (London), with a smoke-grey atmo-
sphere, escapes this fate. I
The Sydney Repertory Society intends to
form a Ladies’ Committee, to advance the
interests of the society, on the lines of the
Ladies’ Committee of the Melbourne Reper-
tory Theatre. A meeting will shortly be
called at the Australia. The first perform-
ance of the Society will take place on Octo-
ber 2, when a six days’ season of Shaw’s
“The Devil’s Disciple”—one of his “Plays
for Puritans” —will commence. Miss Aggie
Thorne (Mrs. Deery) will appear in the
leading part.
Crowds attract crowds. If the police
would allow 50 men to stand in Martin.
Place in a group for five minutes, 2000
would fight to join them. The sub-conscious
influence of a small, noisy section of a con-
cert hudience works up the encore into a
public nuisance. The McCormack man-
agement has been able to truthfully advertise
a fair guess at the numbers turned away at
the nightly stampede outside the Town Hall.
John McCormack draws like Melba. But
the audience on Saturday night reached a
frenzy of excitement that would have en-
cored an organ-grinder, and demanded the
frequent reappearance of his monkey. The
choir seats were chiefly filled with young
women and girls. Those of the front row,
being mostly in the flapper stage, almost
foamed at the mouth with excitement when
the mighty tenor fled from their outstretched
hands. John may have escaped with his
coat tails intact, but at one or two crises
in the evening it looked as if he would be
lucky to get away with his life.
John McCormack is probably sitting on
the highest Alp in his vocal career at the
present moment. Until the rabid encore
fiends had hounded him into singing himself
husky on Saturday night, the unrivalled
beauty of his voice floated, tone-perfect, over
an enraptured crowd of adorers. The male
section, towards the back of the hall, re-
lieved its feelings by standing up at intervals
and snapping out a clamor like short, sharp
barks. Many warblers have given us Lan-
don Ronald’s “Down in the Forest.” Only
two. Melba and McCormack, can sing the
final bar with the exact reproduction of the
sweet, high note of a skylark—“ Only a bird.”
Vincent O’Brien, at the piano, began to show
signs of encoritis, at the end of the uproari-
ous evening. John’s mouth was also falling
down at the corners with sheer fatigue,
when some genius discovered that turning
off the lights would be the only way to get
rid of the audience. The youthful May
Huxley will have to watch her breathing
more carefully. She was obviously short in
the wind, and red in the face in consequence,
both in “Spring’s Awakening,” her best effort
on Saturday night, and in some of her trills
in “Lo Here the Gentle Lark.” The flute
obligato, by Schienpflug, was the most artis-
tic end of the gentle lark’s sotto voce
remarks. Fiddler Donald Mcßeath was de-
lightful when he fiddled the music that once
hummed in the brains of Schumann and
Saint Saens.
Now that “A Fair Highwayman” is col-
lecting her popgun and riding breeches in
order to depart from this town the average
woman realises that she was not built to
carry firearms. A good, plain kitchen poker
that won’t go off is our best weapon when
the cat is again mistaken for the burglar
Madge Titheradge as a toy highwayman
makes a fair attempt to shoot the nearest
star, by aiming straight up in the air when
she ought to be slaughtering at least one
robber on the stage heath. Lizette Parkes
is far more convincing as a mere girl
Charles Quartermaine is an English-French-
llighlander. Red ribbons dangle on the
Highland part of him —his legs. The French
part has a fine Cockney flavor. He gambols
through several acts as playfully as a
tame elephant—that’s some more of the
French part (as Vicomte de Chamerolles)
bubbling over the top of his Hieland socks.
From N’ York comes a p.c. of the fiercely
Deadwood Dick order of illustrated
matter. It shows a fake scene on (or al-
leged to be on) the old Success. The
“prisoner” looks like an early Moody and
Sankey convert except that he lacks the
furnishment of a hymn-book. His eyes are
roiled up to Heaven, and his hands are
meekly clasped across his stomach. There
are the usual accessories—heavy chains, as-
sisting convicts, and a warder with ex-
tended arm (a warder in these bad old days
spent his whole time with one arm ex-
tended. furiously ordering some criminal to
receive two lashes without the option, and
his arm got fixed that way. and
when he was buried the extended hand ap-
peared above the grave and pointed to-
wards Heaven). This great effort is lab-
elled “The British Convict Sjiip Suc-
cess, oldest ship afloat, launched at Moul-
mein, Burmah, 1790.” An American
pressman writes across the back : “You will
see by this that Australia is being adver-
tised in our Harbor; but not, perhaps, the
sort of ad. you would prefer?” This re-
minds me of the question a little girl asked
Prof. W. James. “Is a skunk smell better
than no smell ?”
The Actors’ Association of Australasia is
putting on its grease-paint for a great cor-
roborree in this humble but important
spot on September 19. The programme
for this matinee is almost terrifying
in its length and importance. All
the highest-priced mummers in this city
will give their assistance. For the first time
on any stage “The Bishop and the
Buns,” a comic interlude, written by Kodak
O’Ferrall (of The Bulletin) will sniff the
footlights. It must be a bath-bun, for
Walter Bentlev is to appear as his Gaiters
of Bath
The long-legged beauty-actor, Herbert
Clayton, is now writing sketches and play-
lets amid the fastnesses of the Thames. He
was out here about three years ago in the
“Ivin" of Cadonia” and was something of a
matinee pet. A recent cable flashes the
news Clayton’s success in a comedy
sketch. “A Cold Douche.” This was pro-
duced at the Empire.
From “Rosna” (the lady who was once
this paper’s Adelaide Chatterer), in London:
Kate Rooney’s departure for a three years’ warble
in Uncle Sam’s land was a lightning-change act. She
accepted the U.S. offer, let her house on the
Thames for seven years, stored her household gods
away from moth and rust, and departed—all within
a fortnight. Mr. Kate, whose name is Kirkham, and
whose calling is the collecting of things artistic, has
removed himself to the New York office of his firm
meanwhile.
Yours affectionately.
VANDORIAN.
Hungary, where the bands of music come
from, is an exciting country for the poli-
tician. Count Tisza, the Premier, has
fought three duels this year with members
of the Opposition. He would probably
silence a want-of-confidence motion with a
Maxim gun.
There is a bright Maorilander amongst
the scientists who are to journey from
Britain shortly to attend the Conference of
the British Association for the Advance-
ment of Science in Australia. That is
Ernest Rutherford, F.R.S., who has made
a specialty of research in radio-activity.
Forty-two years ago he was born in the
small town of Nelson, and was partly edu-
cated there, partly at Christchurch, partly
at Cambridge (Eng.), and. generally, where
fitting opportunity offered. In 1892 he
lifted his B.A. and a senior scholarship at
Canterbury College; in 1893 he added
M.A. to his assets, with first-class honors
in mathematics and physics; next year he
was B.Sc., and collected a notable scholar-
ship ; then he went to Cambridge and piled
up research degrees and studentships until
his old masters in M.L. leaped like the
jubilant angora. In 1898 he was ap-
nointed a professor of physics at the McGill
University, Montreal, Canada, and his re-
putation grows as he progresses.

MELBOURNE CHATTER.
Melbourne, September 15, 1913.
My Dear Yarrie, —
The All Australian Exhibition, which is
to advertise our industries for a whole two
months, had its beginning on Saturday, in
the Exhibition. The general public were
admitted in a plain, commercial way at
1.30; but that being lunch-time in the Best
circles, the G.-G. was not programmed to
declare the show open until 2.30. Until
the functionary arrived, no one took the
slightest notice of the drifting flocks of
visitors. Exhibitors in their shirt-sleeves
tinkered away distractedly and abstractedly
with hammers and let the crowd go by.
One high functionary was overheard to say,
“Oh, when we get this opening fuss over,
we’ll be able to show things.” At one en-
trance a couple of cleaner men, with enor-
mous brooms, shoved the last of the dust
out of the way. At the other door a band
lolled about in the midst of instruments
carefully bagged in green baize. Before
the magic hour of 2.30 this same band,
neatly uniformed and arranged in a circle,
was standing to attention and playing up-
roariously. The neat little G.-G. and tall
Help Burnham whipped out of the purple
motor and waded through an east wind.
They were received in stately manner by
the Gaud Mayor, Prime Minister Cook,
secretary Ashby and a regiment of commer-
cial headmen. The G.-G. made some appro-
priate remarks, after which a rush was
made for the feasting marquee out in the
oval. Here more speeches flowed pleasantly,
until a sudden rainstorm drummed fiercely
on the canvas roof and almost drowned the
talk. By the time Thomas Hogg got up to
propose a Commonwealth toast he had to
shout to make himself heard, and the un-
easy congregation opened what umbrellas
it had to ward off the drops from the wet
canopy. And while they were “opening” it
outside, the opened show inside was getting
along drily without talk. The timber ex-
hibits are very striking. South Australia
has a big show of woods, and there are
Tasmanian timbers on view that are as
beautiful as any in the world. In particu-
lar there is a Tasmanian oak that has been
burnt off in the old, blundering way, and
roughly condemned as “no good.” Prejudice
and stupidity are fearful drawbacks. It is
on record that one of the oldest Tasmanian
squatters, with this and other beautiful
timbers growing all round him, imported a
whole shipload of cedar to build houses with !
After the speeches the organ had an up-
roarious innings—much to the delight of
the Hennessys. Good musical programmes
are advertised for the days and nights of
the Exhibition, and sports are to rage in the
open air.
The Ice Carnival that served to farewell
the two German instructors was not the
absolutely last glide of the season. The
management of the rink were induced to
hold one more slither on Saturday, Sep-
tember 13, when all the fancy dresses of
Society were aired once more. The season
was closed regretfully, for the lively patron-
age of the G.-G. was worth a lot to the
business. The two flattered German in-
structors departed by boat for the Father-
land loaded with presents. The G.-G. per-
sonally presented “Fraulein” with a bouquet
and a trinket or two, and told her how he
had enjoyed the sport while following her
lead. Nancy, the picturesque daughter of
the Frank Maddens, won a prize for skim-
ming about as a dragon-fly. Clarence
Weber was picturesquely unclad as a
Roman gladiator, and collected a prize for
looking “perfectly lovely.” The manage-
ment kindly allowed the revel to whirl until
long after the discreet 10.30 closing bell
had tolled the end of the season.
The Ladies’ Kennel Club Show, in the
Guild Hall, was all pow-wow, bow-wow and
staccato excitement. Two exhibitors, Mrs.
Keep and daughter Heather, arrived laden
with two poms and two blue Persian eats,
named respectively Mona Lisa and Napo-
leon. Napoleon, the Persian, suddenly
glimpsed a chance of escape from his ever-
lasting satin cushions, and dashed down an
unhallowed lane off Lonsdale-street in search
of the adventures any healthy cat desires.
Heather followed at top speed, clutching
Mona Lisa, and mamma and several she-
attendants rushed after her. All the plain
and colored youngsters of the district
gathered like a ragged army and joined in
the chase. Puss at last took cover in a
store, and when the proprietor had re-
covered from the shock of being charged, he
captured the harried brute and restored
him to his panting mistress. Later on the
recovered Napoleon, with a sullen glow of
resentment in his eye, carried off the prin-
cipal cat prize. Edith Hope-Campbell, a
great dog-lover, took the money at the door.
Her collie, Derryboy, a consistent winner of
champion prizes for years past, met with an
accident some time ago. and had to be
destroyed ; so his bereaved owner turned her
back on yelping dogdom and attended to the
cash that dieth not. Louise Pinschof, being
secretary of the show and a busy girl,
scarcely had time to acknowledge congratu-
lations on the prizes attained by her Gordon
setters. Mrs. Charlie McEvoy was cata-
logued as the owner of a great sleepy cat
or half-lion that weighed 14 pounds and
would be heard bounding on the roof of a
seven-storey warehouse, if he were allowed
to do such things, which he isn’t. Audrey
Chirnside showed a champion terrier bred
by Mrs. Aberdeen, wife of the Viceroy of
Ireland. Mrs. Lauchlan McKinnon ex-
hibited her beloved fox terriers. Two Swiss
dogs with pasts were stared out of coun-
tenance. Though worth enormous sums,
they were recently stolen and sold for a few
shillings. Some dishonest people have a
ridiculous idea that one dog is as good as
another, and that no dog is worth much. It
is about time that someone invented a com-
pletely new type of dog—for preference a
toothless dog that will only howl once a
night and then subside with a rattle.
The Sandringham golf outbreak wound up
last week, and the enthusiasts are blaming
the rival sport of horse-racing for some of
the G.-G.’s absence. Popular Ivo Whitton,
who is adored by the girls, won the Open
Championship, and Ben Lempriere got away
with the Amateur —decidedly the most popu-
lar win of the meeting. President Scobie
McKinnon, the big Scot who wears a tam-o’-
shanter with an air—gave a tea party to
mark the conclusion of the devotions. Some
rain fell, but it was borne in a hardy Scotch
spirit, and host Scobie beamed hanoily at
a marquee full of cheery people. The rain
made the steps of the club house very slip-
pery, and one pretty girl had a nasty fall.
However, she showed some nice silk stock-
ing and a very neat pair of ankles, so the
accident had its compensations. Mrs. Dr.
Bird gave a big tea to the visiting women
before the finals.
The British Parliamentary visitors are
invited to the Zoo, on Wednesday afternoon,
September 24. President Dr. Charles Ryan
and his wife will receive the guests, overawe
the hyena and monkey, and superintend the
refreshments.
The final important charity dance of the
winter will be the Newsboys’ Ball. These
brisk trading kids have always been pat-
ronised by vice-royalty, and the Best People
attend their benefit sprees in myriads. The
best and cleanest of the craft always attend
the gaudy show and present bouquets; but
thev are commanded to take off their hob-
nailed shoes before they trespass on the
smooth Town Hall floor. The function will
happen in October: meanwhile, befeathered
committee meetings are filling the upper
rooms of the Town Hall with shrill discus-
sion. After the first week or so of October
has gone, the Best People will have time
for nothing but horse-races and dress. In
November. Faith and Hope (as regards
puntings) put poor Charity into the back-
ground.
The Lilydale Hunt Club had its race
meeting at Moonee Valley, last week. G.-G.
Denman went out unofficially and saw his
gentleman rider, Highett, on Noogah. win
the Hunt Club trophy for brewer Mc-
Cracken. Mrs. Arthur Syme brought
her two girls up from Lilydale. One
daughter wore a wreath of roses for a hat,
and looked pretty enough to eat. Mrs.
Norman Fenner supported a feather like an
oriflamme, and Milnes. Millers and
McCrackens competed brilliantly in the
Best-Dressed Ilardicam
An unusual and finely dramatic feature
in connection with a Melbourne social dis-
turbance that culminated in divorce was
withheld from the public. The accusing
wife and the very fair co-re. were excellent
friends up to the point where the domestic
pot boiled over. Only the day before they
had foregathered for four o’clock tea at a
popular Collins-street feedery, and on that
occasion May, the wife, opened wide her
heart to Clara, and confided to her that she
had succeeded in entirely reforming John.
“You know he was something of a rake
before our marriage. Well, dear, he is now
as circumspect as a man can be. He lives
for me alone. He is content with our simple
home life, and his great love for me will
always keep him straight. I’m certain.”
Then followed a long, confiding, heart-to-
heart talk on the influence for good a true
woman can exert upon the life of a bad
man. Revelations came within 24 hours,
and now May admits that nothing in con-
nection with her divorce inspires so poignant
a bitterness as recollection of that tea-table
confidence.
The daughter of Hat Mills Kozminsky is
receiving congratulations on her engage-
ment. The fiance is Dr. Leon Jona, of the
Lister Institute, London.
The Hore-Ruthvens, who were here _ in
the time of G.-G. Dudley, are advertising
the arrival of a baby—quite an event in a
household which has hitherto been absorbed
by cats and fox terriers. The head of the
house is now stationed at the Staff College,
Quetta. India. Sister Mrs. Malcolm has
been staying in the vicinity. Mrs. Mqlcolm
is the exceedingly smart woman who swept
through Melbourne like a beautifully-dressed
meteor at the time of Kitchener’s visit.
Dodo Chenery (her mother was a lovely
Watson of the big clan of Riverina
daughters, and her aunt is the beauty of
the Chirnsides’ sons’ wives) had very strong
stage leanings, and was encouraged to per-
sist with her footlighting by Titheradge.
Dodo got her chance when the star she was
understudying fell ill, and she did so well
that she was fairly well started on the
London boards. Now, however, a man has
intervened, and she is booked for matri-
mony with Mr. Wilfred Newbury. The
fiance is a son of the late Cosmo Newbury,
of Melbourne, and manages a gold-mining
company in West Africa. The two woolly
clans are buzzing the news about. All are
delighted that “dear Dodo” is really going
to settle down and drop the grease paint.
Mrs. Harper, the widow Staughton and
the widow Wrixon lately presented the
officers of the cruiser Melbourne with a set
of flags. They firstly had an idea of making
the banners with their own fair, patriotic
hands ; but they found that charity bazaars
and so on ate up too much of their time,
so the work was passed on to experts. The
finished products were placed in a box of
mountain ash, carved decoratively with
blossoming gum branches by Miss Birch.
All the dames, followed by a man humbly
carrying the casket, boarded the grey
cruiser while it was drily in dock at Wil-
liamstown. The captain and the com-
mander, with their respective wives, were
waiting to receive them, and presently there
was a little flag-flying exhibition, which was
folffiwpd bv tea and chatter.
Eileen Payne (affectionately known as
“Bluie”), daughter of Charles Atkins, was
the star lady at Christ Church, South
Yarra, last Thursday evening. Thomas
Duncanson Kay, with three bunches of
University letters trailing after his name
like a kite-tail, was the lucky leading man.
The bride’s reverend uncle played the
clergyman’s part, and, at Goodrest, where
the wedding champagne was poured, he
proposed the health of the happy pair. Big
Major Mclnerney said complimentary
things about the parents of the bride, and
Charles Atkins answered him back, as it
were. A fine assortment of mayors was in
attendance, and the Gaud Mayor spoke for
the bridesmaids, two sisters of the bride.
“Chips” Adamson, of Brighton, best-manmd
the bridegroom, who paid dearly for being
popular at college. A batch of Scotch stu-
dents carried him on their shoulders from
his dressing-room to the waiting taxi, and
the new wife had to wander after him by
herself through the storm of confetti.
Eleanor Cameron journeyed to Sydney
last week to heln the Liberal cause. The
smiling lady will be missed hereabouts,
though she wasn’t quite Tory enough to
suit some of our most expensive Wimmen’s
Leaeners. Eleanor prides herself on being
“Liberal or nothing.” And she certainly is
something.
Aida Rebottaro became Mrs. Gargurevich
last week. The knotting was done at St.
Francis’s, in the presence of a family party.
The Rebottaro women are among the
frockiest in Melbourne, so the gathering had
seme of the glory of a draper’s spring show.
The bridegroom is assistant secretary of the
Victoria Amateur Turf Club, and there was
a distinct sporting flavor about the names
on the presents. The pair hurried off to
Adelaide for the honeymoon.
Rose Seaton, quite an identity now on the
Repertory Theatre stage, is giving a show
of her own, in October. She will have the
assistance of Miss Hallenstein, a hard-
working amateur of this city. Miss Seaton
is putting on trimmed recitals of old-time
plays.
Miss Thea Proctor has descended on Mel-
bourne, from Sydney, with an armful of
exquisite fans she has painted. They will
be on view at Gill’s, Centreway, for a fort-
night from Thursday, September 18. Miss
Proctor belongs artistically to Sydney, but
has been working for several years with
George Lambert, in London.
The Sisters of Charity, who manage St.
Vincent’s Hospital, have done handsomely
out of their, last appeal. The sum of
£6040 looks well by itself; and the simple
fact of having it entitles the hospital to a
special Government grant of £2OOO.
The Repertory Theatre Club is much con-
cerned about Shaw and his views. Lawyer
Morris Phillips recently discoursed, in the
Austral Salon, on Shaw’s view of women.
Some of the Club members really listened,
and others dropped into a sort of thought-
ful trance, becoming animated again about
coffee time. Phillips’ view of Shaw’s view
is that Shaw puts women on a high plane.
“He laughs at men but never laughs at
women,” says Phillins. Certainly Shaw
never absolutely guffaws at women ; but he
contrives to make the Sex look absurd —
and women can’t bear that. No woman can
be quite convinced that she ever deserves to
be satirised, and, if she were convinced, she
would hate the satiriser none the less.
Last Thursday’s revival of “Tales of Hoff-
mann,” the Quinlan goods which always draw
a crowd, introduced a Melbourne girl, Eda
Bennie, as the doll that snared the affections
of the impressionable German youth. Eda
left for foreign parts some years ago to have
her voice boiled and polished, and was for a
time with Marchesi. As Olympia, she soon
got over her nervousness, and her pleasing,
although by no means robust, voice was
heard to advantage in the “Song of My
Heart” number; while her comedy business
was capital. A good barrow-load of sweet-
smelling vegetables rewarded the lady, who
took her call with nervous reluctance.
By the way, Edna Thornton, who used to
dress the giddy Guilietta in yellow, now
wheedles Hoffmann into making her a present
of his immortal soul in a noisy green gown,
distressingly proper as regards cut. The
green outfit, coming into collision with her
Titian wig, causes a sustained crash, that
drowns the softer passages of the barcarolle.
From my Adelaide correspondent:—
The Children’s Hospital was forcibly dragged sev-
eral paces farther from the precipice of insolvency
last week, the motive power being a fancy-dress revel
for infants. Mr. and Mrs. Correll were the organisers.
It began at eight o’clock, and about 200 children
were there, ranging from small atoms four years
old or so to large, boisterous and wolfishly-hungry
boys and girls. There were Scotchmen and Dutchmen
and Japs, small policemen, some diminutive Oscar
Asches and Lily Bray tons, and an excellent Tiny
Town set. Also, I regret to state, there were a few
unfortunates in the velvet suits and golden curls of
the dreadful Fauntleroy lad. I wonder if the parents
who force their innocent offspring into these banal
garments appreciate, however dimly, the wrong they
are inflicting on their dear ones. Presumably not.
After all, human nature is rarely all bad. Ladv
Bosanquet was there in pale blue, with a red-cloaked
daughter. Likewise Mrs. Mayoress Bonython, in
smartly-designed black.
The Lady Victoria Buxton Girls’ Club held its
annual meeting during the week. The Dean of
Adelaide was in the chair, and Lady Bosanquet read
the report. Finally,there was tea, and nothing but
the imminent possibility of a collection being taken
up prevented the excitement from rising to the pitch
of delirium and straining the self-control of the com-
pany to bursting point. Amongst those present were
Mrs. McCulloch, Mr. and Mrs. Pope, Mrs. Water-
house, Miss Pe.icock and her sister, Mrs. R. Rvmill
Mrs. VV. B. Wilkinson, Mrs. Howard Davenport ami
the hon. secretaries, Mrs. Charles Todd and
Hornabrook. ®
Dudley, the second of the Charles Angas sons has
gone back to England. Mrs. Ryder, the only Aneas
daughter, arrives soon with her two babies. Thev
are to be settled with their grandparents at Lindsa'v
Park, while Mrs. Ryder goes to India and joins her
husband.
The Governor was at Morphettville on Tuesday
accompanied by a daughter and guarded with splendid
zeal by a brace of aides. Chi-Chi gave his backers
a dividend of £22 9s. Otherwise there were no
sensations.
Glenelg’s Mayoral Rail was the usual howling

with touches of lace and Asiatic embroidery, ami
Lady Bosanquet’s gown of Limerick lace had reliev-
ing touches of black. Her daughter was in softlv-
toned peach-color. Mrs. Lavington Bonython wore
pink moire—veiled with white lace and black ninon
Among the representatives of our old Glenelg nobilitv
present were Mrs. Charles Irwin, Mrs. Soward anil

SUNDAY-SCHOOL TEACHER : “In olden Scrip-
tural times boys were given names because

“Lagani-Namo”: Until recently every
European menage in Papua depended entirely on its
“shooting-boy” for everything in the way of fresh
meat. Wallaby, wild pig, cassowary, ducks and Goura
pigeons are fortunately to be had for the wanting
almost anywhere in the country. I dropped in on a
friend inland once, and his “shooting-bov” and several
others were sent out to get our dinner. An hour
later they staggered in with a twenty-five foot python
for the larder. The remarks that the boss sent hurt-
ling through space must have resuscitated the defunct
“gi-gi,” and he must have swallowed his captors, for.
when the atmosphere cleared again, neither was to be
seen. Judging by the noisy revelry over at the
natives’ quarters that night I think the long piece of
food may have somehow found its way there. As
for us, we had a meal of the usual old galvanized bull.
“Nuki": I was returning-officer at Te
Waimahana for last Maori election, and had two
strenuous days of it. The Maori votes by declara-
tion, and frequently changes his mind at the poll, or
comes back later and wants to vote for the other
man. When he comes to the booth he comes for the
day, and usually all the family come with him, and
if the election lasts for two days they stay the night.
On my first day an old lady roved in about 12
o’clock and inquired my luncheon hour. I told her
returning-offieers had no time to eat anything. She
nodded pleasantly, and returned to the camp out-
side. I noticed that the booth was very silent for
a time and minus all voters, when in walked a
procession—old lady, carrying cakes; her husband, a
teapot; little boy, in charge of bread and butter;
another little boy', with sugar ; two small girls, bear-
ing milk and tin of jam ; another wahine, with plates,
etc. 1 was escorted to the feast with ceremony, and
the booth was closed. Outside I heard a party of
voters arriving, and then the old lady’s voice: “Te
pakeha take te kai. Taihoa te Maori.’’ They
taihoaed all right, and not until one o’clock did my
old lady re-open the door. The voters sat round out-
side and waited with the greatest amiability. I’d
like to see the pakeha do it.
* * * ♦
An un named special correspondent of the
English Westminster Gazette (26/7/’l3) gives a
long description of the utterly different conditions
Australia may offer: Irrigated area, cattle-station,
priokly-pear belt, and so on. His great point is that
Australians talk of things as all rose-colored, and so
get the easy, enthusiastic person we don’t need in-
stead of the real grafter that we do. “1 firmly
believe that success is possible in every State in Aus-
tralia, even to the settler with only a few hundred
pounds; but success is not easily won. All the
agricultural banks in the world will not enable an
easy-going man to succeed. The sine qua non is
Character, and the less capital a man has the more
character he needs. My first word and my last is
that 1 am convinced that Australia will not gtt the
right sort of immigrant until it advertises the ditti
cutties as well as the opportunities of life on the
land, and ceases to paint everything rose-color.” His
advice to the new-comer is twofold. If with money,
buy land already proved, and partly developed; if
without, work in the country for a year or two
before committing himself to anything. And, any
way, “he must make up his mind to work harder
than he has ever worked before.”
* * • *
“('hang": Will back the Western Divi-
sion native of Papua to get a larger and more varied
assortment of food into his pantry than any other
human being'. A short while ago, in
company with some natives, amongst
whom was a Bamu River boy, 1 jaunted
into the hills, taking victuals for the
journey. From time to time the larder
was supplemented by birds, beasts and
reptiles, of which there are plenty in the
hills, and the boys were given almost a
free hand with the tucker, being allowed
to help themselves. The Bamu boy
always took in rice more than double the
amount any of the others did, and also
received his due proportion of meat, bis-
cuits, etc. Also, I found that whenever
a bird was killed which was not consid-
ered fit for eating, Bamu would bag it
for future use. Every evening meal, too,
Bamu used to keep his eye skinned to
see where the remains of my meal would
be thrown, so that he could make ends
meet, in a manner of speaking. When-
ever a snake or lizard was killed, Bamu
secured him and stowed him away with
his rice. One day a 12ft. python was
killed. Bamu got him, too, and ate him
—bones and all—the same evening. On
the return journey I tried him out by
giving him a mat of rice for himself to
eat. He finished it in two days, and
then came and told me he was hungry.
This about broke me up, so when I
reached the Government station I bought him two
herds of dogs, so that he could have a square feed
for once. When 1 left, he was buried to his chin in
bones.
“Machete”: The Papuan Government
Gazette is not more interesting outwardly than
others of its kind. Occasionally, however, there is a
story, perhaps a tragedy, hanging to some item or
other in its pages. In the notices of Intestate
Estates a month or so ago, appeared the item: “Bi.
Native woman. Samarai. 16/9.” The “16/9” was
the value of Bi’s estate. Not so many years ago.
when the Milne Bay rush was giving fair returns. Bi
was Lady of the Manor for a storekeeper on the
field. Gold was fairly plentiful, and so was poison-
ous liquor. One night the storekeeper was on a
spree with several of the diggers, who came and
went during the evening. Next morning his store
looked like a shambles. He had been beaten to a
jelly with his own pick handle; and Bi was also
badly knocked about. When she recovered, she
accused two local diggers of the murder, and told a
fearful tale of butchery. No corroboration could be
got, however, and, though the accused were arraigned,
they could not be convicted. The authorities felt it
was up to them, though, so they charged poor Bi
with perjury, and gave her a thumping sentence
in Samarai gaol, where she died. She had a little
hand-sewing machine, on which she did odd jobs.
Hence the intestate estate of 16s. 9d. The two
accused have long left Papua, and now that Bi has
followed her quondam lord and master to the Shades,
it looks as if the matter won’t be sifted out until
the Great Bang-Tail Muster.
“Pick": Spencer, Gillan. and “A.1,.P>.“
(B. 28/8/’l3) notwithstanding, 1 still assert that,
except when drawn together by the scent of a feast
or a family wrangle, the dingo is an animal inclined
to solitude. At Slapuo cattle station, in the Jingera
country (N.S.W.), I once saw the manager secure 13
dingoes by trickling strychnine into the running
blood of a wild horse he had shot. We watched 11
of those dogs trot up, and they came singly and at
intervals. Had “A.L.8.” seen them carousing around
the corpse, he would have made his affidavit that
they were hunting in a pack. And he would have
been wrong. Regarding the ability of the warragul
to scale dog-proof fences, as stated by “A.L.8.,” I
have never waited long enough on a fence to see
whether a wild dog climbed it or not, but i know
that the sheep-men of Monaro have had no trouble
from the wild dogs of the Nunnick forest since it
was cut off by a rabbit-proof fence with barbed
wire on top.
* * * *
A member of the British Government has
introduced a Bill to prohibit the importation of the
plumage and skins of wild birds. The United States
had previously taken action in the same direction,
and even in Europe, where the “latest fashions"
originally come from, the women who trim themselves
with portions of deceased fowls of the air are pretty
certain to be left presently without, a feather to fly
around with. Why (lie Customs departments should
be dragged into this movement, for the preservation
of handsome birds the Lord only knows. If Australia
wants to join in (lie movement the easiest way of
doing so will lie to extend the operations of I tie
belated hatpin regulation to the feathered adorn
merit of hats. If a woman can be prosecuted for not
“guarding” her ornamental hatpin in the interests
of human public safety there is no special reason why
she shouldn’t be summoned in the interests of gaudy
bird-life for wearing wings and tails. Men are-fined
for shooting protected birds, which is a difficult
offence to deal with. Why not punish their women
kind for flaunting the dead remains of protected
birds in the streets and theatres?

There is a tiny centre in Gippsland. given
over largely to the grand native industry of ’possum
skinning, which is glorified with the name Anno-
domini. One night a few weeks back a meeting of
the local debating society was broken up through a
bitter dispute over the origin of the name. The large
majority held that Annodomini was an aboriginal
word. A fierce minority insisted that it was a cor-
ruption of the pet name of one Annie the Demon, who
ran a grog shanty when the alluvial was being wbrked
at Pie Creek. There were three fights, but no de-
finite agreement.

LONGING.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
1 hate the screeching trams that screech like demons
Babel-led ;
1 hate the turmoil of it all where Mirth and Madness
wed—
It fills my brain with ghastly thoughts, and loads my
heart with lead.
I hale the city's surge and shame, its dregs of all the
earth,
Its humor that is Tragedy, its pathos that is Mirth,
Its Pharisees and platitudes, its faiths of little worth.
1 want, O God, the Open Life, the wideness of the
plains ;
I want to ride across the land with loosely-dai gled
reins—-
Out, out across the verdant vast, where Silence yet
remains.
1 want, O God, but leave, to walk where neither
dross nor din
Encircles me, nor creeds and castes, relentless, hem
me in,
And where no lamp-lit street parades each night its
scarlet sin.
1 want, O God, surcease of strain from counter,
press and loom.
Oh ! lead me where the dreams I dreamed might find
fruition-room
Out somewhere in a valley where the nodding blue-
bells bloom.
Out somewhere in a valley where the roads are clean
and long,
And where the magpies fill the air with sweetness of
their song
(lot where the legimental gums are half a million
strong.
1 want, O God, to dally where the anodyne of ruth
Kails like a benediction on the after-pain of youth -
I want, O God, to wander in the Commonwealth of
Truth 1
N.S.W.
K. J. CASSIDY.
