“Lagani-Namo”: The natives occupying
the foreshores around the Gulf of Papua have lived
in dread of the “Kuku-Kukus” from time imme-
morial. The latter, a nomadic, marauding people
inhabiting the hinterlands, are accredited with super-
natural powers. It is believed of them that they
can make themselves invisible, can kill with a
glance, can transform their bodies to those of pig
or cassowary, are able to see as well in darkness as
in light, can travel through impenetrable scrub
without disturbing a leaf, and so on. “S’pose white
man he shoot Kuku-Kuku, bullets he can’t do” (mean-
ing that they’re invulnerable). A prospector got
close enough to see a few of them, and they put an
arrow through him by way of certificate for the
achievement, but he brought away some interesting
information. According to his observations, they
are lighter hued than any other known Papuans.
They paint their faces with vivid ochres, and with
the exception of a small tuft on the poll, shave all
their hair. Both sexes wear kilts of ramie strings,
but long cloaks of beaten bark, looped over the fore-
head and hanging low behind, are also common co
both sexes. For ornament, women have cane-plaited
rings around the arms, neck and waist. The males
wear a boar’s tusk through the septum of the nose,
and varied decorations of thigh bones. Weapons are
spears, bows and arrows, wooden swords and stone
clubs, with shields of light, tough wood bound with
rattan. The dead are wrapped in leaves and held
upright by sticks. No cooking utensils were seen,
other than sections of bamboo in which green stuff
was being boiled. Probably everything is cooked on
hot stones. Sugarcane, sweet potatoes, bananas,
yams and tobacco are cultivated. Pigs and dogs
are domesticated, and always accompany their owners.
Wide streams are spanned with suspension bridges
built entirely of cane, and dams with movable gates
are thrown across streams for fishing purposes. No
big villages have been seen. In such as there are,
the huts are quadrangular, with either conical or
gable roofs, and made of the leaves of the sago palm.
* * * *
“Celt”: It was just after Long Valley and
before the Criffel rush broke out that, to keep things
moving, we took a contract fencing on Timaru
(M.L.). There was no word then of Antarctic ex-
ploration, but the Timaru merinoes were keen to
move in the direction of Mount Gook, and the man-
ager reckoned a fence along the Manuka Spur would
act as a deterrent. Feed was scarce near the snow-
line, and the paclchorses made heavy work of shifting
chaff along the frozen hillsides. Fritz could swing
a shovel with the best, but where horses were con-
cerned, it seemed safer that I should do the leading.
The first horse got across safely, but a projecting
point of rock made all the difference to the second.
Swung outwards, he missed his footing, and after
that there was no hesitation or delay. He reached
the creek boulders, 400 ft. below, and the thud of
his arrival cracked the silence of the hills. Then
out of the painful stillness, and across the snow-
grass and the heather, came the quavering Teutonic
call: “Vos—vos he hurt?”

They’re just the same in God’s Own
Country. A North Island school inspector, in his
annual report, mentions that one of his school com-
mittees, by a majority vote, lately decided against a
proposal to establish a school library “because it
might interefere with the milking of the cows.”

THE DREADNOUGHT: " H ' m ! They were a

“Ginger”: The carrying of an injured
bush or mine mate long distances (case cited by
“Gouger,” B. 7/8/’l3) in "dreadful difficulties is not
uncommon. A notable case was that of Russian Jack,
a Murchison (W.A.) celebrity. He wheeled his sick
mate 700 miles into Cue Hospital on a wheelbarrow,
doing the trip in a month. The exploit hit the Cue
people fair on the solar plexus and they wanted to
make a fuss over him. The battler, however, said he
was in a hurry to get back and have a “weight” or
two from Dead Bird Gully before things petered out
there. Next morning he got between the shafts of
the barrow and lit out on his 700-mile trail back to
camp. If the Czar has many soldiers like Russian
Jack the next enemy who tries to take Moscow will
be sorry for himself.

“T.H.M.” : Was working at a M.L. saw-
mill a few weeks ago, on the breaking-down bench.
We were cutting a big rimu, full of knot holes,
when the saws went fair through the middle of a
hive of bees. Three of us flew for the virgin bush
so rapidly that we heard only the echo of the boss’s
yell as he urged us to take no notice of the useless
things. Ten seconds later something like a rocket

“Macko”: Sam was a puny squib, yet
lived always in the belief that his strenuous Sandow
exertions had expanded his chicken chest marvel-
lously. He was also a horrible liar, and his affaires
du coeur (illiterately related by himself) were much
scoffed at in the smoke-room of the boat on which
I was returning from Samoa. Sam was a bad sailor,
and I, being the only medico on board, used to look
after him when he took to his bunk. One hot day
as he lay there, with his pyjama coat well open, I
noticed a tiny blue spot on his chest. “What’s that,
Sam?” I inquired. “Oh, that spot,” said Sam.
“Oh, that’s a girl’s name.” “A girl’s name?” I
asked. “Is it a joke? Does her name happen to be
Dot?” “Certainly not,” answered Sam, the un-
truther. “Yer see, I’ve been livin’ in Samor fer
some time, and when a little Samoan girl gets shook
on yer shape, she just tattoos her name on yer.
But I’ve got ter inflate me chest before ye can read
it with the nood optic. Her name’s Elizabeth.”
“P. Jeff Wallace” : Some 40 years ago
there was a fairly numerous tribe of aboriginals in
the Burragorang Valley (N.S.W.). They owed alle-
giance to a sovereign called Solomon, who in early
life had been annexed by a local sett sr and taught
to read and write a little. Solomon always carried
with him a well-thumbed prayer-book and a few
sheets of “copy paper,” and was proud of being the
author of several “budgeree corroborees” written in
approved Anglo-abo. When I struck Solomon’s camp
at the Oaks (a few miles from Picton) in 1862, I
had a “cobbawn yabber” with him, and found him a
very interesting individual. With pride he produced
from a little koala-skin bag, for my inspection and
criticism, his latest literary effort—a eorroboree
written on the soiled leaf of a school exercise book.
It consisted of about five “verses,” each one ending
with the following refrain: —
Mister Cordoo, Mister Cordoo,
Gib plenty tobacco and white money, too,
Budgeree you, Mister Cordoo—
Wuh ! Wuh 1 Wurra —man —-yuh 1
The singing of Solomon’s composition by about 30 of
his subjects was something to dream about. It had
reference to Mr. Cordeaux, of Bendooley, near Ber-
rima, who had the wide reputation of being “good
to the blacks,” some of whom occasionally worked
for him, stripping bark and otherwise making them-
selves useful about the station. Solomon had another
encomiastic eorroboree, dedicated to the Wild family,
of Vanderville, who were also esteemed as “cobbawn
budgeree friends belonging to plackfeller—baal
plurry gammon!”

“Eddyson”: Crotty is a pleasing little
Gippsland township hung on a hill—a township that
is white-washed because glue is cheap, and the pi De
clay slurry from the Red Feather puddlers over-runs
the lowlands. A nice assortment of twining vines
gives it a verdant, vegetarian aspect. There is an air
of peace, also a hint of poesy, about Crotty if
you strike it in one of its sober moments. I found
it on a mild, spring day, and was delighted with its
greenness and whiteness and sweet simplicity, it
suggested one of those aerial Italian villages brood-
ing over the Mediterranean, with which I am so
familiar in the living pictures. At half-past eight
that night I walked abroad with the moon, and all
was calm and bright. The quietness suggested that
Crotty was indeed what it looked, the ghost of a
hamlet. Presently, however, I came to a structure
rather more spacious than the rest, with a formal
semi-official front. From this issued a murmur as
of innumerable bees. The murmur swelled, and
becoming interested, I sat on a stump to await de-
velopments. The murmur became a clamor, the
clamor broke into uproar, with a great battering as
if weatherboards were being beaten or butted off.
Then with a crash, the double doors broke outwards
and about 20 men fell into the road. On looking
more closely I discovered that the 20 men were
engaged in an altercation. Some were biting, some
kicking; the others were content to punch. All were
contributing to the concert; even those who were
busy biting made noises. The inhabitants of Crottv
came forth, and looked on with an air of splendid
impartiality. Unfortunately the fight spread about
the township, and I only saw sections of it at the
finish, but it was a very excellent fight conducted
with great spirit and fine party feeling, and when
the struggle was over, and the combatants assembled
in Cameron’s bar, not a man of them was without
some distinguishing disfigurement. “What did it all
mean ?” I asked a distinguished citizen. “That,
sir,” said he impressively, “was a meeting of the
district Mutual Improvement Association.”

“Rex” : Is there any particular reason (ex-
cepting to annoy a person cursed with idle curi-
osity) why there should be such extreme color-differ-
ence in the eyes of Australian crows? Lately I
have been employed ploughing on a mallee farm,
accompanied daily by a flock of the birds gorging
grubs and worms from the up-turned soil. The bulk
of them (the crows) have white eyes, while the
optics of the remainder are nearly black. This is
not peculiar to any one part of Australia. Various
observant blokes and even scientists have noticed it,
but, so far as I know, none of them have provided
an explanation. Which leaves a great chance for
whatstheirname to rush in where thingumbob fears
to tread.
“Tekelo”: The white man got an extra
share of his burden when dysentery made its appear-
ance on Gilorua estate, Papua. The conditions were
ideal for the scourge to spread rapidly. The season
was dry and hot, and the only water supply a puny,
sluggish creek. From this a hundred boys took
their drink, and in it they washed. The isolated hos-
pital was simply a fly thrown over a ridge pole,
finishing within 18in. of the ground. Both ends
were open to allow any breeze that might be stir-
ring to blow over the fevered sufferers. A per-
manent structure was impossible, because the boys
refused to stay in a place where Death had visited,
so the establishment had to be frequently removed.
The sufferers lay nakedly on beds of kura-kura grass
strewn on the ground. Sometimes there were as
many as 20 of them. At the foot of each bed a
small hole was dug, beside which stood a tin of lime
for antiseptic purposes. Many of the patients,
through superstition, refused diet. A favorite dodge
was to empty the food into the bed and lie on it.
Some, just on the road to convalescence, suddenly
saw “devils” or visions, and bolted into the scrub.
These cases helped the death-rate. No boy died who
reported early and who kept his bed. A constant
watch had to be kept on the healthy boys.
They had wild ideas about diet. Oppor-
tunities were taken to hurl fat, green lizards, bush
rats, juicy cassowary and other titbits into the
sick camp. Often, at 2 a.m. or thereabouts, I
would be attracted by a bright fire hospitalwards,
and, sneaking over, I would surprise a weird group
of naked skeletons cooking the forbidden tucker. Is
it any wonder that, out of 60 boys that were at-
tacked, 20 lie buried in the scrub?

“Hock”: I was present at a scrap, one
moonlight night, between a wild and a tame slut
that beat anything in dog warfare I'd ever seen.
Jealousy on the part of the domestic flea-breeder
was the cause of the disturbance. Her mate, a fine
big kangaroo dog, had been away on his usual spring-
time jaunt and brought home her wild ladyship wit 1
him. That night there was the mother of all dog-
fights in the camp between the new and the old love.
They stuck to it until the warrigal lady got a sig t
or a sniff of me, when the hussy bolted. I examine
her opponent in the morning, and she bore the ap-
pearance of having been cut and slashed about wi i
a butcher’s knife. I poisoned the wild bitch a nig
or two later, and she had scarcely a mark on er.
I’ve had a lot of experience of the dingo, and tan
confidently contradict those who say the beast is a
coward. It is true that he promptly scoots from a
man with a weapon, but that does not prove him 0
be a cocktail by comparison with other wild annnu s.
It merely shows that he follows their almost unner
sal example.
THE RECONCILIATION.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Arrangements have been completed for a 20-rounds
match between Sam Langford and Jack Johnson, to
take place at the Cirque de Paris. It looks as though
a reconciliation had been effected between Johnson
and Langford, formerly the bitterest of opponents.
— Melb. paper.
They quarrelled—why, I quite forget—
And drifted far apart;
Each had his colored brother “set,”
Cold hatred filled each heart;
Sam would not liquor up with Jack,
Jack would not drink with Sam ;
Each on the other turned his back
In church, or pub, or tram.
Their bitter hate
Nought could abate —
Not e’en the prospect of a gate 1
But now, it seems, sweet amity,
And friendship’s priceless boon,
And faith and love and charity,
Exist ’tween coon and coon 1
Sam is not now by Jack reviled,
For brotherhood (or cash)
Has triumphed. Being reconciled,
The two will meet to bash,
And wallops rain
With might and main,
To prove that they are friends again 1

“Harrison O.”: What is “Billy T” driving at
when he alleges (P.P. 21/8/’l3) that “it really looks
as though old-fashioned melodrama were going to
pieces in Australia”? “During the last 12 months,”
says “Billy,” “public interest in the bad ass and
the good idiot, the imbecile, angrv father and the
fatuous, gentle one, has shown itself shattered” ; and
he quotes “Within the Law” and “On Our Selec-
tion” as examples of the “new” drama which have
caught the public fancy. The assumption that there
is anything specially new in either of these shows
is unwarrantable. “On Our Selection” is novel
merely through being Australian, but this class of
play is as old as “Struck Oil,” and older. Also, I
can’t detect any essential newness about “AVithin
the Law,” in which the angry, idiotic father, who
demands that his son shall give up the girl or be
cut off with a dollar, is a leading character, as are
also the good but imbecile hero, the wronged woman,
and others. The most unusual thing about the two
shows was the first-class acting, which appears to
have deluded “Billy T” into the vain belief that he
was witnessing new ideas.
“Screen”: An Australian-made biograph gives the
story of “Moondyne,” by O’Reilly, embellished with
the histrionic talent of Redgrave, Bryant and others.
O’Reilly was a Hussar, exiled to Westralia for auda-
ciously spreading the gospel of Fenianism in his
regiment. “Moondvne” was penned long before the
discovery of AVestralian gold, and only has vitality
now because it made fiction out of what was sub-
sequently fact. O’Reilly did not believe in the “non-
sense” about gold, but only took it as a good Active
base. Yet natives from the Vasse gave him the
idea, and he worked it up so well that Hargreaves
was actually sent over from N. S. AVales to prospect
for the sordid metal. He reported that there was
none, but of course he never reached the Golden Mile.
Necessity was lately the mother of a simple inven-
tion at a Brunswick (Melb.) picture “palace.” The
Lyric had leading place in the photo-play business
thereabouts until a rival establishment, fitted with a
stage for vaudeville purposes, started to mix song-
and-dance with its flickergraphy. Then, for the
moment, the honored name of the Lyric was changed
to Mud. There was sufficient platform space in front
of the back wall, where the plaster screen is fixed,
and something had to be done to convert the plat-
form into a stage. The “Fraser Folding Proscenium”
met the difficulty. It is a light structure of wood
and steel, with hinged sides which swing forward, and
are bolted to the floor. Also the two parts of the
proscenium-top, with curtain attached, swing for-
ward and fit together in the middle. Twenty sec-
onds only are required for converting the Lyric pic-
turey into a vaudevillery, and now the management
mixes the entertainment, with novel effect. AVhen a
vaudeville turn is ended the wings of the proscenium
are swung back to their original positions, the top
disappears, and the back scene, being removed, leaves
the screen exposed for film reflections as before. This
Fraser Folding Proscenium device (duly patented)
should commend itself to picture-hall proprietors
wherever it can be adopted.

“Norbell”: Referring to Beerhobrii Tree’s forth-
coming production of the Bible play, “Joseph and
bl " s . !h'e’ln'(mu a London contributor to “Poverty
Point” says it will be a more elaborate affair than
the Rev. George AValters’s olav, produced bv Rignold
in Sydney in 1895. AVell, Tree’s will have to be
very elaborate to be more so than Rignold’s. I was
in the original cast of the latter, and declare it to
have been the most carefully elaborated of all the
productions of that past master of theatrical elabor-
ation. And most certainly the story was treated
with reverence and dignity. We rehearsed “Joseph”
continuously for nearly two months, and to support
its dignity Rignold during that time ruthlessly
eliminated every line or word suggestive of humor
or levity, and inexorably blue-pencilled Whole scenes
of comedy which Mr. Walters had evolved. One
character in the prologue had to say: “Kill a kid,
and dip the coat,” etc. Rignold promptly altered
“kid” to “young of a goat,” because “kill a kid”
meant a certain laugh from the flippant gods.
Also Rignold caused carpets’ to be laid down all over
the place to deaden the stamping and shuffling, and
to bring holy calm to the theatre. Then he had
incense burnt in the “house” to help out the dim,
religious idea with unconscious suggestion. Talk
about “atmosphere ’ ! As for technical elaboration,
one scene of Itignold's play lasted 20 minutes, and
not one word was spoken. It was merely a specta-
cular blaze of sacerdotal ceremony in the Temple of
Osiris, at On. Then recall the scenic wonders and
glories of that artist, the late Brunton! It was
indeed a notable production. Rignold himself had
been very keen on the play for over a year, and on
the first night he was worked up to a pitch of ner-
vous tension that we trembling underlings had never
seen before, not even on first nights of “Henry V.”
or “Caesar.” I will not soon forget the scene where
Rignold, as the promoted Joseph, second only to
Pharaoh, received his long-lost brethren. The breth-
ren (I was one) were wild, woolly-whiskered shep-
herds from Pooncarie, on the Jordan, or the Northern
Territory of Judea, or some other part of the Never-
Never, and on our entrance some city-bred and
irreverent god softly whistled “Get yer ’air cut!”
Someone said, “Lie down!” Rignold, overwrought,
stopped dead ; then he left his throne, walked down
to the “foots,” and said: “I should say, take the
law into your own hands and pitch him out of the
theatre.” Then he went back and resumed —“Can
it be that thou art Benjamin ?” Ah, he was a
Good Australian and a Big Chief 1
“The Immature Male”: Smith was unmarried,
and, being a man who took things slowly, he did
the next worst thing, and bought a gramophone.
“It’ll be company for me,” said Smith, by way of
apology. Brown was Smith’s drinking partner, and
when Brown arrived at Smith’s lodgings, intent upon
the usual Saturday-night’s spree, he found Smith
hanging over the instrument in an ecstasy. Brown,
who was a Philistine by nature, stood it as long as
he could ; then he entreated Smith to “shut th’ dam’
thing up, an’ comandavadrink.” Smith reluctantly
switched off the music, looked wistfully at the heap
of untried records, and went. After a protracted
interval they issued joyfully from the corner pub,
and, armed with a couple of crays and a sheaf of
pigs’ trotters, started to pilot each other home.
Arrived back, Smith inveigled Brown inside, “an’
we’ll have (hie) a quiet li’P musical evening.”
After clawing their way unsteadily upstairs, and
after Brown had been proudly shown the intricacies
of the instrument, and instructed how to grind out
the tinned melodies, Smith left him in sole charge
of the concert, and fell into a peaceful slumber.
“A mos’ wonnerful (hie), wonnerful invention,”
remarked Brown, and got busy on the winder. Every-
thing went joyously until a sudden horrible change
in the music disturbed Smith’s dreams; and, as he
sat up, he beheld Brown furiously turning the
handle, and endeavoring to mince a crayfish. Smith
almost foamed at the mouth at this sacrilege. He
rose up and smote Brown on his bald head with a
pig’s trotter. “Wha-what did y’ do that for?” de-
manded the startled Brown. Then the full igno-
miny of the insult burst upon him, and he grabbed
Smith by the hair and scrubbed Smith’s face with
the remains of the Cray. Smith looked wildly
round for some more ammunition, and his eyes
shone with unholy joy as he pounced upon the pile
of gramophone records. Pa orose. a a —*■—„
10-inch projectiles, and, with a horrible grin of
triumph, caught Brown in the pit of the stomach.
Brown doubled up like a burst dirigible, and,
smartly executing a flank movement, swooped down
upon the enemy’s arsenal, and gave Smith a thick
ear with the “Anvil Chorus.” Smith retaliated
with “Abide With Me,” and got “Evervbody’s Doin’
It” in return. The now maddened Smith, driven
from his base of supplies, and wearing a sort of
helmet composed of crayfish shells and wax frag-
ments of Harry Lauder, got busv with a rocking-
chair and a full-sized Webster’s Dictionary; while
Brown tore the horn of the gramophone out by the
roots, and, casting it at the battered head of Smith,
precipitately fled. Anybody who wants to buy a
gramophone, slightly damaged, can have same, cheap,
by applying to Smith.
“Dr. Nil”: There is a new disease. It is called
cinemitis. Symptoms, being subjective, I omit. The
patient rolls the eyes mechanically from side to
side when passing along the street; struts, stares,
stops and starts at nothing. When conversing with
anyone (particularly if there be onlookers) he (or
more frequently she) mouths, mops, mows and
generally chews the atmosphere, producing one or
more separate contortions of the face for each syl-
lable. The body is thrown into melodramatic atti-
tudes and the arms and shoulders into shrugs, shakes
and shudders. The hands are carried to the breast
whenever the patient refers to himself. When he
refers to his companion there is a corresponding
indication. At other times the unfortunate smites
the air, shakes his fists over his head, and waves his
upper limbs about the four points of the compass.
Female patients cultivate a smile of gradually-
expanding sweetness. Males assume as often as
possible a semi-sardonic grin accompanied by a
slow rhythmic nodding of the head. Portentous
sighs, sidelong movements of the arms, with flapping
of the same, and stabbing actions with the index
finger are often noticed. Laughter is always accom-
panied by a holding of the sides. Modesty is in-
dicated by covering the face with the widespread
fingers of the hand and passing the glad eye
through them. The cause of this complaint is too
frequent visits to the cinematograph—especially
American films. There is no treatment. It is in-
curable. P.S.—The correct name of the ailment
is cinemania, but the other is more popular.
Clifton Clarke, newly appointed general manager
of the Mcßickards vaudeville business in Australia,
has leaped considerably since he joined the house, at
Sydney, in 1908. He was brought in as accountant
—a sort of mere corporal under ’Arry Napoleon
Rickards. Next year he was aide-de-camp to the
boss during a triumphal march on England and the
adjacent continent. He accompanied the big chief
again in 1910. In 1912, Rickards having died, and
the business been floated into a comjiany, Clarke was
ushered into the chair of the general secretary, and,
while he is still g.s. he has been given another
stripe as general manager. Curiously enough, he
was trained for the law, and browsed on its arid
surroundings for 13 years in Sydney, Melbourne and
Wellington (N.S.W.). At the last-mentioned town
he added a little variety to things in general by
acting, in his spare time, as auditor to the munici-
pal council, agricultural society, Starr Bowkett
Society, School of Arts, and swimming club ; as in-
structor at the Technical College of shorthand and
typewriting; and as correspondent to the National
Advocate, Bathurst, and Evening News, Sydney.
“8.J.H.0.”: Until the production of the big Eng-
lish “East Lynne” film a short time ago, no moving-
picture drama of any importance had been manu-
factured in the Cold Country. Now the industry
appears to have got a move on. Within a few weeks
film-pictures were made in England of “Hamlet,”
“Ivanhoe” and the Battle of Waterloo. Forbes-
Robertson, who is at present retiring from the stage
at great length, strutted for the “Hamlet” picture,
and the actors were taken to four different villages
to secure suitable backgrounds. The film cost
£lO,OOO to produce. The Waterloo scrap was stage-
managed in Cheshire, 2000 men and 1000 horses
participating in the business.

of J.C.W., Ltd.; but Tallis, Ward and Meynell will
continue to be managing directors. The co. is (mind
to spread to S’Africa and absorb all the “Wheeler
theatres” of that Dominion. “We are putting u p
a new , theatre in Melbourne, right opposite Her
Majesty’s,” said Rupert, “and we have acquired i
fine site for another theatre in Sydney.” The new
Melbourne house is to “give a better chance to the
higher comedy than has hitherto been the case in
Australian theatres.” Most Australian theatres were
designed for large, spectacular productions. The
with a view to giving Australia what the increas-
ingly-serious Bart, calls a “rollicking revue-time ”
the firm has arranged to transplant the whole of
the “Come Over Here” production from London
Opera House. The next panto, is to be “The Forty
Thieves” ; and “I’m collecting for it all the attrac-
tions I can discover in England, the Continent
America, and —and everywhere.”
“Norbell”: A recent Bulletin par. mentions that
moving-picture-man Carroll, of Bananaland, has dis-
covered “Wild Australian Cannibals” on exhibition
at Chicawgo (U.S.A.). Carroll might have discov-
ered a similar exhibit on tour in his own Queens-
land, as a side-line chiefly at agricultural shows. I
first encountered the Wild Man at Roma. He was
chained by the leg, and was reclining at the bottom
of a “pit,” constructed of half-inch pine. There was
a wire-netting screen over him, possibly to prevent
his escape, and obviously to stop admiring patrons
from pelting him with peanuts and pricklv pear.
He had a raw shin of beef beside him, and wore a
ferocious wig, a beard of tangled horsehair, and an
old bag. I noticed that whilst his chain was mas-
sive enough to tether an elephant or an M.L.C., it
was attached to his leg by a tin anklet and a piece
of string. Altogether, he was an unconvincing kind
of cannibal, and he winked at me when no one else
was looking. However, the unsophisticated West-
erners took him seriously enough, and sparred up
their sixpences. Wire-whiskered Druids from way-
out gazed. in awe, and speculated as to where they
“cot ’inn” One sunburnt relic whispered that they
“locked ’im up in Maloney’s shed at night.” Whilst
travelling from town to town I got to know this
wild man and his partner, the spruiker, in their
private capacities. As side-showmen they had tried
everything, from five-legged sheep and Indian giants
to the petrified woman and the performing flea, but
they assured me that the Wild Man was the winner.
Said the spruiker: “And, mind you, it’s no take-
down. All the banners and placards read, ‘The
Travesty Wild Man.’ And you’ve heard me spruik-
ing.” I had, and his spruik was: “Come and see the
false pretender wild man,” etc., and so da capo. So
this par. can hardly give the show away.

MESSRS. BROWN, BOYER! were among the first to
realise the inherent advantages of turbo-blowing and
compressing machinery, and in the early days devoted
a large amount of time to research and experiment in order to
P' rfect the design of this class of plant. As a result, the
machines constructed by them are not only designed along the
lines which experiment has shown to be correct, but they
embody the results ot a very wide practical experience in the
operation of turbo-blowing and compressing machinery under
all conditions of working. Up to January, 1912, they had
•nstalled over 80 machines, representing about 47,500 h.p.
Their experience has enabled them to eliminate the
features of design which have been found to affect the satisfac-
tory operation of such machines, with the result that the
turbo-blowers and compressors now constructed are flexible,
efficient and absolutely reliable in operation even under the
most severe conditions of service.

POLITICAL
POINTS
Adelaide Outer Harbor is to be lengthened
by 200 feet at the inner end, and by 800
feet at the outer end. The latter section is
to be dredged to a depth of 36ft. at low
water, a suggestion to make it 40ft. being
laid aside for the present. But the Ceramic
draws 35ft. when fully loaded, and there
are bigger things to come. Some of Aus-
tralia’s harbors are cutting things altogether
too fine.
Each political party in South Australia
has a new leader. On the Labor side John
Verran’s star is quite eclipsed—chiefly by
reason of his indiscreet tongue. If some
thoughtful friend had presented John with
a bag of profound silence on his last birth-
day he would have given him the thing that
John most wanted. Crawford Vaughan has
taken the Opposition leadership in Par-
liament, and now his younger brother “Jim”
has been elected President of the Political
Labor Organisation. They are sons of the
Government photo-lithographer, fluent
speakers, and well educated. J. H. Vaughan
worked his way up on scholarships from
Norwood State School to Prince Alfred
College and the Adelaide University. He
is a lawyer-, a member of the Leg. Council
and not 34 yet. The new President of S.A.
Liberal Union is a lawyer, too —contrary
to recent custom. A. J. McLachlan, who
takes the place left emnty by Vardon’s sud-
den death, was C. C. Kingston’s junior
nartner, and has worked up a good position
ss a commercial and constitutional lawyer.
He is without Parliamentary experience,
having had no luck at elections.
THE PREVAING BUOYANCY.
“We are,” he said, “a State in the very heyday
of a vigorous and buoyant youth.— Holman.
THE VICTIM: “ Well, I don't see anything to
skite about in landing a feller into this
predicament !"
There was nothing impressive about the
attitude of the Senators on the Chinn in-
quiry who professed to be insulted by Ice-
berg Irvine’s suggestion that their chair-
man’s examination of the glib Mr. Chinn
was a “frame-up.” De Largie had prac-
tically invited the insinuation by fluting,
previous to the event, of the pro-Chinn facts
which were going to be revealed. He was
an obvious political partisan in this inquiry
from the inception of it. As far as Irvine’s
comments on the chairman reflected on his
chosen colleagues, they were the victims of
circumstances, to say the least of it. But
why attempt a denial of the circumstances?
China has a lot of troubles on hand just
now, and in the course of one of the many
scrimmages one or two Japanese, who
wouldn't keep out of the way of the bullets,
were shot. When a strong nation falls into
a trouble like that it possibly hangs the
shooters if it can find them, and pays a few
hunured pounds in compensation. When a
weak nation meets the same sort of trouble
it is called upon to hang the offenders, to
pay more compensation than the dead men
and all their families earned since Creation,
to apologise abjectly, to salute the enemy’s
flag, and to hand over a fortress or two or a
province or something like that. It is also
warned not to do it again. Moral: Be a
strong nation, otherwise your whole Northern
Territory msv be seized because some brown
alien fell into a drain at Townsville and
broke his neck.
The Westralian Farmers’ or Country
Party had a conference the other day, and,
after pondering whether it should advocate
“the revision of the tariff downwards,” de-
cided to be frank about it and go straight
out for: “Abolition of Protection in favor
of a Revenue Tariff.” In other words, the
fool-farmer of Westralia proposes that he
shall pav as much Customs taxation as he
does to-day. but shall pay it on goods which
can’t possibly be produced in this country,
so that the tax shall not assist in adding
to the local population. Apparently the
fool-farmer’s idea is that the further away
he keeps the consumers of his wheat the
better. This year he paid 50s. a ton to
ship his product to Europe. That is to
say, he got Is. 4d. per bushel less for his
wheat than he would have done if the con-
sumer had been on the spot. Apparently he
likes getting Is. 4d. per bushel less for his
wheat than he would get if the local con-
sumption were equal to the local supply.
If it is good for the farmer to have to cart
his wheat great sea-distances to find a
market, why isn’t it good for him to cart
it great land-distances? And in that case,
whv does he howl for handy railways?
There are times when this paper feels a
trifle tired even of the Commonwealth
Bank, and thinks it might get more of a
move on it. The editor of The Bulletin
advocated a ten-shilling Government note
many years before it came into existence,
and he tries to live up to his creed by using
the article in place of the lamentable, pallid
half-sovereign. One day last week he sent
£5O of good currency down to the Common-
wealth Bank in Sydney to be changed into
ten-shilling notes, with a view to putting
them in the safe for the maintenance of the
house and the family and the dog till fur-
ther notice. The emissary learned that the
bank couldn’t supply its own notes at the
moment —presumably they were locked up
somewhere and somebody was out —but some-
thing might be done in the afternoon. Feel-
ing interested, the emissary tried elsewhere,
and the Bank of North Queensland panned
out for 60 pieces of the desired paper. Later
in the day, when the Federal institution had
got its second wind, it yielded up the other
forty. But it was a slow process, and sug-
gested that the Commonwealth Bank wasn’t
pushing its paper on J;he market with any
great vehemence.
“G.V.”: The dispute over the efficacy of
the Powellising process for preserving tim-
ber grows monotonous. While
Westralian officials are sound-
ing its loud timbrel those of
Maoriland have just reported
that the process is not a suc-
cess, and that they do not in-
tend to recommend its further
use. To people who have trav-
elled on the Manawatu line the
failure seemed to be pretty
patent long ago, thousands of
sleepers having been thrown out
after being at work only two
years. Certainly the report
adds that the powellised tim-
ber built into Government
House four years ago is sound
in every-way. But four years!
Why, timber sawn out of a
three-year-old poplar would last
much longer than that under
cover! Anyhow, so far as
Maoriland is concerned, the field
is again open for the holder of
some other process to pull the
official leg; and there is vast wire- and leg-
pulling capacity concealed about some of
these “process” people.
Seeing that the N.S.W. Government pro-
poses to start a new Superannuation Fund
for State civil servants, how about doing
something decent for those who subscribed
compulsorily to the old Fund—the one that
came to such awful grief? It was robbed
in the most heartless fashion to pay com-
pensation to men who were sacked in the
retrenchment days of George Reid and others.
When it became bankrupt, officials were
told that they might stop subscribing, and
get back, in their old age, when they left
the service, the money they had actually
paid in, with 3 per cent, simple interest —
not compound interest. Many accepted the
scandalous offer because they feared that,
if they supplied any more contributions,
they would simply be subscribing to a dead
or repudiated horse. Even men who were
transferred to the Federal service can’t get
their money back out of the old fraud of
the old Freetrade party.
A Parliamentary Handbook, compiled
under the direction of Hibbert Newton,
clerk of the Vic. Legislative Assembly, will
serve as a pocket treasury of knowledge to
the local politicians of Watt’s State. A
table of all the Vic. Ministries that ever
were is included in this handy book of
records and up-to-date departmental direc-
tory. Also the constitutions of the
two Houses are interesting, for they re-
mind the reader that the undemocratic
character of the House of Rich Landlords
begins in the fact that no man under the
age of 30 has the suffrage for it, nor any
man of any age, nor any woman what-
soever. A schoolboy can gather the dates of
the accession of every British sov. since
the Conquest from the Handbook, and the
anxiety of Leg. Councillors to retain an un-
interrupted tenure of their blessed seats
for a long term of years is explained under
the heading of “Title of ‘Honorable.’ ”
When they have been there for not less than
10 years continuously, their Gracious Maj-
esty is pleased to approve of them remaining
Honorable evermore. The little volume is
blue covered, and the color holds fast.
From the strangers’ gallery:—
Speaker Harry Morton, of the N.S.W. Assembly,
is not nearly so imposing a figure as his interesting
predecessor, Henry Willis. The latter's visage
seemed to indicate that its owner was not only
something of a lawyer (and what Willis doesn’t
know about Parliamentary law is hardly worth know-
ing), but also something of a dictator. To see
Henry deal with W. H. Wood was a joy. He was
always as placid as an oyster. Morton, on the other
hand, seems to be uncomfortable in his millinery.
When a member moves, he watches him as though
in fear of a flying water-bottle. And his jaw is not
of the Caesar-Willis species. When he is called
upon to check disorder, he doesn’t speak with the
fluent ease of Willis; he may even be found guilty
of being without visible power of pronouncing the
right word. On the whole, however, Morton is a
good Speaker, though it is doubtful whether he
would seem half so good if there hadn’t been a
better one before him to teach the House much that
it had forgotten or never knew about table manners.
It was in the days of Henry Willis that the N.S.W.
Assembly learned not to eat politics with its knife,
not to drink its public beer out of its hat, not to
put its front extremities in the trough, not to bark
during divine service, and not to deposit its feet
on the cruet at dinner-time. These were all valuable
lessons. Incidentally, it learned not to put a whole
water-melon in its mouth at once.
THE RIVAL LIFE-SAVERS.
Splintery Wood, deputy-leader of the
Opposition in N. S. Wales Assembly, has
achieved a record. He has been “removed”
by three Speakers—McCourt, Willis and
Morton, all of them taken from his own
side of the Plouse. It seems about time that
Wood was chopped up.
The State Liberal party in N.S.W. has a
leader who puts both feet in it, and a deputy
whose capacity for being ejected, even by
Speakers belonging to his own party, is
something over the odds. Within the last
few days the combustible deputy has been
ejected again, and the clumsy leader has
gone down on a censure motion once more.
Wade’s charge was, in effect, one of corrupt
land-jobbery—the sort of charge that
should be investigated before a Parlia-
mentary committee and supported by wit-
nesses on oath. The idea of hoping to get
any sane Legislature to convict on such a
matter merely on the strength of some
loose and casual speeches was too absurd
for words.
One of these days the present system of
selecting candidates by Liberal leagues and
Labor leagues will strike its well-earned
sheol. Each party is so hungry for the
“bob” which helps to keep the funds afloat
that it is ready to take practically anybody
into its League. Sooner or later, in some
electorate where things are pretty evenly
balanced, it may happen that two really
brilliant organisers will clash, and as the
result of their efforts everybody will join
both Leagues. Then Labor will bring along
an incurable drunk from some outside
town, and dress him up nicely, and keep
him sober long enough to prevent the jest
being discovered, and its block vote will
make him the selected Liberal candidate.
And at the same time Labor will find itself
saddled with an unknown but selected can-
didate, and discover when it is too late that
he has just finished his seventh term in
gaol, and that an enemy has done the deed.
In consequence of these happenings selec-
tion by leagues will fall into disfavor, and
some improved form of preferential voting
will take its place.

Panua’s only newspaper, the Times
(Port Moresby), / is working three shifts to
get an amendment of the Constitution to
allow of the election of four members in an
otherwise nominee Parliament of 10. The
barrackers for the ourelv nominee House
allege that they object to the franchise in
the interests of Brown Brother. But the
Australian in Papua is very much the same
kind of man he was before leaving Aus-
tralia, and if he’s good enough to govern
himself in a State he’s not too bad to partly
govern himself in a dependency or a Pos-
session.
A plaster bust of Charles Windeyer, an
early P.M. of Sydney, for many years occu-
pied a bracket in the Old Central Police
Court in George-street, as a memorial of a
good and upright magistrate. Before com-
ing to Sydney he had been the first reporter
to take notes in the House of Lords. When
the new police court in Liverpool-street
was built a niche in one of the court rooms
was provided for the bust. Last week the bust
was taken down and refurbished, and looks
all the brighter for it. Windeyer came to
Australia in IS2B with a view to settling
on the land, but persons of legal experience
being needed he was induced to rush into
official life. He was Mayor of Sydney, and
grandfather of the late Justice Windeyer.

The ribald person who edits London Daily
Herald permits this to appear in his paper’s
description of the Commonwealth building
ceremony:—
Arived there, Herr George Von Wettin was cheered
by the usual select mob of leading citizens, includ-
ing the one and only George Reid, Iv.C.M.G. —Knight
Commander of the Monkey and the Goat.
This seemingly is a playful reference to
George’s disposition to do monkey tricks and
act the goat in the course of his public ap-
pearances. It is a terrible thing for a man
to have to live up to his reputation as an
“amoosin’ cuss.”
APPLE DAY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
At the annual fruit-growers’ conference held in
Victoria, it was agreed that March 20 be proclaimed
Apple Day, and that the executive should interview
the Mayor of Melbourne to arrange its organisation
on the same lines as Wattle Day.
To turn loose in the city street
A squad of pretty rangers,
Attacking all the men they meet
With apples sour and apples sweet,
Suggests a heap of dangers.
The wattle mess was bad to start;
Oh, save us from the apple tart!
Good Mr. Hennessy, forbear!
Don’t touch this matter lightly!
Remember what a world of care
Resulted in that garden fair
When Eve, so spruce and sprightly,
In partners with a snake, they say,
Inaugurated Apple Day!

SPORTING
NOTIONS
The exact meaning of George Gray’s
challenge to the English pro. billiardists (he left
r red Lindrum out oi ic) was made clear by Reece’s
acceptance. Reece stipulated for three matches. He
proposed that after playing with bonzoline balls —Gray
to give 2000 in 18,000 —they should play a second
game with ivories. The man witn the highest aggre-
gate in the two matches is to have choice of halls
tor the third struggle, and if Gray gets the bonzoline
choice, he is to concede 2000 points as before, whereas
if Reece gets the choice with iyories, they will play
on level terms.
N.S.W. Bowling Association annual re-
port is a mixed sort of document which breathes of
financial success, but admits defeat in the inter-State ’
matches. The inter-State struggle was peculiar, how-
ever. Five matches were played, and N.S.W. lost
three and won two. But although the Ma State lost
the majority of the matches, it scored 475 points as
against the 429 scored against it. So that although
it went down, the N.S.W. Association wks 46 points
ahead. The worst defeat was that sustained at the
hands of South Australia, when the Crow-eaters rolled
up 103 points to the N.S.W. 94. The best win was
that over Tasmania, when Ma clawed out with 120
to 72. Victoria just won by 96 to 93. Queensland
by 80 to 79, while Westralia was convincingly, but
not overwhelmingly, defeated by 89 to 78. Nine
inter-association matches yielded four wins, three.
defeats and two unfinished games. In this case
N.S.W. scored 881 points to the 828 of the Victorian
and Queensland Associations, and so had a point
majority of 52. This season saw the Rawson Cup
pass into permanent possession of the City Club, that
lucky crowd having won it three titnes. The Rawson
Cup competition thus comes to an end. St. George
was runner up. As regards finance- —otherwise splosh
—the Association is on velvet. It started the season
with a credit balance of £lB6 14s. 9d., and winds
up with £233 18s. 9d. to the good. It proposes to
invest the money in Government debentures as a pro-
vision for its old age.
* * * *
Among the mannikins who pervade a big
Melbourne course, the stipendiary stewards are known
as “the three blind mice.” “D’ye drop?” says one
of the weeds. “The club’s put ’em there to ‘see
how they run.’ ”
Mclnsplosh of Australia blew up with a
loud bang in Britain the other day, and raised much
dust with a proposal to raise £5OOO in connection
with the next Olympiad. Exactly what the cheerful
Sydneyite was aiming at is not quite clear. At first
it looked as though he proposed to create a sort of
Imperial fund from which Imperial Olympians would
be sustained, cherished and organised. The Briton
looked at the idea with a cold and fish-like eye after
his papers had roared themselves hoarse with enthu-
siastic approbation, and remarked that as each part
of the Empire was a separate nation in the eyes of
the Olympian Immortals, Mclnsplosh had better con-
fine his activities to Australia. Whereupon Mac rose
magnificently to his feet and the occasion, and re-
marked with a loud roar that he never intended any-
thing else. Well, the idea is a good one. It’s a sad
but patent fact that a sporting success on a big
scale at the Olympiad would he a better, advertise-
ment for this country than the abolition of total
abstinence, or the production of a new Buddha, or
the discovery of another Twelve Apostles. But it is
so, and that’s an end on’t. It will take every bean
of £ 5000 to properly train and equip an all-round
Australian Olympian team; and it is worth the
money.
Summer baseball has been placed on its
feet in Sydney, and the competition will begin to
roll on October 4. Grounds have been successfully
bartered for, and there is sufficient vim behind the
new side-show to make it a serious interloper in the
domain of other summer sports. The number of
players liable to be drawn from the cricketing ranks
is particularly large, and enough to make some of
the bat and ball authorities shiver in their shoes.
Talking about cricket: Uncle Sam’s cricketers are
evidently very small fry, as the Mayne team re-
cently smote an All-American crowd to smithereens,
and won bv an innings and 47 runs. In the Aus-
tralian tally of 402, Macartney biffed 186. The
significant point is that the United States is a land
where baseball flourishes all the year round.
N.S.W. Amateur Athletic Association has
not earned any peacocks’ plumes for its head-gear with
the management of the season’s long-distance events,
which have been spread over three successive week-
ends. The 10-Miles Cross Country premiership hap-
pened on Victoria Park Racecourse; the 12-Mile
Marathon on the roadway round Manly, and the Five-
Mile on Randwick Racecourse. Could a worse pro-
gramme be devised ? No wonder there were only 30
starters. If amateur athleticism ever dies in this
country, it will be due to the bungling of the
bodies which control it.
* * * *
J. Dalgleish and H. Hayden have had some
rare tussles in the N.S.W. long-distance events thi3
year. In the 10-Mile Championship, Dalgleish had
Hayden for runner-up. In the Manly Marathon,
Hayden won, with Dalgliesh second. And in the
Five-Mile Hayden ran third.
The big road race from Murrurundi to
Newcastle, which the N.S.W. League of Wheelmen
intended to run off next month, has been abandoned
and dropped overboard. The reason for this drastic
action is given as “lack of support.” Whether it
meant lack of prize contributions by traders and
others interested in the game, or just lack of support
by wheelmen themselves, is not quite clear; but it
is strongly suspected that it was the hole in the prize
list which caused the structure to collapse. Manager
Sherwood of the Dunlop Rubber Co. had donated £25.
He has notified that the race shall be run next season,
and that he himself will take on the heavy task
of providing an attractive prize , list.
E. Childs and A. D. Fraser left Goulburn
(N.S.W.) at 7 a.m. on Monday morning to break
the motor-bike record of shr. lomin. for the run of
133 miles. At Mittagong, Fraser had to retire owing
to a smash, hut Childs, on his 3A-h.p. Continental-
shod James machine flew on alone, and
succeeded in cutting no less than 30 minutes off the
record. He ran through in 4hr. 45min. This is an
average speed of 28 miles an hour and is- regarded as
a good performance. Still, it is a remarkable thing
that a motor-bike should he only about 7-J miles an
hour better than a foot-driven jigger. That’s how
it works out according to the time put up in the
Continental Goulburn to race.
The preparations for Henley-on-Parra-
matta are being pushed on, and a big bill of fare
prepared for the afternoon, in which 11 races will
take place between 3 p.m. and 4.30. This is some
crowding, a» Wailingiord would say. Tlie only
handicap the atiair iauors under is mat all Sydney
nus nad a little too much Henley-on-Parramatta
already. It has fodnd Henley, the loud politician,
who has brooded over the Parramatta for ever so
long, a trying circumstance, and is likely to regard
the function called after him as a great weariness.
“0.K.”: Re the Cups: The least unwise
punters are waiting for the A.J.C. Spring Meeting
to give them a line of reasonable comparison between
Sydney and Melbourne gees for the coming Cups,
'iliuslar in the new season there is no saying whether
the strongest Victorian fancies—r-Sea Prince, Uncle
Sam, Andelosia, Abdieator, 1 eramorz, Sheriffmuir,
Anna Carlovna, etc., etc. —are worth haciting against
the “good things ’ of the Ma State, it is certainly
up to the three-year-olds to assert themselves in the
big handicaps and w.f.a races during the next two
months. Since Prince Foote’s days of glory there
have been no brilliant three-year-olds, and the possi-
bility of a Poseidon or Prince Eoote coming from
this season’s batch is very vague at present. Ande-
losia is believed to be the best of the young Vic-
torian crop. All going well with this fine colt up
to the first Saturday in October, he will meet Rad-
nor, Marculfus and the rest of the N.S.W. lot in the
Sydney Derby, and put their form to the test. Not
many Vic. horses are going over for the Epsom
and Metropolitan, apparently, but the few that try
their luck may suffice to indicate the probability, or
otherwise, of the Sydney trainers picking the plums
out of the Melbourne carnival pudding. Meanwhile,
all provincial tips are mere guesswork, based on
provincial form and private gallops. The value of
Sea Prince’s erratic performances, or of Uncle
Sam’s masterly victory in the Memsie Stakes,
t’other Saturday (when Audacity and Sea
Prince followed him home) cannot be gauged.
The best of the Sydney veterans might have
been equal to winning the same race as easily
as Duke Foote could have won it a year ago. The
one thing certain about Sea Prince is that he runs
like a six-year-old stallion of uncertain temper, who
is likely to do his worst, rather than his best, in a
crowded field. With Uncle Sam, on the other hand,
there is no question of his gameness, but there is
a grave doubt concerning his nerves. Given a cooler
temperament, the slashing chestnut would be every-
body’s choice for the Cups. Last year, with light-
weights up, he won the Caulfield and finished third
for the Melbourne after being much bumped and
blocked. It isn’t the extra weight that can stop the
big fellow this time. He can carry Bst. 71b. with
ease if he can be got as fit as he was in October last.
But there were no first-class three-year-olds in the
handicap competitions at that time. Some that were
moderate then may have developed into solid four-
year-olds, as in Piastre’s case. Anything may happen
to spoil the chance of even an Uncle Sam at his
improved best. And until after the Derby and
Metropolitan at Randwick nobody can say what this
season’s form really is.
The N.is.W. Rugby Union team in Maori-
land continued its disastrous career on Saturday,
when it went down to the Maoriland team in the
second test by 25 points to 13, in the presence of
9000 delighted Islanders. The match took place in
Dunedin, and though the day was fine, the ground, as
usual, was heavy. The Australians are said to have
made the better display, but the dour Dunedin Scots-
man went for points instead of appearance—and got
JERK Y'S NULLIFIED NULLA.
After surrendering to Pat Bradley , A ho.
Jerome displays his fractured hash-weapon to
his Australian brothers.
’em. Still the invaders had the better of the first
half till just before the interval, when a lucky field
goal made the Maoriland score nine to eight. After
the Athol brose in the pavilion, Australia went to
pieces in the forward division, and though the backs
put up a great struggle, the team was overwhelmed,
and with wild shouts of “Scots wha’ ha’e!” from the
audience and mixed shouts from the charging players
the Maoriland score was carried upward till it
wanted only one point to make it double that of Aus-
tralia. But there is stuck, and there it was when
the whistle blew. Still it was a bad defeat. So far,
the Australian team has done shockingly, having lost
five games out of the six played. Yet, curiously
enough, the single win was against the formidable
Taranaki crowd —always one of the toughest of Maori-
land gatherings since Maoriland history began.
On the other hand, the N.S.W. League
Rugby team seems invincible, and is stalking through
the Maori islands as it likes. So far, it has won the
whole eight matches played, and scored 272 points
to 77. It has smashed up Auckland, Taranaki, Can-
terbury and Wellington among the provinces, and an
All-Maoriland team was routed by 33 to 19. The
victim on Saturday was Wellington. Quite 7000 people
stood in the mud of the sloppy Basin Reserve and
saw an amazing thing—a first-class Maoriland pro-
vincial team ending its first half without having
scored a single point, while the enemy had kicked up
18. Nothing like it was ever viewed in Wellington
before; and an angry audience howled threats of
murder at its spanked representatives. The Maori-
land team knew that if it wanted to leave that
ground alive it had to do sorhething. It played
with frantic energy and temperate order in the second
spell, and scored exactly 18 points. But while it did
so the Australians a' l 'led another 16, and so won by
34 to 18. The progress o c this League team is unique
in the annals of Maoriland football.
* * * *
The struggle for the Soccer State Cham-
pionship still drags its weary way in and around
Sydney, although the weather is far too hot for goo' 1
football. The end is not yet. Newtown, which blew
out the shining light of Granville by two goals to
one, qualified for the final on Saturday last, but will
have to wait a fortnight before it can play, as the
match between Sydney and Annandale which was
to supply the other party to the final on Saturday,
ended in a draw —four goals each. So Sydney and
Annandale must play off their draw, and the winner
will battle with Newtown on Saturday week. If
there is another drawn game, presumably the con-
test will rage into next month.
Melbourne followers of League hoofball
have had poor luck in this year’s final games, and
on Saturday the 43,614 who stood in the rain during
the ’Wood-’Roy discussions mostly wished they had
stayed at home by the fireside and saved the £1587
thev “parted” in the aggregate. In truth, there was
little football save in the first quarter, when the two
eighteens played smartly and cleverly, ’Hood to
somewhat better purpose. Then the rain fell and
poured pretty steadily to the end. The crowd saw
little through the forest of umbrellas, and the players
slid about like skating novices. What cleverness
there was lay mostly with ’Roy, which got a six-
goal lead in the third quarter. It was hopeless to
try and overtake this advantage on such a day, and
though the Magpies reduced it slightly, the later
stages of the discussion were void of interest, and
few were sorry when the bell rang. Scores: ’Roy,
11 goals 14 behinds; ’Wood, six goals seven behinds.
This leaves ’Roy and St. Kilda to battle out the
final, and a joyous scuffle should ensue if the day he
fine, which it probably won’t be. The following is
Show week, and it is always wet at Show time.
The prelude to the big game was the Met.
Ass. final, between Leopold (South Melbourne
Juniors) and ’Wood District, a soothing, harmonious
spectacle, in which the Leos. always had a trifle the
better of the Junior Magpies. The latter ultimately
made the identical score that their seniors subse-
quently accumulated —six goals seven behinds—as
against Leopold’s 10 goals six behinds; hut the ’Wood
Juniors, as leaders in the first round, still have
another chance to see if they can do any better.
Though a few accidents have occurred,
this year s games have been comparatively courteous
and innpcuous happenings. Melbournians who saw
the Yankees play—when the U.S. fleet was here
—their curious, deliberate, weariful game, in which
the players seemed to be sprawling on the ground
over the ball most of the time, will recollect the
prominent position of an ambulance on the ground.
It wasn’t needed that day, but —last season 10
players were killed outright on U.S. football
fields, and 185 were injured, many seriously. And
that was the lowest death rate this century. The
season of 1909 was a record, 30 being killed and 216
injured In the last dozen years 217 players have
been kUlod and 2426 iniured ! What with his simple
baseball and his deadlv football, the Yank is a
curious animal in his games.
When Mayne proposed to take an Aus-
tralian cricket XI to the United States, the idea was so
new that the thing was done before people got a chance
to grasp what the new development meant. Last week
came word via London that Mavne’s team was
stranded owing to poor gates, and likely to start
for home overland, per boot. Just before the news
of the stranding came it was found that the Rev.
E. F. Waddy hnd made arrangements to take a team
to Ceylon. Then someone—this world is full of
Someone, and he is a near relative to “A Voice,”
who interferes at public meetings—asked where the
thing was going to end. If Mayne and Waddy could
go galloping off on so-called holiday jaunts and
taking the cream of the plavers with them, it meant
that sooner or later the holiday-team would become
a boodle team pure and simple. Already there seems
to be evidence that the Mayne team has become a
business proposition, for when Mr. Arnott cabled to
Mavne asking if he wanted money the reply came
back that no cash was required. The tour had been
such a success, in spite of mountainous expenses, that
another had been arranged for next year. The holiday-
team gag is not taken seriously. It was the be-
ginning of a burst of Private Enterprise in cricket,
in which the various associations of Australasia would
train players who, immediately thev became worth
watching, would jump into good places in Private
Enterprise teams going abroad ; and then after a
while we should have the Rev. E. F. Waddy running
N.S.W. cricket and Mayne doing likewise for South
Australia, with a third entrepreneur in charge of
the Victorian game. Obviously the time has come
for the axe. Out of respect for his cloth, presumably,
the N.S.W. Cricket Association didn’t use it on the
Rev. Waddy, but it gave him a backhander to go on
with. It reserved the right to tear his team to pieces
at the last moment if any of the members were
required for Australian cricket. The rev. gentleman
squealed loudly, hut, if he had only known it, he
was lucky not to have something much more drastic
to squeal about. It is safe to bet two to one his is
the last team of the kind that will get a permit.
* * * *
The half-caste Maori boxer Ruenalf, who,
some years ago, made some considerable dust, came to
an untimely end in the Bombala district of N.S.W.
A fight had been arranged, the ring properly pre-
pared and the floor padded. The local constable
acted as referee. Everything went well till near the
end of the 12th round, when Ruenalf suddenly
stopped fighting, walked to his corner and sat down.
He remarked that he didn’t feel too good, and next
moment he collapsed and rolled to the floor. He
does not appear to have received visible damage, and
as the medical evidence was that he died from
meningitis—which is inflammation of the membranes
of the brain or the spinal cord, and is usually caused
by tubercular infection —the verdict was death bv
mischance, and the men who had been lumbered in
connection with the unfortunate affair were released.
* * * *
Ilolberg, the Dane, is eminently unlucky
in his fights in Australia. Against Mehegan he had
absolutely no chance, and was practically fighting out
of his class. Then he met McCoy, of Melbourne, and
failed to get the decision. Now, in Brisbane, he has
been disqualified within half a minute of time for
hitting in “holts.” Apparently, over eagerness to
score a win caused the plucky little Dane, who in
Sydney fought a most gentlemanly scrap always, to
lose his head, and probahly he was so excited that
he did not hear referee Marre’s order to step back.
Neither Jerome nor Bradley apparently heard Scott’s
orders in Sydney the same night, and referee Scott
got one or two while struggling to tear them apart;
so perhaps the baresark spirit was very much abroad
in the land iust then. The Brisbane battle was a
very close affair. Ilolberg by his eagerness practi-
cally gave Thomas the verdict, which was otherwise
very doubtful.
* * * *
Patrick Bradley, the Unbeaten, is 27
years of age, and on Saturday night he consisted of
exactly list. lib. of thoroughly trained, hardened
meat, muscle and bone. He consisted only of material
useful in fighting. He was at the Sydney Stadium
by appointment with Dave Smith, to fight the battle
of his life. His chance was to have arrived that
night, qnd he was there to make the best of it and
rise magnificently to the occasion. But Smith was
down with tonsilitis, or something of the sort, and in
his place came the 44-vear-old Jeremiah Jerorqe, of
Queensland. The Prophet had been leading the lazy
life for a week after his hattle with Mansfield, and
had stepped into the breach at practically no notice,
untrained and unprepared, a man whom Smith had
already beaten. He weighed list. 111 b., of which at
least lOlh. was a burden and not an asset. Small
wonder men who were asked to bet against Bradley
demanded four to one on. In his fight with Mans-
field, Jerome had won easily enough, hut he had
given the Hebrew warrior many opportunities to right
cross him. and nobodv dreamed that, in his un-
prepared state, he could last more than a round or
two with the perfectly-trained, hard-hitting; clever
Pat Bradley. But Jerome is like what
didn’t sav about his wife —all things to all men.
The scrap he fought with Bradley was as different
from that with Jerome as a grindstone is from a
cheese. Bradley got the verdict, it i s troc-
he acquired it by the unusual method of tit- ’ i:t
such a walloping that the Prophet broke hi= h"
on the side wall (or perhaps it was the roof 1 of
enemy. Had Jerome been in condition Patrick n ,
ley had certainly been knocked out. Even as'it 1 '
this writer firmly believes that, had Jerome’s h!
not been injured, Pat would have gone the ll
the floor. He certainly could not have stood anon, 0 *
battering like that Jerome served out to him h,^ 1 ’
sixth round, when he was beaten to his knees Ji
glad to take nine seconds. Of course it is n '
possible that Jerome was too exhausted to lot#
such another blizzard at such a speed But 11
writer saw him after the fight, and is stromrlv f
the opinion he could have blizzarded again. M ‘ V °‘
Bradley rushed into the fight with vim Jerom ■
commenced, as usual, with elusive tactics, and in in
seconds had roused Patrick to unholy anger u
objected to being fooled with, and made an unhoiv show
of, and he went after Jeremiah with evil intent
But it took two minutes of fearsome rushing to catch
the Book of Lamentations, and when he succeed?
Bradley probably wished he hadn’t, for he a Is!
caught two terrific wallops about the head that must’
have made him see fireworks. After that for several
rounds it was a fierce scramble of infighting. Jerom
more than held his own -till the end the third
round. Then Bradley began to hold and punch and
before he could be stopped he did some damage hi
this unfair method. Jerome gave all that and a bit
more back in the fifth. But Bradley was made of
iron. He came again, and again, and’ battled grimlv
for a chance to let loose his grisly punch. He hail
no hope. When he did get a chance to let it go
Jerome wasn’t there; and when he was there his
presence was to such purpose that Bradley would
possibly as soon have had his absence. In the sixth
the now desperate Bradley came in like lightnin-'
and roughed Jerome into a sitting posture between the
ropes. Jerry sprang up and let go an awful bom-
bardment that brought the house roaring to its
feet. Bradley was smashed and driven right across
the ring. In vain he tried to hang on—he was
beaten off. He had no time to hit, as he was literally
smothered under a shower of blows. Some whizzed
up and caught him on the face; some crashed from
east and west and caught him on his ears and the
head generally; some came whirling downward and
caught him across the back of the neck; and some
crashed in straight from the shoulder, and sent him ,
staggering. He was hard as iron, but steel would
have given way under that battering, and Bradley
was beaten to his knees, where he was glad to take
nine seconds. Jerome had temporarily exhausted
himself, and, although he rattled Bradley again, the
other managed to get into a clinch, and reach the
respite of the bell. The seventh saw Jerome go
slower. He was gathering force for a fresh out-
break : and when it came Bradley could hardly hope
to weather it. He was in a dire plight. But he
still strove to attack his elusive enemy, and for a
while seemed to be getting the better of it. In the
eighth .Terrv drove in a heavy right, stepped back, and
sent the left in quick as lightning. As Bradley came
forward again to the attack Jerome stepped back
and held out his hand, and the towel was thrown
in.
It was a wonderful battle on both sides, but under
the conditions the glory of it easilv rests with Brown
Brother. One of the things the Boxing Conference, 1
which commences at the Hotel Australia on October
3, might consider is whether a boxer who has broken
a bone should have the decision given against him
because he is unable through the injury to go on.
Although Bradley fought on with a broken no^e —it
was eminently unwise to do so. And. obviously, it is
quite impossible under modern conditions for a man
to fi'riit on with a broken band. Why, therefore,
should be be penalised for what is either an Act or
Oversight of Providence?

Somebody says lie lias this alleged wisdom
to spare:—
The good rich man who gives largely to charity
does not let his left hand know what his right hand
has done. He just tells the hospital secretary in a low
voice that he need not mention his name, “unless it
is absolutely necessary.” Hence the case of a deacon
who read out in the list of subscriptions, “Anony-
mous (myself), 25.”
The wise rich man makes dead sure that his wife
has got all the things she can think of at the
moment before he starts out as a public benefactor.
Most public benefactors have been bachelors, or
widows, or widowers. Those who didn’t belong were
probably very sorry for themselves before they were
dead. Anyhow, even the loneliest benefactor must
have a few relations growling somewhere.
No woman ever had all she wanted. The fact that
she can’t think of anything else at the moment
doesn’t indicate that there is “nothing else to-day.”
The full requirements of two flappers would pro-
bably exhaust the resources of the earth.
No woman is ever satisfied. No man is ever quite
satisfied. But it is only the exceptional man who
frets and fidgets and kicks about it to the same
extent as his sister. You have to worry at him to
get his ideas for the perfection of the universe. The
woman is throwing it at you all the time.
There comes a time in the life of every husband
when he can no longer thank his wife convincingly
for a burst of indifferent song.
Every wife retains some shred of confidence in her

Concerning Papua’s latest horror-bv a ,

News comes that one Warner, a too-eager ee 'oW .
who was on a radium quest in the glst ’
of Goodenough Bay, has been killed b v jL countr . v
heathens of those parts and duly eatm a ft Kent , le
custom of the country. They waited till h’i« „ the
tion was exhausted, and then winged him I?™,™ 1 '
range with their slings. After that ong
mained but the culinary rites. Brown Broth B re '
that north-east part of Panua has been for s'! ln
time under missionary “control,” but has 1 ° ng
borne an ugly reputation. In 1903 at 1 l n ays
called Peiwa, he was so ill-advised as to attnil® 86
large party of the Armed Native Constabulary ~„a a
a white officer. The A.N.C. got into him with the
bayonet, then newly issued and much annr ( v.J„!
by the Black Guards. The resultant butchery
a howl which penetrated to Exeter Hall ' un f lsed
suited in the official being carpeted. Ho’wever re '
the A.N.C. of those days would swear S’h “
two-inch board for their white officers all mA 1
well. ’ en aed
The Goodenough cannibal is a careful and econnm
ical individual. In this Warner case, having broker
both their unfortunate captive’s arms, they tied hi
to a tree until dinner-time. As a rule,' when th
Papuan raider has captured more prisoners than h
can cat at a sitting, he takes the game home w
to a pole by the feet and hands. That portion „r
tbe bag which is dead is at once cut up if th
weather is warm), cooked and wranped in neat little
leaf-covered packets. This refreshment is eaten on
the road, if the journey is long. Before starting
both thighs of the live prisoners are carefully broken
by being held over one log while they are struck-
with another. This prevents any attempt at escape
and ensures the meat keeping fresh until the return'
ing raiders arrive at the Old Homestead.
An outstanding characteristic of the Papuan can-
nibal is the absence of all hypocrisy. When asked
why he eats human flesh, he will tell you frankly that
he eats it because he likes it. Other flesh food is
plentiful in most parts of Papua, but this does not
prevent a taste for one’s brother man. Of course all
“civilised” Papuans will avow their disapprobation
of the custom, but there is little doubt that most of
them would tackle a human steak at the first safe
opportunity. An old Northern Division constable of
the A.N.C. who, in his ante-police days, had helped
to butch' r Magistrate Green and his' party on the
Mamba Hirer, told me that young women made the
best eating. They were very tender. He added,
reminiscently, that white men were reallv not as
good as Papuans. They carried too much fat, he
said, and when you put a slice on the coals, it went
“too much s-s-s-s.” That ended his reminiscences—
for the day, at least.
THE DISAPPOINTED PRODIGAL.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
His money in riotous living spent,
Home to his father the prodigal went.
“Good-day, my son,” said the dear old dad ;
“I guess it’s a weary time you’ve had!”
“O Dad,” said the prodigal, weeping hard,
“Having sinned, 1 return to the old back-yard:
You must welcome and kiss me and make me laugh ;
Do your duty and murder the Fatted Calf.”
The veteran waggled his old bald head:
“But it can’t be did, my boy,” he said.
“We can give you mutton, or bread and lard,
But Calf is not on the menu card.”
The prodigal frowned, and flashed his eyes;
“I’ve a mind to go back to the pigs and sties.
The Fatted Calf is the recognised thing;
Despatch the butler the beast to bring.”
“I repeat that it can’t be done,” said Dad;
“You know I would give you all I had.
But they’ve confiscated that youthful cow,
To be made into lymph for the doctors now!”

Cleaning the teeth with
tooth=powders or tooth=pastes can never,
under any circumstances, preserve them
from destruction.
That follows from the simple fact that
the parts most exposed to injury the backs
of the molars, the cavities in the teeth and
the interstices between them are the very
parts which remain untouched by either
powders or pastes.
And therefore mischief, once started in
these places, advances undisturbed.
But Odol, being liquid, can penetrate
the minutest crevices, and, as it has a real
antiseptic effect, destroys all bacterial and fer-
mentation processes injurious to the teeth.

In this connection the Directors would like it to be known that Mr. T. L. Taylor
the popular and successful advertisement canvasser of the “Herald” durin«- the
past 17 years, has recently severed his connection with that paper, and” his
services have been secured by “The Mirror.”
When the contemplated issue of 150,000 “A” Shares has been subscribed, the dividend payable thereon
vyouid be £12,000 per annum, so that the balance available for dividends on the “B” Shares", and further
dividends on the “A” Shares, Reserve Fund, Sinking Fund. Depreciation Fund, Bonuses etc should be
£10.485 Bs. Should it ever be necessary to issue the reserve balance of 30.000 “A” Shares thev will be
offered to original Shareholders at par, although the market price may be higher.
„ Special attention is directed to the fact that “B” Shareholders will receive no dividends until the
“A Shareholders have received 8 per cent. The promoters receive their remuneration in “B” Shares and
it will therefore be understood that good results are confidently expected bv them.
The Directors feel that brief reference only need be made'to the large' field available for “The Mirror ”
and it should be sufficient to point out that whereas other cities in the Commonwealth support two ’if
not more, evening papers, a large and prosperous city like Melbourne has only ore.
The Directors propose to offer 50,000 “A” Shares to the public in the form of Readers’ Shares such
issue to be made during the first week of the publication of “The Mirror.”
The voting power of both classes of shares will be equal, but, if at the expiration of three vears
from the date of the first issue of “The Mirror,” the Company shall not be on a satisfactory basis as
hereinafter provided. “B” shareholders will surrender their right of equality and everv share of each
class shall then become entitled to one vote.

Memorandum of Association of The Mirror Limited.
1. The name of the Company is THE MIRROR LIMITED.
2. The registered office of the Company will be situate in Flinders Buildings. Flinders Street, in the
City of Melbourne, or such other place as the Directors of the Company may from time to time determine.
3. The objects for which the Company is established are:—
(1) To adopt and carry into effect with or without modification an agreement which has already
been prepared and is expressed .to be made between the A.E.M. Syndicate of the first part,
Mathias Farmer King of the second part, and The Mirror Ltd. of the third part, a copv
whereof has for identification been endorsed with the signature of J. H. Maddock, Wm.
Deeming, and E. Cocking, three of the subscribers thereto.
(2) To start, acquire, print, publish, and circulate, or otherwise deal with anv newspaper or
newspapers, journals, magazines, books or other literary works or publications, and generally
to carry on the business of newspaper proprietors and general publishers.
(3) To bold or promote competitions of anv description authorised bv law which mav be calcu-
lated to increase the business of the Company, or to advertise or promote the sale of am-
plification issued by it. or in which it is interested, and to give prizes in connection with
such competitions or otherwise, consisting of cash, life or other annuities, scholarships or other
terminable payments, shares or other choses in action,-gifts in kind, or any other description
of bonus or reward, or any rights, privileges or advantages which it is in the power of the
Company to confer. .
(4) To carry on all or any of the businesses of general printers, stationers, lithographers, type-
founders, stereotypers, electrotvpers, photographic printers, photo-lithographers, chromo-
lithographers, engravers, die-sinkers, bookbinders, designers, draughtsmen, paper and ink
manufacturers, booksellers, publishers, advertising, agents, engineers, and dealers in or manu-
facturers of any other articles or things of a character similar or. analogous .to the foregoing
or any of them, or connected therewith.
(5) To undertake and transact all kinds of agency or business which an ordinary individual may
legally undertake.
(6) So far as in law allowed to provide for and furnish or secure to any members or customers
of the Company, or to any subscribers to or purchasers or possessors of any publication of the
Company, or of any coupons or tickets issued with any publications of the Company, anv
chattels, conveniences, advantages, benefits, or special privileges which may seem expedient,
and either gratuitously or otherwise.
