Big Ben keeps regular hours
Big Ben is an alarm clock
that’s on the job every minute of
the day. He rings on time, he
runs on time, he stays on time.
He weighs three times as
much as the average alarm
clock —he rings three times
as loud —he lasts three times
as long.
He has a great big dial you
can easily read in the dint morn-
ing light a cheerful, deep-
toned voice that will wake you
on your sleepiest mornings —
large, strong winding keys that
are a pleasure to wind —selec-
tive alarm calls that ring just
when you want and either way
you want, steadily for five min-
utes or intermittently for ten.
He’s punctual, lasting, handsome,
and the best insurance against over-
sleep that’s ever been heard of. His
work doesn’t end with the morning
call,.he’s a good reliable time keeper
for all day use, and he’s good look-
ing enough for any surroundings.
Big Ben is made in La Salle, Illinois,
U.S.A. , by Westclox. His price is 12/6,
postage 1 1- extra. If you cannot find him in
your town, send a money order to the makers’
Australian office, Westclox, 63 Pitt Street,
Sydney. They will have one mailed to
you by the nearest jeweller.

Business, Robbery, etc.
“Porangi”: The Massey (M.L.) Government has
taken advantage of the small pox scare to adjourn
from month to month the sittings of the Native
Land Courts and Boards. Meantime, long-suffering
tradesmen (Henare applies the “tailioa” clause to
his debts with enthusiastic persistency), solicitors,
native agents, interpreters, money-lenders and the
Henares themselves are cursing the Government, the
Boards and the sinall-pox with a very large and
red-hot brand of anathema. Henare’s rents (long
overdue) are still in possession of the Board, or they
may probably be helping the good cause in some of
Ihe other departments, where the same opportunity
to handle other people’s money dops not occur.
Meanwhile, the Board has intimated that, by Octo-
ber, it may have got sufficiently over its scare to
do a little of the business it is paid for. Of course
members of the same Board mingle freely with the
brown landlords and ladies at picture shows, races,
pubs, tram-cars, and any Other old place—except
where Henare’s money has to be paid to him.
“8.J.H.0.”: “Dodge’s Geography” (which is used
extensively in Uncle Sam’s Gardamn schools) reveals
some interesting information in regard to this part
of the Sufferin’ Hemisphere. It would seem that the
eastern mountains hereabouts “receive an abundance
of rain on their coast or windward side, but the rest
of the continent, which is a great plain, is so cut off
from moisture by bordering mountains that it is
desert, or nearly desert.” No mention is made of
the fact that this desert grazes something like
92,000,000 sheep and 12,000,000 cattle, and yields
about 95,000,000 bushels of wheat annually! Also:
“The largest city is Melbourne. This city has about
nine-tenths of the commerce of the continent.” Mel-
bourne’s inward shipping is only 5,000,000 tons annu-
ally ; Sydney’s is 7,000,000, and other leading ports ac-
count for about 20,250,000. Finally: “The continent
has few good harbors.” But this blunder is excusable,
as there is no special reason why the compilers of
the book should ever have heard of Portland, Twofold
Bay, Westernport, Port Darwin, and other splendid
harbors which have been sacrificed by far-seeing
statesmen to uphold the great and glorious gospel of
Bloat.
» * * *
It was possible to do 60 years ago what cannot be
done to-day on the Murray. Captain George Johnston,
one of the pioneers of the river traffic, in the early 60’s
of last century took a pretty big trading steamer,
the Albury, right up the stream from the mouth to
Albury. Nowadays, even when the river is in flow,
steamers seldom get further than Echuca. Captain
Ritchie, an old river skipper, told the S.A. Parlia-
ment, when talking about the locking scheme the
other day, that modern river steamers can get along
with 2ft. of water at a pinch, of such light draught
are they built. A 2ft. 6in. river he seemed to
think would be quite sufficient to enable a limited trade
to be done. This shows how little is being asked
for by the advocates of navigation. Yet poor S.A.
can get no satisfaction at all from the Eastern
States, so far as Murray waters are concerned. They
say and say rightly, that irrigation must come
first You can get a substitute for river haulage;
but no artificial water has yet been discovered.
* * * *
How these drapery and clothing businesses last!
Bourke-street, Melbourne, between the G.P.O. and
Swanston-street, contains at least four big “stands ’
that originated in the golden ’so’s, and have grown
with the times. The latest to be rebuilt on palatial
lines is the Leviathan, on the “Block” corner of Swan-
ston-street. This was founded in 1853 by Lewis
Levey and Lewis Sanders, the respective progenitors
(now deceased) of the two present heads of the
firm The old men were full of tales of their early
money-making days. The “cost of living” and the
price of clothing had no reasonable limits, and shop-
keepers slept under their counters at night, with
loaded revolvers at hand, lest their ugliest customers
should return by stealth to burgle the stock. It
was a tradition of the old Leviathan Clothing Store
that a truculent bushman once rode his horse into the
shop and refused to go out until he had been served
with duds. Levey and Sanders had been nearly 30
vears in business when their corner block (originally
sold for £3O) was put up to auction, and they tried
to buv it The late J. B. Watson outbid them with
an offer of £35,000. Thirty years later they bought
it from the Watson estate for £96,000, and then it
was time for them both to die, and so the rebuild-
ing of the premises was left to their sons. The new
Leviathan building is worthy of its site. It has five
storevs and abundant breathing space. The shops and
workrooms are splendidly lighted from the windows
of the two frontages, and the flat roof is the happy
lunching ground of the toilers. And all this has
been done by civilisation, as it were. If there were
no civilisation nobody would want any of the artificial
necessaries that are sold at the Leviathan and other
places in the Bourke-street neighborhood.
A MATTER OF HOUSEHOLD BILLS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Give me a simple lettuce and a fork,
A cheerful bird to sing upon the bough,
A bright, wet promise underneath a cork,
The happy prospect of a distant cow;
Forbid the German band to make a row,
And happiness might flutter down to me
Out of the branches of the nearest tree.
I should lie all the morning on my back,
And all the afternoon upon my side,
And when the homing wheels came down the track,
And evening spread her mantle grey and wide,
My pride of leisure would not be denied,
For, resting still, I should but turn once more
And settle down the way I was before.
But when the hour of midnight deep should chime,
1 should arise, and grasp my trusty knife,
Feeling its keen, bright edge that knows to rhyme
With the forgotten law of life for life ;
And I should wander forth with slaughter rife
To slay a creditor before the dew
Of morning washed the waking world anew.
Days spent in peace and nights in laying low,
The butcher, baker, grocer and the rest
Would be a tonic for the mortal woe
That works a fever in my toiling breast;
Life would renew for me her ancient zest,
And I should find the forest shades as good
As when Maid Marion robbed with Robbing Hood.
I have exactly five-and-eightpence here,
And, judging by these bills before me laid,
It will be many and many a sunny year
Before my present creditors are paid.
How cheerfully these small accounts would fade
If 'twixt the sunset and the dawning light
I slew one trembling tradesman every night!
George, with his splendid faith in chance and beer,
Declines to worry o'er the grocer’s threat,
The butcher’s menace moves him not to fear,
The milkman’s pleading brings him no regret;
If Friday happens to be wild and wet
The wages that he carries home to me
Vary from four-and-six to twelve-and-three.
I want a holiday, but not from toil;
I long to lie beneath the greenwood shade,
To smell the freshness of the dun-less soil,
And view the sweetness of the bill-less glade;
Much opening doors has made my soul afraid ;
I long to be where I need never speak
The unkept promise of some coin next week.
But most I long for vengeance sharp and dear,
To settle urgent matters with a knife,
And thus emancipate from bondage drear
The reputable sisterhood of wife.
I know the sanctity of human life,
And knowing that, 1 long to drench in gore
The supercilious smiler at the door.
N.S.W.
MARY McCOMMONWEALTH.

ARE YOU
DEAF?
If you are deaf or going deaf, you will be glad to
hear of a simple and scientific device which restores
hearing, no matter how old you are. No mystery or
secrecy -about it; we describe it fully, and explain
just how it acts. You use spectacles as sight magni-
fiers, and in the same way you use this sound magni-
fier to hear better. Unlike spectacles, the Wilson
Common-sense Ear-Drums are invisible and com tort-
able. No one can tell you are wearing them* they
rest the ear nerves by taking; the strain off them—-
the strain of trying to hear indistinct sounds. What
a relief to have done with that tiring strain. Being
made of the softest Para rubber they cannot be felt,
even when the head rests on a pillow. 1' or the same
reason, a blow on the side of the head will not hurt
the ear. They protect the tender inner parts ot the
ear, that are so sensitive, from cold winds, dust, sud-
den and piercing sounds. „,
So that you may clearly understand how the Sound
Magnifiers restore hearing, we give an illustration ol
one as it lies in the ear. \ou will notice that the
Sound Magnifier is shaped like a trumpet, and so con-
veys even the faintest sounds right to the natural
drum Being of the softest rubber, it cannot hurt
the ear. It fits the ori-
fice of the ear with pre-
cision and comfort. Ihe
division that runs across
the mouth is to allow the
removal and insertion of
the Ear-Drum, and to
The shape of the Wilson
Ear-Drum has been chosen
after years of experience as
the best adapt-
ed to carry the
faintest sound.
Of course, if
you have lo>t
the he a r ing-
nerve com-
pletely, or were
born deaf, we
cannot pretend
to cure you. Only a miracle could
do that, in spite of some of the
absurd claims made to day. But
if you can hear the faintest sound,
do not despair. You will never
regret trying the Wilson Ear-
Drums. \Ve have letters to show
you from deaf people in Australia
and New Zealand, who have been
cured, and you can be cured, too. You may wear
the Ear-Drums day and night, and take them out
whenever you wish. Children can wear them (we
have special sizes for children from six to twelve).
Even a child can insert and remove them without
the least danger. -v •

A MARTYR FOR YEARS.
“For years I was a martyr to liver complaint,”
writes Mrs. Corlett, 112 Clyde Street, Ballarat
(Vic.). “I would get dreadful sick headaches and be
unable to eat. I consulted a doctor, but he was un-
able to do me any good. Eventually I tried Cham-
berlain’s Tablets, and derived such benefit from them
that my health is now perfect.”*

IN BANANA LAND
The formation of the new “Country
Party” in the Assembly leaves Premier Den-
ham as the head of onlv the third party, in
point of numbers, in that body. Rankin, of
the Burrum, has been elected chairman of
the new faction. The Fourth Party is
bossed by Murphy, of Croydon, who is his
own leader, follower, whip, caucus, policy-
moulder and knock-about, and keeps bis fol-
lowing in a state of truly admirable dis-
cipline.
Said Labor-Leader Ryan, supporting the
toast of “The Visitors” at the British Par-
liamentary Party function : “If the British
investor was not willing to renew our loans,
the Queensland Parliament would seriously
think about paving them off.” You have
to remember that Queensland has never bad
the. spirit to start an effective sinking-fund,
while its indebtedness swells by over two
millions per annum, to appreciate the
cynical humor of the remark.
.With the passing of T. B. Cribb, of Ips-
wich, the fourth of Philp’s Ministry of 1002
left this sphere, the others being Leahy,
O’Connell and Dalrymple. As a politician,
poor Cribb was handicapped by almost
every possible disqualification. He was
slow of speech, suffered from some vocal
impediment, and was extremely deaf into
the bargain. No hostile interjection conse-
quently ever upset his equanimity, and his
native gentleness prevented him from ever
insulting even an enemy. A kindly, honest
soul was T. B. Cribb; but what he was
doing in politics was a marvel to everybody
who understood him as well as to the bivger
crowd who heard him but couldn’t under-
stand him.
A speaker of much tonnage at the Great
Corroboree of Bishops in Brisbane remarked
cheerfully that “the Church in the Old Land
led the way to a United Kingdom; the
Church in Australia led the way to a Fede-
rated Australia!” Likewise the Church
( Anglican) dug out Sydney Harbor, and
the Church (Primitive Methodist) intro-
duced the orickly pear. The Church (Wes-
levan) will polish brass beautifully, and the
Church (Presbyterian) built the Tran«con-
tinental Railway. The Church (Cala-
thumpian) will also mend broken crockery.
But what if it didn’t do all these things?
If the Church attends to its own job well,
the Bishop needn’t trouble to claim other
marvellous performances for it. Nobody
really expects it to do outside business, any
more than one expects surgery to make un-
sinkable ships or astronomy to invent a
street tram that won’t make a racket.
The passing out of Peter Macpherson,
M.L.C., removes almost the oldest Gent. One
in practice in Queensland. Born in 1841. at
Arbroath, Scotland, he reached Sydney in
’55, and there served his apnrenticeship in
the pursuit of Costs; but within a few
months of his admission he hung out his
shingle in Brisbane. That was in ’65, and
in Brisbane he remained until the end-
something over 48 years. Twenty years ago
there was little legal work worth having
that Macpherson’s firm did not have a finger
in. But, though he always maintained a
substantial practice, in later years he lost
his long lead. Since ’Bl he has been in the
Bananaland House of Fossils, and for the
last six years was Chairman of Committees.
He was a good specimen of the most respect-
able type of lawyer, and leaves no loud, or
even audible, enemies to rejoice in his de-
parture.
TOTALLY LNADEQUATE.
SMUDKINS : “And do you think for one moment, my dear friend, that that horrib! stuff
is going to quench your thirst?”
SAM THE SOAK : “ Wot! This ere one glass! The Lord forbid !”
The Denham Government’s horizon is get-
ting still cloudier. The farmers’ reps, have
now been told by the conference of their
Association that they must not cling to Digby
or anyone else- —they must go entirely on
their own at the next election. It was
hoped by the Government that they could
be snared, and several farmers’ reps, in the
House badly wanted to be snared; but the
cockies are emphatic in their instructions.
They probably won’t be able to make much
of a third party in the next Parliament;
but they will spoil a lot of the prospects of
the Produce Agency Government.
Sunlight is death to germs ; and the germ
which breeds sectarianism finds the climate
of Northern Australia too much for it. In-
stead of having half a dozen one-horse bun
struggles this year, Croydon launched out
with a United Children’s Picnic. The com-
mittee had a Roman priest as chairman, a
Methodist minister as vice-chairman, an
Anglican rector as secretary, a Salv’army
captain and a second Roman cleric as other
members. Every kiddy attending either a
State or a Sunday school in the district got a
ticket for a free train-ride and admission to
the picnic, and 285 rolled up. The picnic
was such a huge success that a number of
toys and a cash balance of several pounds
remained in hand, and the Methodist parson
and a Roman priest were appointed to hold
the balance in trust for a similar entertain-
ment later on.
“W.L.8.”: Yet another friendly agree-
ment ; and this time the Bananalander will
get ft fair in the neck. The suppliers of
this State’s beer have come to a working
arrangement, whereby the wholesale price
of ale and stout will be “regulated.” Occu-
piers of brewery houses are ’twixt the devil
and the ocean. If they raise the price re-
tail. .they’ll lose custom, and that means
handing over the keys. If they don’t raise
the retail price, there’s bound to be some
scratching to make ends meet, and there
they are again. The yarn is that increased
labor and other costs have compelled the
cos. to take action; still I don’t know any
Queensland brewery that wasn’t making
very fair profits before this action was
taken.
Bread.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Brisbane, Thursday.—A deputation from the
Operative Bakers’ Union waited on the Minister for
Works to-day to ask his assistance in bringing about
the abolition of the night baking of bread.
Mr. Barnes, in reply, said he would like to have
the views of the masters, and he would then consider
the matter. He could not hold out the hope of legis-
lation this session. —Daily paper.
When all the trams have gone to bed
And cold roofs gleam beneath the moon,
I smell to-morrow’s baking bread.
And wish that breakfast would break
soon.
It is a sweet and hungry scent
That one they make with fire and dough ;
It hangs like some night sacrament
Above the sleening roofs and low.
The hidden baker at his toil
May sweat and curse. I only smell
The lovely whiffs of food that coil
About the night, and wish him well.
The hot, delicious scent of dough
That sweetly haunts the silent street
Cheers me immensely when I go
Towards home and bed on weary feet'.
It is a promise of new life.
And cheer and plenty for next day.
(Can you not see the keen-edged knife
Slicing the snowy food away?)
It. is a sign that Life survives,
When all the world seems dark and dead,
And Silence holds in thrall the hives
Where small bees earn this fragrant
bread.
Yet Operative Bakers would
From Night subtract the scent of dough
That cheers wayfarers, bad and good—
Would cease to make the ovens glow.
The poetry of Life goes out
Before the unions’ dull demands.
When there’s no smell of bread about .
And no man by an oven stands.
No cheer athwart the night will blow.
And hearts will be like half-baked dough.
QUEENSLAND.
HALF-A-LOAF.
QUEENSLAND GOSSIP.
Brisbane, September 13. 1913.
My Dear Tiiargomindaii,—
The British Parliamentarians and the
accumulated Bishops have been the chief
dissipations of the week. Some of the Brit-
ish people moved on to Bundaberg, some to
the Downs, and the remainder stayed in the
city and, on Monday were turned loose
upon the University lawns. There they
were induced to graze upon light refresh-
ments. Amongst the herd were D. R. Hall,
Minister for Justice in N.S.W., and Mrs.
Flail (who certainly gave points to the Bull
ladies in the matter of frocking).
On Tuesday the Bishops, accompanied by
all the domesticated curates, and trailed up
by. some prime church stuff, were rounded
up on Government House Gardens, which
dressed themselves in great style for the
occasion.
On Monday evening the Premier and
Premieress gave a party to the British poli-
ticians and their wives. Lady Emmott was
the best-dressed woman of the party. She
wore some sort of a white gown, with hand-
painted adjuncts on the skirt. (Keep these
details secret, or some of our cold-blooded
suburban dames will copy the expensive
model.)
_ The Bishop of Melanesia was to have
given an At Home this afternoon on the
stout little vessel in which he travels round
the Islands. But the Bishop was busy
fighting an old enemy (malaria), so the
guests wandered round on their solitude,
and made friends with the fuzzv-headed
crew.
A perfect orgie of garden fetes broke out
this afternoon. H. D. Maerossan, with the
aid of his wife and infant, opened one at
Wilston. Mrs. Digby Denham uncorked
another at Corinda. Archbishop Duhig
was busy with yet a third at Red Hill. All
this happened on top of a particularly viru-
lent Hospital Tag . Day. Every suburban
station was infested with coin-cadging fe-
males, and each tram terminus was swarm-
ing with (hem. Bilanderson’s “Grafters”
were busy in three motor-cars, and, stopping
at all hostels, penetrated profitablv even into
the furthermost recesses of the bar, where
the she collector could never hope to tread
in these days of Chadstigginspeckbandsniff-
heepuriah.
On Wednesday night the Exhibition looked
like a parsons’ refuge home. The Chief
and Lady Macgregor sat on the platform,
and the aide-de-camn’s uniform, and a red
nightgown, thoughtfully worn by St. Clair
Donaldson to distinguish him from the visit-
ing Bishops, were grateful spots of color.
After our Archbishop bad delivered himself
of a long address the audience was revived
with hot tea. and comforted with acquiescent
buns, administered in the Museum gardens
by various devoted ladies.
The Bishop of Itiverina opened the
Queensland Art Society’s Exhibition on
Thursday afternoon. Tlis Nibs of Riv-
erina delivered several upper cuts to
the cubist and post imnressionist puzzle
drawings that one sees about nowadays, so
P e may have dope art a good turn. Now.
if Norman Lindsay can be persuaded to
address a representative fathering of clergy
on. the modern choir erirl as an art model
we 11 call the deal square.

Two months ago Denham called three
fat and elderly citizens to the Leg. Council
~ I om-pom” Forrest; Collins, of Tamm,,

“Caber”: Destroyed by fire the other dav
the oldest building on the Darling Downs-
the bachelors’ quarters at Cecil Plains. It
must have been in the latter ’4o’s when the
place was built, as the slabs were loop-holed
for musket practice on the aggressive
aboriginal, and history lias it that the
Darling Downs abo. from 1850 onwards was
as harmless as a dyspeptic rabbit. When
Captain Henry Bmart8 mart Russell took up Cecil
I lains in 1840, the niggers were trouble-
some, and when he built himself the first
mansion it seemed a good plan to prepare
tor a siege.
In the matter of a big co. and an attempt
to dodge the stamp tax:—
The crowd which has taken Mount Morgan in hand
no doubt had the French market in its eye when it
promoted a Bill the other day to amend the law-
relating to stamp duty on scrip transfers. The idea
was that Mount Morgan (or any other co.), might
issue warrants in place of scrip, paying stamp dutv
an amount equal to three times the face value of
the share, the warrant being thereafter transferable
without paying any tax at all. In England the
stamp-tax may be compounded in this way, and
when it is intended to unload on the poor Frenchman
it invariably is compounded. For the Frenchman
doesnt like scrip. If he holds scrip and sells it,
tne paper has to be sent to London for transfer,
and that means expense and delay in payment. A
share or ‘bearer warrant” carries 'numbered coupons;
when a dividend is declared, the proper coupon is
torn off and sent to the office, and back comes the
money. Also if the holder wants to raise a loan,
the warrant doesn’t have to be transferred: the man
who holds it is the man who owns it. Of 150,000
Mount Elliott shares it is said that no less than
125,000 have been thus converted into warrants —the
preliminary to the unloading of that stock on to the
Frenchmen. The cost in England of converting a
share into a warrant, and thus compounding the
stamp-tax for all time, has for years been reckoned
absurdly small; it amounts to only Is. 6d. a share
in the case of, say, the Mount Elliott or Cobar, and
would be about is. in the case of Mount Morgan.
But under the Queensland Bill it would have been
smaller still. The stamp-tax in the Northern State
i 3 ss. per cent., and the Bill proposed that the co.
should pay three times that much, calculated on the
face value of the share ( £1), and be done with it.
On 100 shares the charge would therefore be 155.,
while in England it would be somewhere about £5.
The Mount Morgan office may be shifted to London,
as. was threatened if the Bill didn’t pass; but the co.
will probably be mighty sorry for it afterwards.

A DOMESTIC PET.
Our tabby cat won’t tackle a rat
As he used in a manner bold
He'll coil on a chair before the fires flare
Poor tabby is growing old
our tabby cat like our neighbor’s dog pat
Is the wreck of a warrior bold
He’s weary and slim, his eyes are dim,
Poor tabby is growing old.

“0.K.” : After all. a Gov.-General is a gen-
tleman of no importance to one’s memory.
Recently, owing to the cricketing achieve-
ments of his son—two or three Melbourne
journals referred to Lord Tennyson as a
former Gov.-General of Australia. Was
lie? I happen to remember that he was a
Governor of S.A. long ago, but for the life
of me I couldn’t swear to him as an ex.-
G.-G. or otherwise, although Federation is
only 13 years old and I’ve lived in Aus-
tralia all the time.
Richard Hardy, of Nabiac (N.S.AV.),
sticks to it that he will be 110 years old in
November next. Ilis father was ship’s
carpenter on Nelson’s Victory at Trafalgar ;
and Captain Hardy, who caught the
one-eyed admiral as he fell, was his
uncle —unless the old man’s yarns are
utterly obfuscated. It was of this
Captain Hardy that another ancient
mariner related a tale. Nelson fell into his
arms saying, “Hardy. I am wownded.” “Not
morshally, my lord, I hope,” said Hardy.
“Yes, morshailv. in the stummick,” said the
hero. “Kiss me, Hardy.” “I cannot, my
lord,” said Hardy; “I have been eating
onions.” * v
A. T. Saunders, one of Adelaide’s most
persistent letter-writers, lias been digging up
some facts about the brief S. Australian
career of “Bully” Hayes, the late Louis
Becke’s old buccaneering boss. Hayes, who
lobbed into Port Adelaide in a vessel that
was seized and sold from under his feet, was
married in a suburban tabernacle there to a
Mrs. Amelia Littleton, who kept a small
shop in Queenstown, which is just outside
the port. He paid the parson £lO and a
roll of grass-cloth. A few months later a
Portuguese ship, the Estrella, was seized by
Hayes’s agent for a bottomry bond, and
once more the buccaneer had a command.
That was in December, 1857. Immediately
Hayes cut another dash; he got the
Estrella’s gig manned by a crew “consisting
of the elite of Pt. Adelaide,” and won the
Ladies’ Purse at the New Year’s Day
regatta—the purse itself made, by the way,
by Governor Macdonnell’s wife. The “elite
of Pt. Adelaide” won. Of the crew only one
member is now alive —C. H. T. Connor,
in these latter days manager of the Adelaide
Milling Co. “Handsome” Hayes’s subse-
quent career in S. Aus. was brief but rapid ;
he rushed into insolvency, and then
ousl.v flitted before the Court had time to
ask him any questions.

Mr. Edison’s
8 Years’ Work
After eight years’ constant work Mr
Edison is satisfied with the Edison
Storage Battery, and not until it had
met more exacting tests in the
laboratory and more strenuous prac-
tice than the consumer will ever
demand of it, was its public
announcement permitted.
Made from iron and nickel; it totally
eliminates acid; improves upon its
initial efficiency; and combines with
this necessary factor of reliability
light, rugged strength so necessary if
electric vehicles are to become
popular.
These Batteries have been tested
under severe conditions with heavy
loads for a period of three years
which in itself should be sufficient
guarantee of their dependability.
Write us stating purposes you need
Storage Batteries for, and we will
promptly supply all particulars.

CRUST LIKE SCALES
Irritation Unbearable; Itched Fear-
fully, Hair Came Out, Was Quite
Bald. Used Cuticura Remedies,
Now Cured. HairThickerThanEver,
Mays Cottage, Gallants Lane, East Far-
leigh, Maidstone, Eng.—“l was a sufferer
from a kind of skin disease. My head came
covered with a thick crust
like scales of a fish and the
irritation was unbearable.
My head itched fearfully
and my hair came out so
that I was quite bald. I
had suffered for years with
this scalp irritation espe-
cially in the spring and
autumn when it was almost
unbearable. If I scratched
It became a running sore. The spring of
1910 it came on worse than ever. My head
■was completely covered with a thick brown
crust and I was so distressed I did not know
what to do.
“I began to use Cuticura Soap, Cuticura
Ointment and Cuticura Pills. After a few
days the irritation stopped and the crust
peeled off like scales. I continued using the
Cuticura Ointment and washing my head
with Cuticura Soap. My hair began to
grow again. In three weeks my head was
clean. The crust came off in flakes as big
and thick as a sixpence. In another three
weeks my head was thickly covered with
hair. Now lam entirely cured.” (Signed)
Mrs. E. Marsh, January 15, 1912.
Cuticura Soap and Ointment do so much
for pimples, blackheads, red, rough skins,
itching, scaly scalps, dandruff, dry, thin and
falling hair, chapped hands and shapeless
nails, that it is almost criminal not to use
them. Sold everywhere. Liberal sample of
each mailed free, with 32-p. Skin Book. Ad-
dress K. Towns & Co.. Sydney, N. S. W.

The Inky Way.
Senior-reporter H. S. Baker, of Hobart Dailt
Post, is the latest capable ink-slinger to swear off
the duty-book and proceed to turn himself into a
lawyer. He recently acquired his LL.B., and now
seeks his articles in the office of a leading Hobart
law-foundry. The local A.J.A., of which body he has
been treasurer since its inception, threw a fine
travelling rug and some excellent speeches at him
the other day.
“Gip”: It was an article in an American maga-
zine that suggested to me an easy way of making
money. A nice man sat at a barn door and ob-
served the life around him, and wrote six and a half
pages about it in a dear, two-syllabled sort of way
that made you love him. Woodchucks came hesita-
tingly ; chipmunks skulked, then hurried ; red squir-
rels jerked and snickered, and a rabbit paused
amongst the weeds. Also, a rat, a high-hole, a hawk,
two song-sparrows, a Phoebe bird, a blue bird, a
snow bird, and even crows did frightfully interest-
ing things. “That’s just luck,’’ I sneered. “But,
anyhow, I shall go out and observe, and I won’t take
my gun. I’ll take paper and pencil instead and
make some money.” I crossed a paddock and paused
to stare at a hare crouched beside a tussock. She
held her breath and watched me with an alert eye.
“Sixpence,” I remarked to her, and passed on. Be-
fore I reached the fence and crossed into the scrub
at the foot of the mountain from which I intended
making my observations, I passed eight rabbits
waiting to be shot. “Five skins to the pound, I
calculated. “One-and-eight a pound. One-and-eight
and three over. That’s two-and-eight. Two-and-
eight. Two-and-eight and the sixpence for the hare,
3s 2d.” The scrub was thicker now —gum and
wattle, and here and there a low-growing bush It
was still winter. The sun shone hotly, but the
wattles were only budding and the swallows that
vesterday besieged the woolshed and greeted me in
my garden had not yet returned. A thrush threw
out a few lovely notes, and round my head the tiny
goldfinches twittered and chirped as unafraid as
house flies. I had gone slowly and noiselessly, the
spirit of the observer who observes for lucre strong
within me. Nine infant rabbits, all of an age,
peeped from a big hole, and one by one emerged
cautiously. They watched a few seconds for some
movement on my part, then proceeded to feed. Some
nibbled at nettles, others scratched for roots, but
one was just a wee clown. He butted his fellows,
turned somersaults and looked round for applause,
then rushed for the warren in a pretty show of ter-
ror The others tolerated him, but did not J oln m
his game. Beneath in the gully, a doe tore at a dry
tussock, taking the broken pieces in her teeth and
carrying them to her nest. During this wet, mild
winter with but two snow showers and very little
frost her nursery has been full all the time—a hap-
nening hitherto unknown since bunny discovered us
10 o/ll yefrs ago. It occurs to me that I might as
well sit down and take a few observations. So I
and look

NEWS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
We 1 have harried all the highways of the North
Where swarthy people throng the narrow streets,
In ports where many junks go to and forth,
And plethoric the pulse of Asia beats;
Tilt we captured it to send across the seas,
For profit, or to startle, or amuse —
So we hurled it down the seas to the very furthest
quays,
For the printing press to turn it out as News.
We have sallied out and over to the East,
Through mighty ocean reaches locked in calm,
To seductions of barbaric dance and feast
On islands of the mangrove and the palm ;
Till we found it by the mountain or the flood,
Where Yankee pirates snare their blackguard
crews —•
fly the sluggish-moving flood, that is opiate to the
blood,
We raked it out, and sent it home as News.
We have ranged the vacant spaces of the West,
That draws the battered rover to its ways;
Where Love and Life ate but a passing jest,
And Time is but recurring nights and days.
But here we could not light upon the spoor —-
It was wary of the artifice and ruse —-
Like a ghost or shade obscure, it defied the subtlest
lure,
It was wholly incompatible with News 1 ,
And therefore have we harbored in the South,
To gather up the nerve-ends of the race,
Fearing not the searing blizzard, nor the drouth —-
The gleaner* of the whisperings of space 1
We are watchful, in a lazy sort of way,
Of many means that many nations use —
In a lazy sort of way we reflect upon the play
Of all the phantom prodigies of News!
VICTORIA.
C. G. A. COLLES.

Man’s Itching, Agonising Complaint
Soothed and Cured by Zam-Buk.
Mr. Isaac Putt, of 24 Nightingale Street, St.
Kilda, Melbourne, says: “Four years ago I was
attacked by blind piles, which were exceedingly
painful. The itching was indeed dreadful, and it
was agony to get about, while this was intensified
if I attempted to sit dow r n. I tried all the many
ointments and salves that were recommended to
me, but not one of these brought the desired relief.
“A friend who had great faith in Zam-Buk
persuaded me to try this balm. I did so, and was
so pleased with the mild, soothing and cooling bene-
fits I derived that I decided to persevere. In a
short time I realised I should soon be free from
my affliction. Zam-Buk released me from pain
and irritation, banished all congestion and inflam-
mation, and completely cured the piles, for which
I am very grateful.”

VICTORIA.
GRANT HERVEY.
On the Adavale Common.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
ADA VALE is a little place, devoid of
attractions to the sober traveller. I
put in a fortnight there for the
benefit of my horses. The common was
mastered one morning, and a mob was being
bell near my camp by a greybeard of angry
aspect, while the herdsman rede off to look
for more. My two old stagers, both in
hobbles, were among them, and I hurried
U P to catch them.
Ihe ancient objected. He informed me
that I would have to go to the yard, and
there would probably be something to pay.
My financial statement, as usual, showed
a dfficit. so I determined that my live stock
*ho|ld not be impounded.
A footman had taken up a residential
lease close to my selection. Calling on him
with much haste. I presented him with a
plug of vile tobacco, and requested him to
hurry round to the far side of that mob and
make a disturbance.
This he did. and while the ancient was
expostulating with him and turning back
the runaways, I got my property safely to
camp.
Another footman had arrived —a very big
man he was, with a cast in one eye. and a
sour expression.
‘ These are your horses if any questions
are asked,' I informed him. tying them up
where lie was boiling his billy for dinner.
"Bight,” he agreed. “Pass out the
saddles. Won’t do for them to be in your
tent.”
As I had expected, the ancient one rode
up soon after.
Are these your horses?” he asked me.
"No,” 1 answered, “they belong to that
man.”
"Where’s yours?”—with suspicion.
“Why. in your mob.” I replied, as though
surprised at such a question. “Didn't you
just stop me from catching them?”
He rode round the tethered animals and
inspected their brands.
“How long have vou been here?” he
asked the sour-visaged person.
“Just coine.”
“Which way did you come?”
“Thargomindah.”
“Along the road?”
“Along the road.”
“Strange thing I didn't see you?” with
increased suspicion and some irascibility.
‘”Tis strange,” the traveller agreed.
“Must a been lookin’ some other way.”
The old chap rode off then. I smiled
audibly as I watched him go; but my com-
panion looked as serious as a koala.
We had dinner—on separate plots, he
having declined to share my frugal meal.
Immediately afterwards he began to saddle
up my horses.
“What are you doing?” I inquired re-
spectfully.
“I’m goin’ on,” he said. “Seems too risky
to turn my nags out 'ere.”
“Your nags?” I half shrieked. “Look
here, mate, if you don’t want to get your-
self into serious trouble, you’ll drop that
silly joke right now.”
"No joke,” he said serenely, adjusting the
saddle with a practised hand. “Ken pro-
duce witnesses to prove my title to this
turnout.”
lie put on the pack-saddle. “Karn't say
as this gear's up to much,” lie remarked.
“S’pose that’s why yer give it to me.”
He was about to fix his swag on when I
made a grab at the reins. Rut he grabbed
me almost simultaneously, and ran me into
the waterhole.
The water was cold, but I came out of it
boiling. Then I rushed at him. and we fell
over the firewood. We erected unanimously,
and I remember jobbing him hard in the
mouth; but when I came to my senses he
was gone. The horses and gear had likewise
evaporated.
I sat down to meditate.
“Strike me lucky!” I said, sorrowfully,
addressing some disjointed remarks to my-
self. “I thought yen were a person of some
sense and experience.... You’re a whole
complete darn fool!”
After a while I beat round like a dog look-
ing for a scent. The tracks led townwards.
I followed, promising the thief an uncom-
fortable time in the near future, and now
ard again driving mv fist into the atmo-
sphere as I pictured the resumption of hos-
tilities.
In front of the hotel I saw my horses,
tied to the rail. The fellow was in the bar.
drinking, and relating to an arnus d crowd
what had happened to a smart joker down
on the common.
I decided, in the interests of peace, not to
interrupt him. I threw his leprous pack
into the gutter, mounted quietly and rode
away. I reckoned there would be more
hilarity when they came out on the veran-
dah. and it wouldn’t be all on the side of the
big chap either.
Reaching my camn. I oacked up with
orecinitation. mounted again, and headed
for Thargomindah. 210 miles down the
Rulloo. I never saw any more of that in-
dividual. but sometimes amid my dreams I
hear him swearing in thp d : stance.
E. S. SORENSON.
The Send-off.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
TT was my first job on the Booringa
Mail, and when I saw the paragraph
the editor sent out to the composing-
room in place of my column narrative I
knew it was my last. Thus ran the revised
version
Office-bearers and members of the Boorinjra Town
Band met last night at Smith's Royal Hotel to isiv
farewell to Mr. A. B. Jones, who has filled the posi-
tion of Band Master for many years. After partak
ing of a generous cold collation, a presentation of
a medal, suitably inscribed, was made to Mr. Jones.
The presentation was made by his Worship the
Mavor, Aid. E. T. Brown, who, as President of the
Executive Committee of the Booringa Town Band,
presided.
Messrs. F. B. Robinson, A. T. Harriss and other
representative men spoke in support of the presen-
tation, to which Mr. Jones feelingly responded.
I read that over and resigned on the spot.
Also I heard the editor tell the advt. can-
vasser that I was a Socialist, and later on I
learnt that the editor’s wife told the parson’s
wife that I was an Atheist.
And my account of the proceedings was
mild!—much milder than the tale I’ll tell
you now.

brass instruments, which weapons of misery
and destruction they stacked against the
wall while they ate. After a ham and
ginger-beer interlude the Mayor rose, brush-

“KEEP YOUR KIDNEYS WELL, AND
THEY WILL KEEP YOU WELL.”
There is not a drop of pure blood in the body until
it has passed through the kidneys.
For the kidneys are the blood-filters. From birth
to death the blood is constantly flowing through them
to be filtered. The heart pumps the blood, but the
KIDNEYS purify it. They strain out the uric acid
poison and waste water, and dispose of it through the
urinary system.
Every twenty-four hours the kidneys remove about
500 grains of urinous poison and three pints of
water from the blood. But when they are weakened
by a cold, overwork, or excess of any kind, they begin
to lose their power of separating the urine from the
blood.
Then the poisonous waste they leave in the blood
begins to make you feel heavy, dull and drowsy: you
suffer from, headache, sick headache, dizziness, or
rheumatic twinges. The water left in the system
collects under the skin and causes the swellings of
dropsy.. There are urinary disorders and gravel.
Doan's Backache Kidney Pills have a quick, sooth-
ing, tonic, and strengthening effect upon weak
kidneys, gently leading them back to health and
activity.

(Immense confusion, during which the

Presently the Mayor raised from the

to propose the pale secretary’s health. At

had been introduced, and the tone of the

shout occasionally.

There was great applause, in the midst of
which Mr. Jones was seen to flourish his
medal and heard to ask in anguished tones
where HE came in. He demanded to be
heard. The Mayor’s whisky, acting on a
disposition at once noisy and embittered,
caused him to yell in the Mayor’s ear until
he was called upon to reply.
“Yous blokes,” he began, “think yer
pretty blanky smart. I know what yer got.
Yer got 25 bob. An’ yer push this —this —
this —thing off on me. What’s it FOR? I
can’t wear it, I can’t sell it —blimey, I can’t
even POP it! Whatayer done with me
money, y’ cows. I ’eard yer, Teddy Brown,
sayin’ you might ’ave a decent band when
I’m gorn”

BRONSON ELLIOTT.
Our Institutions, Etc.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Interesting and important speeches were delivered
at the luncheon of the Millions Club in Sydney by
members of the British Parliamentary party....ln
a few minutes the train had pulled up at the siding
of the company’s factory. Here the party were
entertained hospitably by the management. ... the
party then proceeded to the Masonic Hall, where a
civic banquet was tendered to the visitors.... Ihe
party was entertained at a luncheon provided to-
night by the directors of the company . ...
The visitors were accorded a civic reception by
the Lord Mayor, and were entertained at dinner at
Parliament House in the evening....
The visitors were subsequently entertained at a
garden party by the Mayor and Mayoress, and in
the evening were the guests of the Governor.
-—News items.
I'M HEY were Parliamentary people, on
their travels round the States,
And they started hale and hearty,
Did that most distinguished Party—
Thank the Fates!
They had come (they said) desiring —
With minds open and inquiring—
To learn all about the Country—its resources,
methods, freights
Features, institutions, aspects, taxes, all that
indicates
What the Herald designates
As the future that awaits
This, the MA of all the States—
Which some quaint folk gauge its “pro-
gress” by the smallness of the rates.
They were welcomed with a luncheon and a
banquet and a ball,
And with speeches dull and witty,
In the Most Congested City
Of them all!
And they felt (they said) the pleasure
In unmitigated measure
Of the courtesies extended to them, here in
our Town Ilall!
They were more than glad to find their lines
in pleasant places fall;
Though their trip was somewhat tall,
They had heard the Empire call,
And they meant to stick to duty just like
Jonathan to Saul!
In the morning, bright and early, they were
brought big cups of tea.
And they swallowed them with gusto;
They were glad to lay the dust-oh,
Just as we!
For they liked (they said) the notion —-
An improvement on the potion,
Such as salts, or seidlitz-powder, they were
• used to, oversea !. . . .
So they dressed and went to Breakfast with
considerable glee—
Every baron and M.P.,
Had an appetite, you see,
And the Breakfast was most noble, as a
Breakfast ought to be !
Ere they started their inspections, per the
usual motor-car,
On behalf of this great Nation,
Came a cordial invitation
To the Bar !
And they guessed (they said) a snifter,
As a bracer and uplifter,
Was the sort of thing that went with a
matutinal cigar!—
And their cicerones told them they were
going rather far;
So, of course —Well, there you are!
Some took fizz and some Three
’Tis surprising how a morning drink w HI 1
often make or mar! J
On arriving at the soap-works—somewhat osA
the beaten track — /
Which had been the first place listed,
The proprietors insisted
On a snack!
“Just a bite” (they said) “to stay you
For the long and trying day you
Have to face, with all its speeches and its
toasts, ere you get back!”
Here a baron looked disheartened as he heard
the bottles crack;
Courtesy he did not lack,
But, as he surveyed the stack
Of refreshments and good liquors, he
seemed taken right aback !
The proceedings at the soap-works occupied
an hour or so ;
Then their guides began to worry,
Saying, “Really, there’s no hurry,
But we’ll go!
Otherwise” (they said) “that function
We arranged at Bondi Junction
Will be upset altogether—and this clock is
rather slow :
You will miss the mayoral luncheon, and
that wouldn’t do, you know!’.’
Seven motors in a row
Made a most imposing show
As they whirled that well-fed party out to
where the sea-winds blow!
Where they stowed the mayoral luncheon is a
secret to this day! ■. ;
Sure it is that after luncheon
Each M.P. looked like a puncheon,
So to say !.. . .
Though it often changed its venue,
It was much the same old menu —
Food and drink, cigars and speeches, rose like
lions in the way—
Afternoon tea made a slight change when
they got to Watson’s Bay! —
But they didn’t care to stay
At a garden-party gay,
With champagne and chicken-sandwich and
wine-trifle in full play !
Then the ultimate ordeal that awaited them
in town —
Dinner with the high Gaud Mayor,
A retired chicken-slayer—•
Got them dowm!
There were twenty-seven courses,
And they buckled-to like horses
For the honor of the Empire and their
Country’s just renown,
Loyalty to Throne and Person, and devotion
to the Crown!
Till an alderman named Brown,
In a necklace and a gowm.
Said — “Youse blokes don't seem much
'ungry! Wot you're drinlcin' wouldn't
drown
A muskeeter —look at Me now!" They
were beaten by a clown.
And Our Greatest Institutions in our Most
Congested Town !
